Blue Chips Rise On Optimism For Earnings --- As Stocks Climb, Treasury Bonds Slide on Concerns Over Fed Rates, Higher Forecast for Oil...
Monday's Markets
Blue Chips Rise On Optimism For Earnings --- As Stocks Climb, Treasury Bonds Slide on Concerns
Over Fed Rates, Higher Forecast for Oil Demand
By E.S. Browning
575 words
13 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS PUSHED ahead on the arrival of earnings season and an absence of significant terrorist activity
over Easter weekend.
But rising oil prices and bearish comments about interest rates from a Federal Reserve official helped knock
Treasury bonds down.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 73.53 points, or 0.7%, to 10515.56, pulling the blue chips out of the
red for the year and leaving them up 0.6%. But volume was light as European markets were closed for Easter
Monday.
With about 750 companies scheduled to report earnings this week, including about 70 members of the
Standard &Poor's 500-stock index, analysts were forecasting a strong 17.4% first-quarter earnings gain for
companies in the S&P 500, according to Thomson First Call, which tracks analyst estimates.
"People are expecting earnings to be better than that, and they are buying ahead of the earnings," said Todd
Leone, head of listed trading at New York brokerage firm SG Cowen. Among the major companies reporting
this week are Intel, Johnson &Johnson, Merrill Lynch and International Business Machines.
Relatively few companies have warned of disappointing earnings to come. That has raised investor
expectations for earnings and stimulated hopes that companies will issue upbeat comments about the outlook
for the rest of the year.
In addition, with markets closed Friday, some investors had sold off some of their holdings last week ahead of
the long weekend, in case of a terrorist act. Yesterday, some of those people bought back in.
Oil news, however, was negative. The International Energy Agency raised its forecast for international oil
demand for a sixth consecutive month.
That helped oil companies, but near-term gasoline futures hit a record $1.1803 a gallon on the New York
Mercantile Exchange, the highest level since the contract started trading in 1984. Crude oil rose to $37.84 a
barrel, nearing the 13 1/2-year high of $38.18 hit last month.
Treasury bonds sagged on comments by Robert Parry, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of San
Francisco, about the Fed's target rate for overnight interbank lending, which affects rates throughout the
market. Mr. Parry suggested in an interview with the San Francisco Chronicle that the Fed target rate, now
1%, would eventually rise.
Gold rose and the dollar was mixed.
The S&P 500 rose 0.52%, or 5.88 points, to 1145.20, now up 3% this year. The Nasdaq Composite Index,
whose many tech stocks have been rebounding ahead of earnings season, advanced 0.61%, or 12.60 points,
to 2065.48, now up 3.1% this year.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.40%, or 0.62 point, to 155.41.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks advanced. On the NYSE, where 1.10 billion shares traded, 1,653 stocks rose and 1,620 fell.

--- Page 8 ---
Page 8 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 10/32, or $3.125 per $1,000 invested, pushing up the yield to
4.232%. The 30-year bond fell 18/32 to yield 5.063%.
The dollar was mixed. It fell to 105.35 yen, down from 106.44; the euro fell to $1.2071 from $1.2096.

THE OUTLOOK: Latin America Grows Poorer Despite Boom
THE OUTLOOK:
The Economy
THE OUTLOOK: Latin America Grows Poorer Despite Boom
By Joel Millman
929 words
12 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Lima, Peru -- ON PAPER, Latin America is enjoying boom times. Surging commodities prices, for everything
from copper and iron ore to soybeans and fish meal, have powered growth in gross domestic product, almost
entirely owing to swelling demand from China. Political violence almost has disappeared. Currencies are
stable and low U.S. interest rates have made borrowing inexpensive. GDP has grown an average of 4%
across the region for the last three years, nearly triple the average in the 1990s.
Yet a gloomy undertone permeated the annual meeting of the board of governors of the Inter-American
Development Bank last month. The reason: Even under such promising conditions, Latin America is getting
poorer.
"It may surprise you to learn that during the last decade Latin American and Caribbean governments
increased social expenditure, in real terms, by about 58% per capita," Enrique V. Iglesias, the president of the
Inter-American Development Bank, told delegates in his welcoming speech. Yet the results were dismal: After
a decade of higher social spending, he said, there are many more poor people now than in 1990, with at least
20 million new poor slipping below the poverty line since 1997.
Meanwhile, Latin America's debt problem is worsening. Despite billions of dollars raised through the
privatization of state assets such as telephone companies, iron mines and railroads, Latin America's overall
debt-to-GDP ratio has risen, to 51% of GDP in 2002 from 37% in 1997. Foreign investment has plunged by
20% in that same period. Unemployment was up to 15% in 2002 from 10% in 1997.
INDEED, DEBT AND unemployment now are linked in a troubling spiral: The more Latin America borrows,
the poorer it gets, the more risk lenders perceive, and the more costly their capital. That results in less
investment and fewer jobs, which leads to soaring emigration.
If all this is occurring in good times, many at the meeting wondered, what can Latin America expect when
borrowing rates begin to rise again, as they must as soon as U.S. rates go up? Historically, every rise of two
percentage points on U.S. rates has translated into upticks of five percentage points or more in Latin
America, Mr. Iglesias warned, meaning that debt is going to get more costly.
Peru's most renowned economist, Hernando de Soto, didn't attend the IADB meeting. As he cheerfully
admits, IADB types tend to regard his argument on poverty -- that freeing the poor from the bounds that keep
them out of the economic mainstream quickly can raise incomes and liberate Latin America from its vicious
debt cycle -- is naive at best.
The Peruvian economist's influential 1986 book, "The Other Path" (the title was a rebuke to Peru's Maoist
Shining Path movement) called on Latin governments to give land titles to squatters. That, he said, would
allow peasants who build homes to collateralize wealth and leverage it into growing businesses. In turn,
millions of informal, off-the-books merchants would be encouraged to expand their horizons, prodding them
into the formal, taxable sector, where their wealth would be channeled into savings accounts, business loans
and employee payrolls. Says Mr. De Soto: "The poor are not the problem, they're the solution."
THE NEW MegaPlaza shopping center in Lima's Cono Norte district is one example of what Mr. De Soto
means. MegaPlaza is a media sensation here since opening last year in a teeming slum. According to its
owners, the sprawling complex, with its stores, food court and the largest Gold's Gym in Latin America, drew
seven million shoppers during the Christmas season -- only a million short of Lima's total population of eight
million -- and has generated more than $30 million in sales and payroll taxes since construction began.
Mr. De Soto explains that MegaPlaza is the result of a government land-titling program, begun with the urging
of his think tank in 1993, during a burst of sweeping revisions under then-President Alberto Fujimori. By the

--- Page 12 ---
Page 12 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
end of the decade, Peru had titled over 350,000 homes in Cono Norte, and that helped some 150,000 local
underground entrepreneurs to become tax-paying "formals." Sadly, he says, land titling slowed in Peru, and
elsewhere, one reason the IADB continues to report such disappointing results.
"In Peru all the land we have titled is worth $9.4 billion," Mr. De Soto says, more than the $8.3 billion Peru
owes to the Paris Club of lenders. Mexico, he calculates, has more than $315 billion in untitled land, more
value than all the petroleum still under Mexican soil.
"We do need to find a way to increase savings," agrees Cesar Gaviria, the former president of Colombia, a
De Soto fan who heads the Organization of American States. "If we don't, almost all the investment we do is
with debt. And we are going to repeat this volatility-of-capital crisis, every three or four years."
The informal sector does create savings, Mr. De Soto argues, although he adds that racial and class
prejudice, and onerous regulations that restrict informal entrepreneurs to their poor barrios, deprive Latin
American economies of those savings' benefits. Mr. De Soto says that the IADB reported that Latin migrants
sent home an estimated $40 billion in 2004, surpassing direct foreign investment in the region. That proves,
he says, that the poor generate wealth even when they leave home. The poor are not the last hope, he says,
"they're our only hope."

Forex View: Dollar's Strength on Jobs News Now Depends on Week's Data
Currency Trading
Forex View: Dollar's Strength on Jobs News Now Depends on Week's Data
By Tom Barkley Dow Jones Newswires
558 words
12 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- The dollar should continue to draw support from investors' recent enthusiasm about U.S. jobs
growth, but how much further it can appreciate this week will depend on a heavy calendar of data releases
and events in Iraq.
The sentiment of investors has generally turned in favor of the dollar since the beginning of the month, when
an unexpectedly robust employment report showed 308,000 jobs were added in March. Yet since climbing to
its strongest level in four months against the euro at around $1.1984 early last week, the dollar has struggled
to establish a new, higher range.
While the payroll numbers spurred optimism about jobs creation and an eventual rise in U.S. interest rates,
investors think Federal Reserve policy makers will need to see that the labor-market growth is sustainable
before preparing the market for a rate increase.
"For the last six weeks, we haven't been able to get into a trend, and the dollar's been fighting to break higher
versus the European currencies especially," says Tim Mazanec, senior currency strategist at Investors Bank
&Trust in Boston. He isn't convinced that the dollar can hold below $1.21 against the euro. "The market
doesn't seem to have appetite" to buy dollars "with Iraq as a backdrop," he says.
The dollar has borne the brunt of investors' fears about both Iraq and terrorist attacks elsewhere -- with
traditional haven assets such as the Swiss franc and gold the main beneficiaries. However, the yen has also
come under pressure since three Japanese civilians were taken hostage late last week by an Islamic
extremist group demanding the withdrawal of Japanese troops from the country.
In holiday-thinned late trading Friday in New York, the dollar was at 106.44 yen, up from 106.28 yen
Thursday. The euro was trading at $1.2096, up modestly from $1.2080. The dollar fell to 1.2817 Swiss francs
from 1.2832 francs, while sterling was little changed at $1.8338 vs. $1.8340.
U.S. bond and stock markets were closed in observance of Good Friday, while major markets in Europe will
remain closed today for the Easter holiday. That means trading will probably get off to a slow start this week.
Anne Mills, currency analyst at Brown Brothers Harriman &Co. in New York, says that while the euro is
poised for further weakness, the situation in Iraq may keep that from happening. "The weight is on the bottom
of the range for the euro, depending on what happens in Iraq," she says. In addition, investors may want to
hold out for another U.S. employment report before buying more dollars, she says.
"I think pretty soon we'll settle down into a situation where investors want to know what happens with the next
jobs number," she says. "I get the feeling that people don't want to change their view on the U.S. and interest
rates at this point."
Data releases in the U.S. are expected to further fuel optimism about the economic recovery, starting with
March retail sales tomorrow. "Retail sales should get the dollar off to a good start" this week, says Investors
Bank &Trust's Mr. Mazanec.

Dollar Weakens Against Euro, As ECB Keeps Rates Unchanged
Currency Trading
Dollar Weakens Against Euro, As ECB Keeps Rates Unchanged
By Jamie McGeever Dow Jones Newswires
744 words
2 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- The dollar weakened across the board after the European Central Bank left interest rates on
hold and jolted the market by giving little indication it intends to lower rates soon.
Expectations of an imminent cut had weakened the euro to a four-month low against the dollar Monday. Its
gradual recovery since then accelerated yesterday after ECB President Jean-Claude Trichet gave an upbeat
assessment of the euro zone's economic outlook and said monetary policy is "in line" with price stability.
The dollar's weak performance against the euro dragged it lower against other currencies as well, although
strong U.S. manufacturing data helped the greenback pare its losses as dealers scaled back positions ahead
of the U.S. jobs report today.
Meanwhile, the dollar was under pressure against the yen for the entire global session yesterday after the
release in Tokyo of data showing improvements in Japanese business sentiment.
The Bank of Japan's closely watched tankan survey, which showed companies in all sectors were more
optimistic about business conditions in the three months ended in March, boosted the yen against most
currencies. The large manufacturers' sentiment index improved to 12, its highest level since June 1997. The
figure was better than the average forecast of 10 of economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires.
The dollar's overall softness, however, coincided with another speculative-driven surge in precious metals,
with silver and gold prices in the spot market spiking to 17-year and 16-year peaks, respectively. Oil and
other commodity prices also remained high.
In New York trading yesterday the euro was at $1.2353, up from $1.2303 Wednesday in New York, but it was
slightly weaker against the yen at 128.25 yen, down from 128.46 yen.
The dollar was even weaker against the yen, falling to 103.76 yen from 104.25 yen Wednesday in New York
and falling to 1.2636 Swiss francs from 1.2641 Swiss francs. Sterling stood at $1.8551, compared with
$1.8437 Wednesday.
The main event for currencies was the ECB meeting. The decision to leave benchmark interest rates
unchanged at 2% wasn't unexpected; however, the tone of Mr. Trichet's subsequent remarks, in which he
appeared to downplay concerns about consumer spending and generally struck a more hawkish tone than
many anticipated, came as a surprise.
"The market sharply reduced the probability of an easing any time soon, so in that context it was a pretty
significant day," said Robert Sinche, chief global currency strategist at Citibank in New York.
Fixed-income analysts at BNP Paribas expect upcoming euro zone economic data to disappoint and the
ECB's inflation and growth forecasts to be revised down in June "This will be the prompt for a rate cut -- in
June," they wrote in a research note yesterday.
Mr. Trichet gave little indication of that happening. "We are not modifying our assumption of a gradual
recovery," he said yesterday, adding that the ECB has "no particular bias" on the future direction of interest
rates.
That the dollar recovered from its lows was mainly thanks to Institute for Supply Management data, which
showed another increase in U.S. manufacturing activity. Employment conditions and the prices-paid
component also rose.
The institute's headline index rose for a 10th-straight month, this time by 1.1 points to 62.5. Economists
expected a decline to 59.5. Readings above 50 suggest expansion in activity. The prices component of the

--- Page 19 ---
Page 19 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
report rose to 86, the highest level since January 1995, while the employment index climbed to 57, the fifth
consecutive month of gains.
Also yesterday, the government reported slight improvement in labor-market conditions. Jobless claims fell by
3,000 to 342,000 in the latest week, broadly in line with analyst expectations.
The producer-price index for February rose 0. 1%, slowing from a 0. 6% rise of January and much lower than
economists' forecasts of a 0. 4% rise. The core rate excluding volatile food and energy prices edged up 0.
1%, in line with expectations.
Taken together, the data aren't enough to alter the view that the Federal Reserve can keep interest rates low
until it sees a clear recovery in the job market.
That is why the nonfarm payrolls report to be released today could have a major impact on the dollar.

Dow Jones Shakes Up Index --- AIG, Pfizer, Verizon Supplant AT&T, Kodak, International Paper; Blue Chips Rise and Bonds Fall
Thursday's Markets
Dow Jones Shakes Up Index --- AIG, Pfizer, Verizon Supplant AT&T, Kodak, International Paper; Blue
Chips Rise and Bonds Fall
By E.S. Browning
1,627 words
2 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE DOW JONES Industrial Average will change its makeup for the first time in more than four years, by
deleting three stocks that were members when Eisenhower was president.
The 30-stock average is adding insurance company American International Group Inc., drug maker Pfizer Inc.
and telephone-service provider Verizon Communications Inc. Those stocks replace AT&T Corp., Eastman
Kodak Co. and International Paper Co.
The three departing stocks had been Dow stalwarts for decades. AT&T was in the industrial average from
Oct. 4, 1916, to Oct. 1, 1928; it re-entered on March 14, 1939. Kodak has been a component of the Dow
since July 18, 1930, and International Paper since July 3, 1956.
The changes to the average, which tracks the prices of blue-chip stocks, take effect when trading begins on
April 8.
The changes won't directly affect the value of the Dow, as the math used to calculate it will be adjusted to
reflect the prices of the stocks concerned. However, the stock market yesterday began the new quarter with a
small gain, partly as a result of a much stronger-than-expected report on March manufacturing activity, and
partly in response to the news of the Dow's new look.
Despite declines by the three departing stocks, the industrial average rose 15.63 points, or 0.15%, to
10373.33, leaving it still down 0.77% for the year so far. The Nasdaq Composite Index, whose many
technology stocks would be among the beneficiaries of a stronger economy, jumped 1.04%, or 20.79 points,
to 2015.01. It once again is in positive territory for the year so far, up 0.6%.
Gold continued its recent advance, rising 50 cents to another 15-year high of $427.80, while the dollar
continued to sag against the yen, hitting a four-year low of 103.76 yen to the dollar.
The revisions to the Dow industrials weren't provoked by any specific event, said Paul E. Steiger, managing
editor of The Wall Street Journal, who oversees the makeup of the Dow Jones Industrial Average.
"None of these changes was triggered by an event such as an imminent merger, which was the case in the
past three instances of changes dating back to the early 1990s," Mr. Steiger said. "Rather, they recognize
trends within the U.S. stock market, including the continued growth of the financial and health-care sectors
and the diminishing relative weight of basic-materials stocks."
The 107-year-old Dow last changed composition on Nov. 1, 1999, when Home Depot Inc., Intel Corp.,
Microsoft Corp. and SBC Communications Inc. replaced Chevron Corp., Goodyear Tire &Rubber Co., Sears,
Roebuck &Co. and Union Carbide Corp.
The performance of the four new component stocks since then suggests that being added to the widely
followed barometer doesn't necessarily herald a lasting price increase for the stocks concerned.
Since the 1999 adjustment, the Dow industrials overall have fallen only about 3% in value, but all four of the
new stocks have fallen harder.
As of Wednesday's close, Home Depot had declined 26% since its addition to the Dow industrials, according
to Baseline. Intel was down 30%, Microsoft was down 46% and SBC Communications had fallen 52%.
Sears, in contrast, was up 52%, but Goodyear was down 79%. Chevron merged with Texaco Inc. in 1999,
while Union Carbide was acquired by Dow Chemical Co. in 2001, so neither of them trades now as a
separate stock.

--- Page 21 ---
Page 21 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Yesterday, following news of the planned changes to the Dow, AIG, Pfizer and Verizon all advanced, while
AT&T, Kodak and International Paper declined. Money managers who specialize in stocks that are included
in the Dow industrials will be likely to buy the first three and sell the last three, which helped explain their
moves.
The changes were announced by Dow Jones &Co., which generates the Dow industrials, as well as a variety
of other averages and indexes. Dow Jones is the parent of the Journal.
Dow Jones noted in a statement that "company additions to and deletions from the Dow Jones Industrial
Average do not in any way reflect an opinion on the investment merits of the company."
The Dow industrial average was created by Dow Jones co-founder Charles H. Dow in May 1896 and has
become the world's best-known stock-market measure. Originally made up of 12 industrial companies, it has
for some years been branching out, despite its name, to include more service and financial concerns.
John Prestbo, editor of Dow Jones Indexes, said that all 30 stocks were reviewed in the course of arriving at
the decision to replace three. "There are no predetermined criteria for a stock to be added or deleted, though
we intend that all components be established U.S. companies that are leaders in their industries," he said.
For the sake of continuity, composition changes are kept rare, Mr. Prestbo said.
The addition of Verizon in place of AT&T means that two children of the phone giant's breakup are now part
of the Dow industrials, and the onetime parent company is out. SBC and Verizon both are "Baby Bells,"
created to be local phone-service providers when AT&T was broken up in 1984 following an antitrust ruling.
Mr. Prestbo said this is the third instance in which descendants have taken the place of broken-apart parent
companies to represent their respective industries in the average. ExxonMobil Corp. is a combination of two
descendants of Standard Oil Co. (New Jersey), broken up in 1911. Boeing Co. and United Technologies Inc.
trace their lineage to United Aircraft Corp., which was split in 1934.
All three companies added to the Dow industrials -- Verizon, Pfizer and AIG -- trade on the New York Stock
Exchange. (William C. Steere Jr., the former chief executive of Pfizer, is a director at Dow Jones.) In 1999,
two of the companies that were added, Intel and Microsoft, were the first two Nasdaq Stock Market
companies added to the Dow, but no Nasdaq company was added this time.
In response to its removal from the Dow, AT&T issued this statement: "While AT&T and the entire
telecommunications industry have reflected the effects of soft demand and overcapacity since early in 2000,
AT&T is a Fortune 40 company with $34.5 billion in revenue last year. The fact remains that AT&T is one of
the world's leading communications companies, and our unrivaled base of enterprise customers to whom we
provide sophisticated networking services -- both domestically and globally -- make AT&T a bellwether of the
U.S. economy. Despite ongoing regulatory uncertainty, AT&T has continued to invest in technology
innovation and advanced networking, while improving our processes and the experience our customers
expect from AT&T."
International Paper, in a statement, said: "The composition of the Dow Jones Industrial Average has no
bearing on the company's continuing focus on adding value for our customers to help them succeed in their
businesses and providing solid returns to shareholders."
Eastman Kodak spokesman Gerard Meuchner said, "This action in no way changes our strategy or our belief
that we will be successful in implementing our strategy. Membership in any stock index has no bearing on our
ability to manage the company for profitable growth."
Most stock indexes posted gains on the first day of the quarter, with the Russell 2000 small-stock index rising
5.01 points, or 0.85%, to 595.32. That marked its sixth consecutive advance, leaving it up almost 7% on the
year and only 1.8% below the record high of 606.12 hit four years ago.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 5.96 points, or 0.53%, to 1132.17, and is now up 1.8% this
year.
Investors continued to bite their nails over the number of new jobs created in March, a report that is due out
today and that could have a sharp effect on stocks.
Yesterday's strong manufacturing news, which raised fresh worries that interest rates will have to rise, sent
the 10-year Treasury note down 9/32, or $2.8125 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which moves inversely
to price, rose to 3.879%.
Crude-oil prices fell 4.2%, the sharpest decline since November, on reports that U.S. inventories are larger
than thought and that the federal government may ease pollution rules, which would help push down gasoline
prices.

'Hey Charlie, Buy Me Some American Cotton Oil!'
At the beginning, the Dow Jones Industrial Average included such stocks
as American Cotton Oil and Tennessee Coal. In fact, there were only 12
original Dow stocks when the average of industrial shares was started in
1896. The one surviving original stock is General Electric Co. (though it
dropped out of the average twice, in the years after the average's launch).
The index's inventor was Charles Dow, who had started Dow Jones &Co.,
publisher of The Wall Street Journal, along with Edward Jones and Charles
Bergstresser in 1882.

In the Fray: If the Strings Have Their Way, Who Will Play the Triangle?
In the Fray:
LEISURE &ARTS
In the Fray: If the Strings Have Their Way, Who Will Play the Triangle?
By Stephen Pollard
875 words
1 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
D8
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A little while ago, I achieved a lifetime ambition. Not only did I conduct a full-size symphony orchestra; we
performed Beethoven's Eroica Symphony, in my view the greatest symphony ever written. And not only was
there an audience; it even clapped.
I say "we," because I considered that we were, for the piece's 50 minutes, a collective entity. The sum of our
individual parts was far, far greater than anything we could individually have achieved. And equally, if any of
us had made a mistake, it would have affected the others, too. I may only have been waving my arms around,
but if I'd waved them at the wrong time or in the wrong place then the result would have been chaos.
What never crossed my mind, however, was that because I was the only one of us who was busy throughout
the piece, I should have been paid more than the horn players, who were only needed sporadically, or even
the strings, which had moments when they were silent. I am not, after all, a "name" conductor. No one has
ever paid money to hear me conduct: They come for the piece. The same goes for orchestra: No one has
ever paid to hear a specific member of an orchestra play. They come to hear the orchestra as a whole.
In the view of 16 violinists in the Bonn-based Beethovenorchester, however, I should indeed have taken the
biggest share of the money. They have decided to sue their management for a pay increase on the grounds
that they play more music than the brass, the woodwinds and the percussion. The violinists argue -- the case
goes to court next month -- that they should be paid an extra 100 euros, or about $121, for each rehearsal or
performance.
The orchestra's deputy director, Michael Horn, told Agence France-Presse that the violinists "feel
disadvantaged because, depending on the compositions being performed, they are required to play more
than wind players." And they are, they say, being generous in their demand. As reported on a BBC Web site,
one violinist said: "We could have calculated the surcharge per semi-quaver, but we chose to take an easier
course." Sometimes, in other words, the brass get a movement or two off -- so the strings should be paid
more.
There's only one word for the idea: ridiculous. Last week I heard the Amsterdam Concertgebouw perform
Bruckner's Ninth Symphony. I haven't added up the number of notes played by each section of the orchestra,
but it's safe to assume that the violins had, as they almost always do, the most to play. But the measure of a
great performance of Bruckner's Ninth -- the same goes for all of the Austrian composer's symphonies -- is
the brass. The strings provide a foundation over which the brass soar. If one of the violinists makes a
mistake, it barely matters: It will be hidden by sound coming from the rest of the strings. But if a brass player
makes just one, tiny mistake, it will ruin an entire passage. The word musicians use is "exposed." They all
were, however, perfect. When the conductor, Bernard Haitink, beckoned them to take a bow after the
performance, the audience rose as one to acclaim them.
If the Beethovenorchester's violinists had their way, the brass players would have been given a sandwich and
a cup of coffee as a thank you and been grateful for it. The violinists would have scooped up their payment in
gold bullion and returned to their mansions to be waited on hand and foot and fed peeled grapes. They had,
after all, played the most notes.
I heard the downside of what it means to be exposed a few days ago when the English National Opera
performed "The Rheingold," the first part of Wagner's Ring Cycle. The introduction is -- should be -- one of
the most delicate, haunting, evocative passages in all music, depicting the flow of the Rhine itself. Listening,
one should be transported out of the opera house and into a different world. Unfortunately, however, the
ENO's brass is far from top of the range, and the first few bars were marred by a series of split notes. The
Rhine it was not. Working on the logic of the violinists from Bonn, perhaps the brass players responsible
should have their pay docked.

--- Page 24 ---
Page 24 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
I have to confess never to having heard the Beethovenorchester perform, but I do have one observation that
the dissident fiddlers might care to ponder. Their orchestra is not -- how to put this politely -- one of the
world's leading bands. It's not even one of Germany's. Indeed, there are some who would argue it's not
Bonn's. If I were a violinist in the orchestra, I'd think long and hard before demanding that my performance be
measured before I am paid. And I might find the orchestra manager asking me, "If you are so great, why
aren't you playing in the Berliner Philharmoniker?"
---
Mr. Pollard last wrote on soprano Deborah Voigt.

Stock Market Quarterly Review -- Wall Street Underwriting: Stock Underwriting Kicks Into Higher Gear --- Proceeds From Equity Issues Triple...
Stock Market Quarterly Review -- Wall Street Underwriting: Stock Underwriting Kicks Into Higher Gear
--- Proceeds From Equity Issues Triple From Weak Level, And Disclosed Fees Surge
By Susanne Craig and Randall Smith
2,667 words
1 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A YEAR'S WORTH of stock-market gains finally kicked one of Wall Street's most lucrative businesses, stock
underwriting, into higher gear in the first quarter.
Even though the stock market sputtered late in the first quarter after a terrorist attack in Spain, proceeds from
U.S. issuance of stock tripled from a very weak level in the first quarter of 2003, when stock markets around
the world bottomed out just before the U.S.-led coalition invaded Iraq.
Though first-quarter global equity and equity-related issuance increased only 4.3% from the fourth quarter, to
$141 billion, the higher volume level makes it clear that "investor sentiment has changed towards equities,"
says Jon Sandelman, president of the securities unit of Bank of America Corp.
It has been a rough few years for Wall Street, which has seen underwriting fees dry up as stock markets
tumbled. Despite the sideways movement in the recent quarter, the overall outlook for stocks has improved,
lifting the mood for the big firms. Not only are major stock-market indexes more than 25% above year-earlier
levels, but investors also seem to have more confidence in the economy and U.S. companies, Mr.
Sandelman says, despite the recent wave of corporate-governance scandals.
The increase in stock issuance -- which provides higher fee income than does the bond side -- was enough to
fuel a 50% pickup in global disclosed underwriting fees. The total hit $4.86 billion in the first quarter, the
highest level in two-and-a-half years, according to Thomson Financial, which tracks underwriting activity.
A widely followed barometer of Wall Street's bread-and-butter business, global disclosed fees peaked at $7.9
billion in the first quarter of 2000, at the height of the stock-market bubble, and tumbled to a postbubble low of
$2.6 billion in the third quarter of 2002.
Richard Peterson, chief market strategist at Thomson Financial, called the first-quarter activity level "robust."
He added, "Considering where we were at this time last year, sitting there waiting for calamity -- and now
we're seeing prosperity." Gains in the overall volume of stocks and bonds sold globally were somewhat less
dramatic than fees, rising 14% to $1.6 trillion in the first quarter, from the first quarter of 2003, Thomson said.
That is because even though U.S. interest rates remained surprisingly low in the first quarter, so many
corporations and individuals already had refinanced their debt or home mortgages after rates plummeted in
2002 and 2003, that there wasn't as much need to do the same thing this year.
The volume of U.S. mortgage-backed securities issuance, which reflects home-buying and refinancing
activity, plunged 53% in the first quarter to $139.3 billion from the year-earlier quarter, according to Thomson.
The busiest underwriters for the first quarter were Citigroup Inc., with $162.7 billion of stocks and bonds,
followed by Merrill Lynch &Co., Morgan Stanley, J.P. Morgan Chase &Co. and Lehman Brothers Holdings
Inc. Reflecting the pickup in non-U.S. activity, Deutsche Bank AG surged to No. 6 from No. 9 in the first
quarter of 2003.
Issues of stocks and bonds in Deutsche Bank's home turf of Europe rose to 43% of the global total in the first
quarter of 2004 from just 33% of the total in the first quarter of 2003, Thomson said.
The leaders in fees, reflecting their higher profile in more lucrative stock or junk-bond issues, were Morgan
Stanley at $511.4 million, followed by Citigroup, the Credit Suisse First Boston unit of Credit Suisse Group,
Merrill Lynch and Goldman Sachs Group Inc. Morgan Stanley's rank at the top of the fee tables was driven by
its No. 1 ranking in leading initial public offerings and other stock issues.
"The recent period was marked by intense demand for capital by world-wide corporations and agencies and a
powerful performance by Wall Street in assembling, marketing and completing such financings," Thomson's

--- Page 26 ---
Page 26 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Peterson said. "The results were even more impressive given the fact that political and economic issues
dominated the financial news of early 2004."
Still, the terrorist bombings in Madrid had a damping effect on stock markets, which may yet spill over into
underwriting. "I think the market initially underreacted to that. We have seen a lagging effect that has
tempered investor sentiment over the past few weeks," says Goldman Sachs's David Solomon, co-head of
global equity capital markets at the New York investment bank. "This will take some time to play out."
The most important factor driving the big gains in underwriting fees and stock issues was the re-emergence
of initial public offerings of stock. IPOs carry the richest fees, as much as 7% of proceeds, and offerings by
U.S. companies issuing stock globally rocketed up to $6.8 billion from less than $1 billion a year earlier.
Jay Chandler, head of equity syndicate, Americas, at Merrill Lynch, noted that IPO activity accelerated from
last year's first quarter even though the number of deals fell from the fourth quarter. "Investors have shown
strong interest in IPOs during the quarter, although recent market weakness has affected the trading
performance of some deals," he said.
IPOs spanned the globe in origin, with bellwether deals in both Europe and Asia and the three largest coming
from Belgium, Japan and China. The largest U.S. offering of the quarter, insurer Assurant Inc.'s $1.76 billion
deal, was the largest U.S. IPO since Travelers Property Casualty Corp. went public in 2002.
"This quarter marked the return of the IPO calendar across both sectors and geographies," said Ted Pick,
Morgan Stanley's Global Head of Equity Syndicate. Dan Simkowitz, head of Morgan Stanley's
equity-products group, noted that Shinsei Bank was the first major Japanese financial institution to go public
in a decade. Despite the pickup in activity, there was no announcement yet on the much-anticipated IPO of
Google Inc., the Internet search service.
Many other global stock and bond issues were driven by corporate strategic decisions more than market
ebullience. For instance, Marathon Oil Corp. on March 25 sold $900 million in stock to help pay for Ashland
Inc.'s minority interest in Marathon Ashland Petroleum LLC.
The quarter's second-largest stock issue after the $4 billion IPO of Belgacom SA was a $3.8 billion sale by
General Electric Co. to fund its acquisition of certain entertainment assets of Vivendi Universal SA.
In convertible issues, Morgan Stanley's Mr. Simkowitz said companies' refinancing needs weren't as much of
a factor in the first quarter as they were in the past two years. "If companies needed to refinance near-term
debt, the bulk of them did it in 2002 and 2003," he said. As a result, he added, most convertible issues were
driven by specific events. Mining company Freeport-McMoRan Copper &Gold Inc., for instance, raised $1.1
billion in March via convertible preferred stock in order to buy back shares from a major stockholder.
In bonds, Jim Merli, global head of debt syndicate at Lehman Brothers, noted that low interest rates,
particularly in shorter-term securities, and a stable market provided "a very positive backdrop for fixed
income." The expectation that the Fed won't raise rates in the near term also helped the market.
The issuance of U.S. investment-grade debt rose moderately in the quarter. Goldman's Mr. Solomon said
most of the refinancing that corporations wanted to do has been done during the past few years, "which
should slow the flow down in the second or third quarter."
The market for U.S. corporate investment-grade debt was aided by a number of large offerings in the quarter,
notably a $6.7 billion financing by the Pacific Gas &Electric unit of PG&E Corp., which plans to exit
bankruptcy-law protection from creditors in April. The fact that Pacific Gas "can come out of bankruptcy,
secure investment-grade ratings and raise $6.7 billion" at such relatively low interest rates shows the health
of the bond market, says Raj Dhanda, co-head of Global Debt Syndicate at Morgan Stanley.
Generally low interest rates also spurred more high-yield, or junk-bond, issues by companies with
speculative credit ratings. "Available terms have never been more attractive," said Morgan Stanley's Mr.
Dhanda. As a result, high-yield issuance had one of its best quarters ever. Overall, according to Thomson,
global high-yield debt issuance climbed to more than $45 billion in the quarter, from $24.4 billion a year ago.
Heading into the second quarter, the biggest cloud on the horizon is the market weakness following the
Spanish terrorist attack and new questions about the strength of the U.S. economy. With the stock market off
its postbubble peak, Bank of America's Mr. Sandelman noted that the market has been more sideways to flat
recently. If that continues, he said, Wall Street's fee harvest may slow a bit amid the uncertainties.
Goldman's Mr. Solomon is a bit more optimistic. "Although the market is still clearly nervous about what
happened in Madrid, overall we have good expectations for equity issuance in the second quarter," he said.

Stock Market Quarterly Review -- Essay: Technology Changes the Dynamics of High Oil Prices --- Potential of Expensive Crude to Cripple the...
Stock Market Quarterly Review -- Essay: Technology Changes the Dynamics of High Oil Prices ---
Potential of Expensive Crude to Cripple the Economy Has Lessened Since the 1970s, but Risks Still
Remain
By Peter A. McKAy
1,356 words
1 April 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C12
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
TO UNDERSTAND the potential of high oil prices to wreak economic havoc -- and how that power is
changing -- think of oil as a creature in a Hollywood blockbuster.
During the energy shortages of the 1970s, expensive crude oil was like Godzilla, a monster creating
widespread inflation and stomping out economic growth. Now, it is more like a gremlin. It isn't exactly going
to eat Tokyo, but it can cause all sorts of mischief and generally gum up the works, even to the point of
causing serious damage.
There are many reasons for the switch, but all boil down to the increasing importance of technology and
services in the U.S. economy, which in turn reduces dependence on old-fashioned, oil-intensive industry.
That change, coupled with the sagging (until recently) foreign-exchange value of the U.S. dollar, helps explain
why the recent rally in oil prices to a 13-year high of about $38 a barrel hasn't sparked rampant fear of
1970s-style inflation.
Although prices have eased somewhat recently, to almost $36 a barrel on the New York Mercantile
Exchange, oil still is far from cheap by any historical standard. Nearby crude-oil futures contracts, the price of
which traders most actively track, have closed every day this year above $30 and did so on 162 Nymex
trading days last year -- a record for closes above $30 since the contracts began trading in 1983. At $36 a
barrel, prices still are more than 50% above their 10-year average and about 13% above the average since
the outbreak of war in Iraq last year.
Meanwhile, it seems as if stock-market investors have hardly noticed. Major indexes barely budged during
the first quarter, although there were some signs of jitters. The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 96.22
points, or 0.9%, to 10357.70, breaking a string of three consecutive quarterly gains. The Nasdaq Composite
Index fell 0.5%, or 9.15 points, to 1994.22, ending a 5-quarter winning streak. The Standard &Poor's
500-stock index inched up 1.3%, or 14.29 points, to 1126.21.
But just because oil's role is changing, that doesn't mean the risks traditionally associated with an energy
run-up have erased. Those dangers may manifest more slowly, or in less obvious ways than they did
previously, but they continue to be lurking monsters.
A sustained run of high oil prices still could create pitfalls for the economy and stock market. Expensive crude
could drain corporate earnings, nudge the Federal Reserve to raise interest rates quickly and siphon off
money from consumers' pockets that they would spend on other items.
Expensive oil "is more significant than most people give it credit for, because it's something that kills so
slowly," says energy economist Phil Verleger. "The effects are just beginning to work their way through the
system, really."
As a share of gross domestic product, or the value of all goods and services produced in a nation, energy
spending peaked in 1981, according to the U.S. Energy Information Administration, then declined more than
47% through 2000, driven by increased efficiency of many everyday devices, such as air conditioners,
heaters and refrigerators. As a result, many stock investors have been less prone to worry about ripple
effects from fluctuations in energy prices.
Still, some Wall Street pros are nervous again, albeit for different reasons than in the past.
Stuart A. Schweitzer, of J.P. Morgan Investment Management Inc., a unit of New York bank J.P. Morgan
Chase &Co., for instance, says he didn't worry much about $30-plus crude as long as it wasn't part of a
broad-based commodities rally that would signal a spike in inflation. In the past 12 months, however, the
Dow Jones-AIG index of 20 commodities is up more than 32%, and other broad indicators also have risen

--- Page 31 ---
Page 31 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
sharply. Some individual commodities are near historic highs. Copper is up 90% at $1.36 a pound. Silver is
up 78% at $7.945 a troy ounce. Soybeans are up 73% at $9.95 a bushel. Gold is up more than 27%, hitting a
15-year high yesterday of $428.30 a troy ounce on Nymex's Comex division.
In that context, Mr. Schweitzer says crude oil could become a trip wire that causes the Federal Reserve to
raise interest rates quickly in order to curb inflation. "People came into the year expecting them to raise
rates anyway," he says. "But what crude may do now is make them prone to raise sooner rather than later
because they don't want to let [oil prices] run too far, along with everything else."
Phil Flynn, an analyst with the Chicago commodity brokerage house Alaron Trading Corp., says he believes
oil is likely to hit $40 a barrel, and cut into consumer confidence. "We're already in a tight supply situation
world-wide," he says, noting that high prices have discouraged inventory building. "Demand hasn't been hurt
at these price levels, but it can have a psychological effect on the economy."
Already, consumers are seeing some tangible effects of the run-up in crude prices. The EIA this week
announced that the average nationwide price of regular unleaded gasoline set a new high of $1.758 a gallon,
and predicted prices will move even higher this spring.
Another issue is the increasing role of the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries, disconnecting
crude-oil prices somewhat from normal cycles in which high prices discourage demand, in turn pushing prices
lower.
Until the late 1990s, OPEC members often cheated on their own production-cutting quotas. But as commodity
prices sagged during the late stages of the dot-com boom, oil exporters began acting as a more cohesive
cartel. As long as they stay cohesive, they can cut production even in a high-price environment and still be
effective.
That may be the scenario the markets now face, as OPEC confirmed plans yesterday to cut its output by
about 4% beginning today. Energy-consuming countries have lobbied the cartel to hold off on the move and
stem the high prices, but it is unclear if OPEC will reverse course or adhere to the new quotas.
In the past, OPEC has been hesitant to nudge oil prices lower when there are few signs of inflation in
oil-consuming countries. That has been the case in the U.S. lately, because the spate of high prices has
come at a time when job growth is low and producers have little pricing power to pass along energy costs to
consumers.
That wasn't so during the energy shortages of the 1970s, which hit at a time of relatively high employment
and growth. "You had a much higher pass-through on all sorts of commodity prices to consumers back then,"
says Steve Strongin, director of commodity research at Goldman Sachs Group Inc. "Energy still has a lot of
pass-through, obviously, but it's a very different market."
If energy costs stay high this time around, many analysts believe consumers saddled with high energy bills
will spend less money on other items, leading to slower growth. Mr. Verleger says that may happen by the
fourth quarter of this year or the first quarter of next year, leading to a stock-market slump. "I just don't see
much likelihood of the economy getting a greater oil supply to keep that from happening," he says.
Economist James Glassman of J.P. Morgan Securities is a little more optimistic. He says prices will remain
high, driven by increased industrial demand around the globe.
The only problem would come, he says, if consumer demand stays weak for the finished goods companies
use the oil to make -- a scenario he believes won't play out. "You wonder whether the markets are just going
through a learning process right now," he says.
Document J000000020040401e0410002x

Pork Highway
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Pork Highway
654 words
30 March 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A18
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
It's amazing what a little White House backbone on spending will do. President Bush has been sending
signals that he's itching to veto this year's six-year highway authorization bill, and lo, Congress is suddenly
deciding to spend less.
We're still talking enormous, to be sure, but at least the House has backed down from the truly gargantuan.
Last week, Don Young's House Transportation Committee passed a $275 billion package that is down a cool
$100 billion from the wish list the Chairman had once said was his bottom line.
This is still more than the $256 billion Mr. Bush proposed in his budget, which is a 17% increase over the last
(and all-time record) six-year plan. But it's a lot better than the $318 billion the Senate passed last month
when it still thought the President was kidding. The entire House is expected to take up the bill this week, and
we hope Mr. Bush holds the line by telling House-Senate conferees they have to meet his number or else.
His veto-rattling has already got Mr. Young to drop his plans to raise the federal gas tax by five cents and
index it to inflation. California drivers, who are already paying more than two bucks a gallon at the pump, are
surely thankful. So are some of the Alaskan Republican's colleagues, who unlike Mr. Young don't hold their
seat for life and weren't relishing the thought of going home this fall to campaign for re-election on a gas tax
hike.
Mr. Young brays about the nation's infrastructure "crisis," but these transportation measures haven't been
about alleviating congested highways or fixing deteriorating roads for some time. These are huge grab-bag
spending bills that target home districts and reward lobbyists. And they are entirely divorced from any kind of
rational policy debate.
Ronald Utt of the Heritage Foundation notes that "$700 billion (inflation adjusted) of federal highway
spending since 1970 has added only 7% to our road system, a performance that makes the proverbial $600
Pentagon hammer a benchmark for cost-effective government."
In this year's House proposal, Mr. Young tackles the nation's transportation crisis by setting aside $1.2 billion
for bike paths. His bill runs more than 800 pages and contains some 2,800 earmarks -- 1,000 more than in
the last highway bill and more than Democrats ever dreamed of when they ran Congress. Funds have been
designated for hiking trails, nature centers, obesity programs for children, battlefield preservation and two
Indian casinos in Michigan.
Mr. Young and his Democratic spending mate, Minnesota's James Oberstar, have also stuck in a little
"reopener clause," so they can revisit the bill within the next 18 months. They've rigged the spending formula
for states in such a way that few of them will meet their "minimum guarantee" for gas-tax payback. So instead
of sticking with this highway spending limit for six years, the tax-and-spend Republicans will be able to go for
the gold again once President Kerry has been elected, or once Mr. Bush is in a second term and more likely
to placate Congress in return for other votes.
Mr. Bush's $256 billion line-in-the-sand isn't anything to celebrate, but at least it's an amount that would be
covered by the current federal gas tax of 18.4 cents per gallon and wouldn't add to the deficit. Furthermore,
by keeping his word Mr. Bush will send the right message to GOP appropriators for other budget fights to
come. Holding his ground now would pay off in the future.
Backing down, by contrast, could open the spending floodgates in an election year, and further alienate
fiscally conservative voters who are already wondering if there is still a point to having a GOP Congress.

Gold's Post-Nasdaq Revival --- Mining Stocks Have Been Surging Since Early 2001 Amid Worries About Economy and Terrorism
Abreast of the Market
Gold's Post-Nasdaq Revival --- Mining Stocks Have Been Surging Since Early 2001 Amid Worries
About Economy and Terrorism
By E.S. Browning
1,398 words
29 March 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AFTER ENDURING THREE years of misery as his tech-stock holdings declined, Rich Garrett last year
shifted his retirement money to a place he considered safer: gold-company stocks.
Sure, gold makes him nervous, too, he says -- especially since most of the mining stocks he owns have been
down this year. But overall, Mr. Garrett's gold stocks have soared since he bought them last year. Today, he
is one of a number of ordinary Americans who have rediscovered an investment that once was considered
hopelessly outmoded -- gold.
"You always get a little twinge when you see your investments go down, but after what I experienced in the
Nasdaq, this is nothing compared to that roller-coaster ride," says Mr. Garrett, a 59-year-old salesman from a
St. Louis suburb. "I feel a lot more comfortable with gold than with the Nasdaq, or the Dow, for that matter."
Since the start of 2001, stocks of the world's gold and silver mining companies, as measured by the Dow
Jones World Precious Metals Index, have more than doubled in value, while the Nasdaq Composite Index
has fallen 21% and the Dow Jones Industrial Average has declined 5%. Last week, amid continuing fears of a
resurgence in terrorism, gold rose 2% to $422.20 an ounce on the Comex division of the New York
Mercantile Exchange, nearing the 15-year high of $426.40 reached during January.
The question is whether gold is a lasting refuge again, or a flash in the pan.
One way to see how attitudes toward gold have evolved is to look at a group of recently minted gold
investors, the clients of St. Louis money manager Wistar Holt. Mr. Holt became interested in gold and
gold-mining stocks in 2001 as a means of protection against the falling market and has been proselytizing for
gold ever since. Because of continuing doubts about the economy and the prospects for the broader market,
he has kept clients mainly in gold stocks, which are easier to buy than the actual commodity. He also has
them invest in bearish mutual funds that bet on market declines, which did badly during the recent stock
boom but have done better in recent weeks.
"The price of gold has gone up because there is a lot of mistrust in the stock market, in all aspects of the
stock market. It is hard for average investors, if there is such a thing, to know who to trust anymore," says
Philip Salembier, a 55-year-old architect and former tech-stock investor who switched to gold about three
years ago on the advice of Mr. Holt.
Some investment advisers are horrified at the thought of putting clients into gold stocks. While gold is
moving gradually back onto some investors' radar screens, it remains the controversial province of a hardy
group of aficionados. In the past, the doubters point out, although gold has sometimes been viewed as a
refuge for nervous investors and a hedge against inflation and dollar weakness, it has been anything but
safe.
The gold market, in fact, in the late 1970s went through its own bubble, which popped noisily in 1980 after
the metal traded briefly above $800 an ounce. The ensuing bear market in gold lasted, with ups and downs,
almost 20 years, and gold fell as low as $254 in 1999. But the metal, and the stocks of the companies that
mine it, took off again around the start of 2001. It was then, as investors realized that the tech boom was over
and began looking for other options, that they rediscovered gold.
Even some fans of gold still see it as a temporary investment. "This is just a short-term way to get through
the bear market. It isn't like we are going to be here our whole lives," says Bob Boly, a civil engineer with the
Metropolitan St. Louis Sewer District and a Holt client.
But, propelled partly by the weakness of the dollar and partly by worries about terrorism and an uncertain
economy, gold stocks have remained strong even as the broader stock market has recovered during the past

--- Page 36 ---
Page 36 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
18 months. The Dow industrials on Friday slipped 5.85 points to 10212.97. For the week, the industrial
average gained 26.37 points, or 0.26%, while the gold index rose 1.78%.
Some of the new gold bugs acknowledge that the stocks have risen so much that they wonder how much
further they can go. And they say friends and colleagues sometimes laugh at them.
"I have discussed gold with my brothers," says Mr. Salembier, the architect. "They just laughed at me. They
said, `That is entirely too risky.'"
Some people "really looked at me strangely" when he talked about gold, says Mr. Boly, the engineer, but he
adds that one co-worker recently decided to switch some of her own investments to gold.
Looking at his account statement, he says, "It kind of scares me. I mean, is this going to go down as things
did after the late '90s? But I am confident that if it does, [Mr. Holt] will get us out."These investors are aware
that the wider stock market has recovered strongly since the fall of 2002, and some have begun wondering
whether it is time to broaden their investments. But Mr. Holt is telling them that the economic outlook remains
uncertain and that gold has more room to run.
"I have even called him and talked to him about when it is going to be time to get out, because the Dow did
go up a lot last year. But I agree that there still are a lot of uncertainties out there. Even though this is volatile,
there is still a lot less uncertainty being in gold stocks," says Mr. Boly, 46. "Sometimes I think we should sell
everything. But what are you going to get into?"
Richard Durley, a research scientist at a large pharmaceutical company in St. Louis, is hedging his bets in
more ways than one. He has put about half his investments in gold stocks and the other half in small
biotechnology companies that he thinks will do well even if the rest of the market has trouble. The main
reason he has turned to gold is that he thinks the growing U.S. trade deficit ultimately will become hard to
fund.
"People have been buying U.S. securities, particularly the Japanese and the Chinese, but they will be buying
gold in the next few years," says Mr. Durley, 61. "I think the U.S. dollar will experience a substantial decline
over the next few years."
People sometimes tell him he is crazy. "Yes, I could be nuts, but this is the way I am seeing it for the next two
years," he says. If the stock market were to retreat, however, and stocks started looking cheaper, he might
shift a bit more money to the biotech stocks, he says. Many of the new gold investors are sticking with it
largely because it has worked.
"I don't know that I understand the technical aspects fully, but it makes sense to go into gold when the
economy isn't that good," says Judy Sewing, a 54-year-old consultant to health-care and fitness companies
who lives in Clayton, a St. Louis suburb. She began using Mr. Holt to manage her money shortly before the
terrorist attacks of Sept. 11, 2001. After those attacks, she says, her friends were impressed at how well her
portfolio held up.
"I was recommending my broker to other people," she says.
Some of the gold investors are people who were burned by the tech bubble and are going to the other
extreme.
"I was heavily invested in the Nasdaq, primarily through my retirement plan, which is a self-directed employee
pension plan," says Mr. Garrett, the salesman. He did so well with Nasdaq tech companies during the bubble
that for a long time he was sure the stocks would bounce back. "I held and held, through 2002."
He gave up after losing half his savings, he says, and happened to see an ad in the St. Louis Post-Dispatch
for Mr. Holt's firm.

Thurday's Markets: Blue Chips See Sharpest Gain Since October --- Techs Lead 170-Point Rebound,
As Investors' Attention Starts To Shift Back to Fundamentals
By E.S. Browning
575 words
26 March 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AFTER SLUMPING for more than a month, the Dow Jones Industrial Average jumped more than 170 points,
its sharpest gain since October, as investors began shifting their attention away from the terrorist threat and
back to corporate fundamentals.
Traders said the sharp gain was stimulated less by the day's news than by a hope that terrorism concerns
may be abating and that earnings and economic news would begin to stimulate stocks again.
Resurgent technology stocks led the rebound, which actually began Wednesday, after the Dow industrials fell
almost to nearly 10000 and then recovered.
With the Dow down for five sessions in a row, and for 10 of the 13 previous sessions, investors were pleased
that they had held the line at the psychologically important 10000 milestone. Short-term investors saw a
chance to ride stocks up, and bearish investors got out of the way.
At the end of regular trading yesterday, the Dow was up 170.59 points, or 1.7%, at 10218.82. It was the
biggest point or percentage gain since Oct. 1, although the industrials still are down 2.3% on the year.
The strongest gains came in technology stocks, which led the market up last year but then suffered the most
during the recent pullback. The Nasdaq Composite Index, laced with technology stocks, jumped 3%, or 57.69
points, to 1967.17 -- its biggest one-day percentage gain since July and its largest point gain since March
2003, more than a year ago. The Nasdaq, which had been up more than 7% for the year as of late January,
before its recent swoon, now is down 1.8% since the year began.
Earlier this month, investors were unnerved by a series of events, including the Madrid bombing, an attack on
Taiwan's president and Israel's assassination of the founder of Palestinian terrorist group Hamas. Now,
investors are shifting their attention elsewhere, said Gordon Fowler, chief investment officer at Philadelphia
money-management firm Glenmede Trust.
"When a terrorist event has happened . . . the market reacts negatively and then tends to bounce back as the
fear of the unknown dissipates," Mr. Fowler said. "It allowed investors to turn off CNN and turn on CNBC
again."
The economic numbers weren't surprisingly strong, but they were reassuring. The Commerce Department
confirmed its earlier estimate that the economy expanded at an annualized rate of 4.1% in the fourth quarter
and that corporate profits surged. Gold, oil and Treasury bonds, all of which had served as refuges during the
market slump, fell in price for a second straight day.
Treasury bonds were hurt by a warning from Federal Reserve Bank of St. Louis President William Poole that
the Fed shouldn't hold interest-rate targets down too long. The 10-year Treasury note fell 8/32, or $2.50 for
each $1,000 invested. The yield, which moves inversely to price, rose to 3.741%. The dollar weakened
slightly against the yen and the euro.
Stock trading volume, however, was only average. So while the rally was encouraging, said Michael Driscoll,
a stock trader at New York brokerage house Bear Stearns, "It's like junk food -- looks good, tastes great, but
it doesn't leave you feeling really filled when all is said and done."

Tech Stocks Rebound, but Dow Industrials Fall for 5th Day
By E.S. Browning
651 words
25 March 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
TECHNOLOGY STOCKS finally rebounded, but the move wasn't enough to break the gloom in the broader
market, which continued its slump.
News of strong new-home sales and rising orders for durable goods didn't lift worries about the earnings
outlook or the threat of terrorism. While the Dow Jones Industrial Average managed to stay above 10000, it
suffered its fifth consecutive decline, falling 15.41 points, or 0.15%, to 10048.23, another three-month low. It
is down 4% so far this year, and has fallen in 10 of the past 13 trading sessions.
Technology stocks, which had been down on 10 of the previous 13 days through Tuesday, enjoyed a small
bounce, but investors saw it more as a technical rebound than a lasting change in direction. The Nasdaq
Composite Index, filled with big and small tech stocks, rose 0.40%, or 7.68 points, to 1909.48, leaving it still
down 4.7% on the year.
"We are in the middle of a correction right now," said Matthew Johnson, head of U.S. stock trading at New
York brokerage firm Lehman Brothers. "It is a risk-averse environment. There isn't anybody in a hurry to go
out and own stocks."
With interest rates near 46-year lows and the economy clearly improving, investors normally would be eager
to buy stocks. But with recent employment reports indicating that hiring remains sluggish, and with the world
suddenly erupting with signs of instability and terrorism, investors are pulling back from stocks.
Now they are waiting for the March employment report, due next week, and for indications whether
first-quarter earnings, due in the middle of April, manage to meet very high investor expectations. Investors
also are waiting to see whether recent violence and other tensions in the Middle East, Spain and Taiwan
quiet down or get worse.
During the day, investors were shaken and stocks fell on news that a bomb had been discovered buried
under passenger-train tracks between Paris and Basel, Switzerland. But stocks recovered after news spread
that the bomb was defused.
As technology stocks were bouncing, some of the investments that have served as refuges for frightened
investors paused in their recent advances.
Gold, oil and Treasury bonds all pulled back, as some investors cashed in some of their recent gains.
The dollar rose 1.6% against the euro following hints of a European interest-rate cut from the head of
Europe's central bank. But the U.S. currency fell 0.5% against the yen in New York trading, hitting its lowest
level since Feb.17.
One reason for the tech-stock bounce was optimism about quarterly results at computer-chip maker Micron
Technology Inc., which reported after regular trading. Micron's results indeed were better than analysts
expected, but traders weren't sufficiently impressed and the stock fell 3.9% in after-hours trading.
During regular trading hours, the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.24%, or 2.62 points, to 1091.33,
down 2% on the year.
The Russell 2000 small-stock index fell 0.59% to 557.63, its lowest finish this year, although it still is up
0.13% on the year.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks were mixed. On the Big Board, where 1.49 billion shares traded, 1,145 stocks rose and 2,089 fell.

NEW YORK -- The dollar split its performance against its major rivals in range-bound trading, with a lack of
fresh economic news and persistent jitters over global terrorism hamstringing activity.
The dollar slipped a little against sterling and the yen but managed to eke out meager gains against the euro
and Swiss franc. Intraday movements, however, show that the dollar ended on weak footing against the
common currency, having given back much of the gains it had chalked up earlier in the global session.
Capital-flows data for the month of January released by the European Central Bank were largely
euro-negative, the highlight of which was a combined 22.4 billion euros ($27.63 billion) net outflow of portfolio
and direct investment from the region. This put the common currency under pressure right away.
But with a nervous market "hunkered down" amid jitters and confusion over mounting geopolitical tensions,
there was little enthusiasm to push currency pairs out of recent ranges, according to Steve Barrow, chief
currency strategist at Bear Stearns.
Slightly hawkish comments from Austrian central bank governor and ECB governing council member Klaus
Liebscher and news that the new leader of Palestinian militant group Hamas is considered a hard-liner
quickly took the wind out of the dollar's sails and helped to push the price of gold higher.
In late trading in New York, the euro was at $1.2328, compared with $1.2333 late Monday in New York, and
off against the yen at 131.60 yen from 131.77 yen. The dollar was at 106.78 yen compared with 106.79, but
up at 1.2590 Swiss francs from 1.2585 francs. Buoyed by merger speculation, sterling strengthened to
$1.8495 from $1.8462 a day earlier.
Meanwhile, Japanese monetary authorities are rumored to be buying dollars around 106.40 to 106.50 yen,
preventing a run down to 106 yen then through the psychological support barrier at 105 yen, market
participants say.

Geopolitical Fears Hurt Stocks --- Dow Industrials Hit a 2004 Low Amid Dearth of Economic News; Investors Turn to Gold, Treasurys
Monday's Markets
Geopolitical Fears Hurt Stocks --- Dow Industrials Hit a 2004 Low Amid Dearth of Economic News;
Investors Turn to Gold, Treasurys
By E.S. Browning
758 words
23 March 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WHEN INVESTORS FOCUS more on world instability than on business fundamentals, stocks tend to fall.
That is what happened yesterday, as the Dow Jones Industrial Average gave up almost 122 points amid
worries about terrorism, Asian stability and the price of oil.
The economic news many investors are waiting for -- the March employment report -- isn't due until next
week. Most first-quarter earnings reports don't arrive until later next month. As they wait, investors have faced
unsettling news reports, including Israel's assassination of a Palestinian leader, tensions between China and
Taiwan, and continuing nervousness about the terrorist bombing in Spain.
"It is fear" that is weighing on stocks, said Todd Leone, head of listed trading at New York brokerage firm SG
Cowen. "It's all the geopolitical stuff."
Late in the day, the Dow fell almost to 10000 before recovering. When the index was down 174.44 points, at
10012.16, bargain hunters stepped in and began buying, and the blue chips bounced.
The Dow finished down 121.85, or 1.20%, at 10064.75. It is down 3.7% on the year, at the lowest level since
Dec. 15. As recently as Feb. 11, the industrials were at a 2 1/2-year high of 10737.70, but have fallen 6.3%
since then. The index hasn't finished below 10000 since Dec. 10.
Investors' focus on unstable world situations could be seen across financial markets. Gold rose $4.90 to a
two-month high of $417.40 an ounce. Gold's jump could have been affected by Asian buying following
Taiwan's close election. The losing candidate challenged the outcome amid controversy over an apparent
attempt on the life of Taiwan's president, who was declared the winner. U.S. Treasury bonds, another refuge
in times of worry, also jumped.
"People are worried about another terrorist attack," said Edgar Peters, chief investment strategist at Boston
money-management firm PanAgora Asset Management. "What we are getting now is a lot of short-term
political uncertainty. That is dominating things because we don't have a lot of fundamental earnings and
economic news."
With interest rates exceptionally low, Mr. Peters said, the stock decline should be limited. Low rates promote
economic growth by helping reduce expenses for companies and individuals alike.
Aside from the political concerns, however, some investors aren't persuaded that first-quarter earnings will
live up to expectations. Most of the earnings reports aren't due until mid-April, and when investors are
nervous, it is common for them to bid stocks down in late March as they wring their hands over the coming
earnings news. Because stocks are up heavily over the past 18 months and because investors have been
counting on strong corporate results, a level of angst is typical at this time.
Those concerns have been fueled by sharp oil-price increases, fueled partly by the Middle East's unsettled
politics and partly by OPEC's plan to cut production. Crude oil futures fell by about $1 on the New York
Mercantile Exchange, amid talk that OPEC might delay output cuts.
"Many culprits are blamed for bringing the major stock averages back to where they were in mid-December,
but dominant among them is the price of oil," wrote Bob Doll, president of Merrill Lynch Investment Managers,
a money-management arm of Merrill Lynch, in a weekly report to clients.
In the stock market, all the major indexes finished at their lowest since December. The Nasdaq Composite
Index, whose many tech stocks led the market gains last year, fell 1.58%, or 30.57 points, to 1909.90, down
4.7% on the year. The broad S&P 500 fell 1.30%, or 14.38 points, to 1095.40, down 1.5% this year.

TOKYO -- For weeks, gold has been relegated to spectator status as upstarts like platinum and silver set the
pace for the precious-metals market.
Some market participants, however, says gold could benefit from a weaker dollar and creeping inflation
fears.
"On the charts, it looks like gold will now definitely hold above $400 after a long consolidation phase," said

better part of the past month just under the psychologically important $400-an-ounce mark. During the same
period, platinum has jumped about 7% and the usually demure silver has outshined all other precious metals
by gaining 10%.
