Shortages Fuel Commodity Prices --- Bottlenecks in Transportation And Slack Production Capacity Are Blamed Instead of Demand
Commodities Report
Shortages Fuel Commodity Prices --- Bottlenecks in Transportation And Slack Production Capacity
Are Blamed Instead of Demand
By Peter A. McKay
1,297 words
18 March 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A LACK OF NEW plants, pipelines and ships to deliver raw materials to keep the world economy running is
helping to keep commodity prices high, adding to the effects of roaring demand from China and the weak
dollar.
Supply-chain bottlenecks have gotten little attention since the big run-up in commodity prices -- including the
jump in crude oil, which yesterday hit a 13-year high above $38 a barrel -- began more than two years ago.
But analysts say delivery problems may already be causing short-term price spikes and could spark
temporary shortages of some items as the world economy struggles to recover.
"The economic danger here is physical dislocation of commodities, not inflation or any of the other usual
suspects," said Steve Strongin, director of commodity research at Goldman Sachs Group Inc.
For commodity prices to trigger inflation at the consumer level, demand would have to rise significantly,
analysts said. Companies otherwise have trouble passing along higher costs to consumers, as the airline
industry's experience with rising fuel prices suggests. Commodities also play a smaller role in the overall
economy than they once did, as a result of the rise in service jobs.
Still, analysts are growing increasingly concerned about production shortages and transportation bottlenecks.
Aluminum-smelting capacity, for example, has grown recently at about half of its average annual rate since
1961, according to Goldman Sachs. The crude-oil market, which is dependent on tankers and refineries,
shows a similar trend. And the Baltic Dry Index, a widely followed average of freight-shipping fees along the
world's busiest routes, is more than triple its 20-year average, as the dwindling availability of ships has
pushed the costs to use them through the roof.
In the past decade, analysts say, many companies have held off adding commodity-production capacity, even
during price booms, because those periods have coincided with events that discouraged such investment.
That is beginning to change, as companies give the green light to the type of projects that would normally
occur much earlier in a global economic recovery. But the game of catch-up could be a tough one. "The
supply chain has just gotten so much longer than it used to be," Mr. Strongin said.
He and other analysts say capacity issues may be pushing up prices of certain commodities, which were
already rising for other reasons. Through yesterday's trading in New York, copper futures have jumped 29%
to $1.3505 a pound in 2004, near a 10-year high on Comex. Silver, which is used in film and other industrial
applications, is up almost 22% to $7.26 a troy ounce, near a 16-year high. And soybeans, which China has
imported heavily, are up 25% to $9.94 a bushel, near a 16-year high at the Chicago Board of Trade.
These jumps come on top of a broad commodity rally that was already under way. Since the start of 2003, the
Dow Jones-AIG Index of 20 commodities rose 36% to 150.223. The Reuters-CRB Futures Index, which
comprises 17 commodities, is up 20% to 281.23.
The dollar's weakness, of course, makes items traded in the U.S. currency, including commodities, appear
more expensive and the price moves more extreme. Since the start of 2003, for instance, the euro has risen
against the dollar by 17% to $1.2237. That means that oil is up only 5% in euro terms, compared with 22% in
dollar terms.
The seeds of the commodity-infrastructure crunch were sown in the 1990s Asian financial crisis, which
discouraged capacity-building in Asia at the worst moment, right before a boom in the region's most populous
country generated demand for greater deliveries of raw materials, Mr. Strongin said. In between, the dot-com
bubble diverted capital from commodities and signaled that the U.S. economy would be increasingly driven by
services, not manufacturing and other traditional, resource-dependent sectors.

--- Page 53 ---
Page 53 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
R. Michael Jones, chairman and chief executive of Platinum Group Metals Ltd., said it has been at least six
years since small mining and metals-exploration firms such as his have garnered much interest from venture
capitalists and other investors. In November, the Vancouver, British Columbia, company raised $2 million
through a private stock placement, which will go toward ramping up precious-metals exploration projects at
eight sites in Canada and South Africa.
But Mr. Jones estimates that it will be at least three years before any of those new mines produce significant
quantities of platinum and palladium. Both are critical components in emissions-reducing car parts. For the
year to date, platinum is up 13% to $906.80 a troy ounce in 2004, near a 24-year high at the New York
Mercantile Exchange. Palladium is up 39% to $275.30 an ounce as auto makers have substituted it for
more-expensive platinum.
A tight shipping market, meanwhile, is causing some disruptions. Robert M. Landry, marketing director at the
Port of New Orleans, said demand in China, along with the weak dollar, is driving up shipping rates and
causing ships that used to service his port to stay in Asia or go to the West Coast rather than to New Orleans.
As a result, total cargo at the port, including imports and exports, fell last year to about nine million tons and is
likely to fall 11% or so this year, Mr. Landry said."If there were more ships, and if rates were lower," he said,
"it would definitely help us."
"When you get price spikes in a market like this," said Stanley Bedows, vice president for institutional
commodities trading at brokerage concern Rand Financial Services, "the sky's the limit."
In other commodity markets:
WHEAT: Futures at the Chicago Board of Trade rose sharply as a pickup in world demand triggered heavy
fund buying. May rose 19.5 cents to $3.915 a bushel after Egypt purchased U.S. wheat in a tender and the
U.N. World Food Program issued a fresh tender for Iraq.
CRUDE OIL: Futures at the New York Mercantile Exchange settled above $38 a barrel for the first time since
October 1990, during the buildup to the Persian Gulf War. April futures rose 70 cents to settle at $38.18 a
barrel, following a Department of Energy report showing U.S. gasoline inventories have tightened.
---
Supply, Demand and More Demand
Delivery bottlenecks and demand from China have pumped up prices of raw
materials and commodities. Futures prices are rising at a pace that far
exceeds
inflation. Many futures prices are seeing double-digit gains since
year-end 2002, even after these gains are adjusted to exclude changes in the
value of the dollar.

Shelling Out for Designer Eggs --- Farmers Launch New Varieties Aimed at Healthy Eaters; Raising a Vegetarian Egg
Shelling Out for Designer Eggs --- Farmers Launch New Varieties Aimed at Healthy Eaters; Raising a
Vegetarian Egg
By Katy McLaughlin
1,661 words
17 March 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THERE ARE DESIGNER shoes, designer sheets, and even designer teapots. And now for breakfast, cooks
can scramble up some designer eggs.
In an effort to capitalize on the latest fashions in healthy-sounding foods, companies are rolling out new types
of eggs that make a range of health claims not usually associated with omelets.
The new products, often referred to as designer eggs, include "omega-3" varieties that come from chickens
raised on special feed that sharply boost their eggs' content of heart-healthy fatty acids. There are lower
cholesterol eggs and organic eggs. There are even "vegetarian" eggs, which come from chickens that aren't
fed any animal products, and "cage-free" eggs. The latter are produced by birds that live within the walls of a
barn but not in individual cages.
Some brands layer multiple weighty attributes onto their eggs. Gold Circle Farms, for example, sells a
cage-free, omega-3, all-vegetarian feed egg that has six times as much vitamin E as the average egg.
Despite the fact that specialty eggs usually cost $1 to $3 more per dozen than regular eggs, they are selling
briskly. Eggland's Best, based in King of Prussia, Pa. -- which sells a patented lower-cholesterol egg -- says
sales rose 26% last year to about $120 million. Egg Innovations, a Port Washington, Wis., company that sells
everything from organic to vegetarian to omega-3 eggs, says sales are up by half from last year. Designer
eggs now account for roughly 5% of the total egg market, up from about 3% five years ago, according to the
Egg Nutrition Center, a division of the National Egg Board, a trade group for producers.
It's happening at a time when conventional-egg prices are already as high as they've been for two decades --
averaging $1.57 a dozen in January, the most recent month for which data are available, up from $1.18 a
year earlier. The price increase stems in part from increased demand driven by the popularity of high-protein
diets like Atkins. In addition, new industry production guidelines that took effect in 2002 have reduced supply
by cutting the number of chickens that most egg-production facilities house in a single cage.
With their high-tech-looking plastic containers stamped with lots of health information, the new designer eggs
may make shoppers accustomed to simply grabbing a foam package of generic Grade A's think they've
wandered into the pharmacy section of the supermarket. Gold Circle Farms, for example, says three to four
of its DHA Omega-3 eggs contain as much heart-healthy fatty acid as three ounces of salmon. Eggland's
Best eggs are marketed as being 16% lower in cholesterol and 25% lower in saturated fat than conventional
eggs and packed with vitamin E, iodine and lutein, a nutrient that is good for eye health.
Still, some health groups, including the American Dietetic Association and the American Heart Association,
say the newfangled eggs aren't all they're cracked up to be. One concern is that consumers on a budget will
waste their money on the eggs, thinking they provide a bigger nutritional punch than they actually do. A more
serious worry is that consumers might think that the eggs can replace foods such as fish and vegetables in
the diet. The Web site of Gold Circle Farms, a unit of Hidden Villa Ranch in Fullerton, Calif., for example,
includes a press release that advises consumers to, "Skip the Fish. Get Your DHA Omega-3 in an Egg."
"It's a nice marketing strategy," says Roberta Anding, a spokeswoman for the American Dietetic Association.
However, "I would probably say you're not going to get enough payback" nutritionally for the extra cost of the
eggs, she says. While designer eggs are being marketed as a breakthrough source of omega-3 fatty acids, a
nutrient that has gotten a lot of attention because research has shown it can protect against heart attacks and
strokes, the American Heart Association hasn't changed its stance on eggs at all since their invention.
"If I were recommending someone increase omega-3 fatty acids, I would recommend they consume fish,"
says Alice Lichtenstein, vice chairwoman of the nutrition committee at the American Heart Association. That's
because to get the same amount of long-chain omega-3s as in a six-ounce serving of salmon, you would

--- Page 58 ---
Page 58 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
have to eat an omelet made with 11 omega-3 eggs -- which would mean consuming nearly eight times as
much cholesterol as the American Heart Association recommends per day.
The current popularity of eggs reflects a dramatic change from previous decades when many Americans
stopped eating them altogether out of fear of high cholesterol. Part of the reason for the shift is that after
decades of recommending that people eat no more than three egg yolks a week, the American Heart
Association published guidelines in 2000 saying that a person could eat an egg a day without affecting blood
cholesterol levels.
Designer-egg skeptics say that a diet that includes fish and a variety of vegetables provides plenty of
omega-3s, lutein and vitamin E, and makes the newfangled eggs unnecessary.
Gold Circle Farms says that its eggs contain the single most valuable type of omega-3, DHA, while fish
contains a combination of DHA and EPA omega-3s. The two abbreviations refer to long-chain fatty acids,
which are most commonly found in fish. Short-chain fatty acids are another kind of omega-3. Found in
vegetable oils, walnuts and flax seeds, they haven't been shown to have the same dramatic health benefits
as the long-chain variety.
Eliseo Guallar, an epidemiologist who has done research on omega-3s at Johns Hopkins Bloomberg School
of Public Health, says the difference between the two long-chain varieties, DHA and EPA, is unknown, and
that the body can convert one to the other. Gold Circle Farms says its own scientific research suggests that
DHA is superior.
Designer-egg producers say it is hard to put a price on good nutrition, and that even pricey eggs are cheap
compared with fish. Charles Lanktree, president of Eggland's Best, which makes a low-cholesterol omega-3
egg, says the company has conducted studies that prove its eggs are healthier than regular eggs. Some
companies also claim that their eggs taste better than regular eggs -- and point to awards as proof. The
American Culinary Institute, an organization of professional chefs based in San Francisco, last year judged
Eggland's Best as the best-tasting eggs on the market.
People concerned about animal rights and the effect of intensive farming on the environment also say that
cage-free, free-range and organic eggs provide alternatives to eggs produced from chickens raised in
crowded cages.
Producers manipulate the nutritional profile of an egg by altering the feed given to chickens. Omega-3 eggs,
for example, are the result of hens fed a diet rich in algae, flax seed or fish oil. Cholesterol in egg yolks can
be lowered slightly by not feeding hens animal fats. Lutein levels are augmented by giving chickens larger
quantities of products like marigold extract, which is usually given to hens to make yolks yellower.
Eggland's Best's low-cholesterol egg has about 180 milligrams of cholesterol, while an ordinary egg has
about 215. The American Heart Association recommends that people limit themselves to about 300
milligrams of cholesterol per day.
---
To Make a Designer Omelet . . .
. . . You've got to crack some cage-free, omega-3 eggs. Here's a primer on
what some of the new specialty-egg terminology means:
TERM: Omega-3
WHAT IT DOES MEAN: Eggs have more omega-3 fatty acids than regular eggs
(usually between 100 and 200 milligrams per egg.)
WHAT IT DOESN'T MEAN: Doesn't necessarily mean the egg has the same kind of
heart-healthy omega-3s found in fish.
TERM: DHA omega-3
WHAT IT DOES MEAN: The egg contains one of the longchain omega-3 fatty
acids.
WHAT IT DOESN'T MEAN: Doesn't mean eggs have as much total long-chain
omega-3s as fish, because fish also contains high levels of another omega-3
that eggs don't have.
TERM: Lutein
WHAT IT DOES MEAN: Some designer eggs contain slightly more lutein, a
nutrient beneficial for eye health, than do regular eggs.

--- Page 59 ---
Page 59 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
WHAT IT DOESN'T MEAN: Doesn't mean specialty eggs have lots of lutein. A
one-third cup of cooked spinach has 20 times as much as most designer eggs.
TERM: Cage-free
WHAT IT DOES MEAN: Chickens have not been housed in cages.
WHAT IT DOESN'T MEAN: Animal were housed within the walls of barn, but did
not necessarily have access to the outdoors.
TERM: Free-range
WHAT IT DOES MEAN: Chickens have access to the outdoors.
WHAT IT DOESN'T MEAN: Doesn't indicate how much space the animal had nor
how much time each day it spent outdoors.
TERM: Certified organic
WHAT IT DOES MEAN: Among other things, chickens never received any
antibiotics, had access to the outdoors, and ate only organic feed , under
U.S. law.
WHAT IT DOESN'T MEAN: Doesn't mean that the nutritional profile of the egg
is different from regular eggs.
TERM: No hormones
WHAT IT DOES MEAN: Just what it says no hormones were ever given to the
animal.
WHAT IT DOESN'T MEAN: Doesn't mean other egg producers use hormones,
because the USDA doesn't permit hormones in poultry.
TERM: No antibiotics
WHAT IT DOES MEAN: Indicates that no antibiotics were used in production.
WHAT IT DOESN'T MEAN: Doesn't mean no antibiotics were used at all. Some
producers do administer antibiotics to sick animals and remove them from
production until they are well again.

Dollar Faces Test This Week: Can It Sustain Recent Upturn?
Currency Trading
Dollar Faces Test This Week: Can It Sustain Recent Upturn?
By Jamie McGeever Dow Jones Newswires
528 words
15 March 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- The dollar starts this week on a strong footing, extending its corrective rally that has lifted it to
multiweek or multimonth highs against many of its major rivals.
The issue now is whether it can sustain the upward momentum that has been fueled by technical factors. Or
will the lack of fresh dollar-positive fundamentals begin to surface and tempt investors to resume their bets
against the dollar?
Among the points of focus for currency traders this week will be U.S. portfolio flows data for February, to be
released today, tomorrow's meeting of the Federal Reserve's rate-policy committee and February's U.S.
consumer price inflation data Wednesday.
Barring an unforeseen shock from any of the above, there's a good chance the dollar will continue to claw
back some of the ground it has lost in recent months. However, an absence of fresh dollar-supportive news
would likely limit any gains.
Currency analysts at Deutsche Bank hold a bearish long-term view on the dollar. They see the euro at $1.35
in 12 months, well up from the $1.2222 level it ended New York trade Friday. But they argue that the
reduction of risk appetite across all financial markets as investors unwind hitherto profitable positions --
stocks are near their 2004 lows, gold is firmly back below $400 a troy ounce and high-yielding currencies
have all fallen sharply in recent weeks -- could support the dollar in the near term.
Late Friday in New York, the euro was trading at $1.2222, well below $1.2338 late Thursday in New York,
and was lower against the yen at 135.56 yen from 136.93 yen. The dollar was up at 1.2835 Swiss francs from
1.2702 francs and little changed against the yen at 110.89 yen from 110.94 yen late Thursday in New York.
This theme of reducing risk was highlighted Thursday by the terrorist attacks in Madrid. In knee-jerk moves,
stock markets slumped, bonds rose and the dollar fell.
But Friday, currency-market participants reverted to their recent unwinding of so-called carry trades in which
low-yielding currencies like the Swiss franc and dollar are borrowed to invest in high-yielding units such as
sterling and the Australian and New Zealand dollars. As speculators pared back on carry trades, the dollar hit
near three-month highs against its Australian and New Zealand counterparts and a near two-month peak
against sterling. The pound fell as low as $1.7905 Friday from above $1.90 less than a month ago. Both the
Australian and New Zealand dollars have lost almost 10% of their value against the dollar in the last month.
A major source of support for the dollar lately has been the sizable dollar-buying intervention in currency
markets from Asian central banks, especially the Bank of Japan. Indeed, the scale of yen selling from
Japanese monetary authorities has prompted comments from Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan and Treasury
Secretary John Snow that it's neither sustainable nor healthy.

Decline Leaves Dow Industrials Down for Year
Wednesday's Markets
Decline Leaves Dow Industrials Down for Year
By E.S. Browning
821 words
11 March 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THE STOCK SELLING accelerated, wiping out all of this year's gains for the Dow Jones Industrial Average,
as fears spread that a long-forecast stock pullback finally may be under way.
The blue-chip average fell 160.07 points, or 1.53%, to 10296.89, the sharpest percentage decline since
September and heaviest point drop since May.
Suddenly, the Dow is down 1.5% on the year, back at a level not seen since Dec. 19. It has been a year since
the Dow industrials have fallen as much as 5% from a high, but they now are down 4.1% from the 2 1/2-year
high of 10737.70 hit on Feb. 11.
Volume on the New York Stock Exchange, 1.65 billion shares, was the heaviest in a month. Nasdaq volume,
2.1 billion, also was the biggest in a month.
Why? At the root, traders said, was simply a growing sense among short-term investors that stocks have lost
momentum. With stocks trending down since mid-February, bullish investors had tried to bid them back up as
the day began, but the selling pressure won out.
The Nasdaq Composite Index rose 50% last year and the Dow industrials rose 25%, leaving investors sitting
on big profits. Now, some of them are taking money off the table. Short sellers, bearish investors who borrow
stock and sell in hopes of profiting by buying it back cheaper after a decline, accelerated their selling.
Within the Dow, the big losers were some of last year's strongest stocks, notably economically sensitive
shares such as Alcoa, Caterpillar, International Paper and DuPont. Only two of the 30 Dow industrials stocks
managed gains: Cigarette-maker Altria, formerly known as Philip Morris, a classic refuge because of its
steady sales and large dividend, and Procter &Gamble, a defensive stock that just offered a strong earnings
projection.
"We are finally getting our first correction in more than a year," said David Briggs, head of stock trading at
Pittsburgh mutual-fund group Federated Investors. "Maybe we have come a little bit too far, too fast."
If there was a catalyst for the selling, Mr. Briggs said, it was Friday's news of disappointing February job
gains, as well as nervousness that John Kerry could be elected president and do away with some of the Bush
administration's tax cuts. That led to the fear that stock gains this year might be overdone.
As for Mr. Briggs, he says he is getting ready to buy. "This is setting up a nice little trading opportunity for a
seasonal spring rally," he said. "Some time in next couple of sessions I think it is probably going to be wise to
put a little bit of money to work."
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index, whose many consumer-product stocks have held up better
than most other stocks, has fallen less than the Dow. After yesterday's decline of 1.46%, or 16.69 points, to
1123.89, the S&P 500 is up 1% on the year and is only 2.9% off its own Feb. 11 high.
The Nasdaq Composite Index, dominated by the technology stocks that were last year's big winners, is 8.8%
off its January high and is at its lowest level since Dec. 22. Yesterday, which was the fourth anniversary of the
day when the Nasdaq composite reached a record 5048.62, marking the beginning of the bursting of the
technology bubble, the index fell 1.55%, or 31.01 points, to 1964.15. It is down 2% for the year.
The dollar strengthened against the euro but weakened against the yen. Gold fell after rising Tuesday. Crude
oil also declined. Treasury bonds, which have soared since the soft jobs report, put in small additional gains
as money fled stocks. A weak economy, with low interest rates, is good for Treasurys.

Owning a Piece Of California --- First Batch of Record $15 Billion Bond Issue To Hit Market in June; Who Should Buy Them
Owning a Piece Of California --- First Batch of Record $15 Billion Bond Issue To Hit Market in June;
Who Should Buy Them
By Jeff D. Opdyke and Aaron Lucchetti
1,191 words
10 March 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
IT'S CALIFORNIA'S latest Gold Rush. Now investors have to decide whether they want a piece of it.
Last week, Golden State voters approved a $15 billion municipal-bond offering, the largest muni issue ever,
to help shore up the state's ailing finances. Thanks to California's low credit rating -- just two or three notches
above junk status -- the bonds' projected yields will be higher than most other state muni bonds. But so will
the risks.
Although the chances of default are slim, buyers of the California bonds could experience a bumpy ride if
questions mount about the state's financial health. In that case, prices of the bonds would drop, and investors
who try to sell their bonds before they mature would take a hit.
The bond offering from California is the latest and biggest in a flood of muni-bonds issuances over the past
two years. The stock-market collapse and economic slowdown beginning in 2000 put a big hole in many state
budgets, and many have used debt to help plug it. California, thanks to the decline in its huge high-tech
industry, experienced a more severe drop in revenues than virtually any other state -- prompting Gov. Arnold
Schwarzenegger to champion the bond issue as a remedy.
Details of the California bond sale are expected to be finalized in April at the earliest, with the bonds being
rated in May. The first allotment -- about $6 billion or more -- could go on sale as soon as June. The bonds
with the shortest duration are expected to mature in nine years; the longest-dated bonds will mature in 14
years.
In general, owning municipal bonds gives investors a break come tax time, because the interest income is
typically exempt from state and federal taxes. In most states that perk is reserved for residents of the state
issuing the bonds.
Yet Californians might not be the only investors interested in these bonds. Residents of states that don't
impose an income tax -- among them Texas, Washington, Nevada and Wyoming -- can reap the full tax
benefits of any state's muni bonds. Indiana and Utah, meanwhile, allow investors to exempt the interest paid
on the munis of any state.
Moreover, investors in states that impose low personal-income taxes -- such as Michigan, Colorado and
Illinois -- may find the California bonds more appealing than those offered by their home state with lower
yields.
It's still too early to tell exactly what the California bonds will yield, though the early read is that they'll pay
investors anywhere between 0.20 to 0.60 percentage point above the highest-rated bonds from other states.
Right now, that would be roughly equivalent to a rate between 3.3% and 4.3% depending on the bond's
duration, though that's very likely to change by the time the bonds are actually priced.
While 4% may not sound exciting -- even in this low-rate era -- to someone living in California in the top tax
bracket, it's the same as getting a 6.1% yield on a taxable bond.
The yield also compares well with other states. A 10-year general-obligation bond from Florida, for instance,
currently yields about 3.2%.
Some of the California bonds may carry so-called private bond insurance that guarantees the principal and
interest payments should the bonds default. The downside: The extra cost of this insurance results in slightly
lower yields.

--- Page 68 ---
Page 68 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Buying the California bonds will be fairly simple: Brokerage firms such as Merrill Lynch &Co. and Morgan
Stanley will have access to the bonds at the offering price. Like most municipal issues, the bonds will be
issued with a face value of $5,000 apiece. That amount typically also is the minimum investment.
On the day the bonds are first issued they'll be sold by a large syndicate of brokerage houses and banks led
by Lehman Brothers. Investors who are clients of firms in the syndicate should have no problem buying the
California bonds through their broker. But even if a particular firm isn't represented in the syndicate, that firm's
clients will likely be able to buy bonds for its investors at the offering price in the so-called gray market.
Getting in at the offering price may be the best hope for locking in the best rates. Investors who buy the
bonds after they've begun trading may well have to accept smaller yields, as the market bids up the value of
the bonds. (Investors will also have a chance to buy into California's separate $12.3 billion education-bond
issue, which is expected to go to market later this year.)
As with any investment, of course, there are risks to owning a piece of California. For one, bonds can go into
default, meaning the state, at some point, might not have the resources to pay. Because of California's low
debt rating, that fear does creep up. Yet while municipal-bond defaults have happened before -- most notably
in California's Orange County in 1994 -- they are exceedingly rare.
Though California's debt rating is slightly above junk-bond status now, there are signs it could soon improve.
Standard &Poor's, the New York bond-rating firm, recently said it would review California's bond rating with
an eye toward upgrading it, which would mean rising prices for the bonds investors already own.
The bigger risk for investors is that interest rates will rise. That would drive down the value of the bonds in
the secondary market because investors are more eager to own newer issues paying higher rates. One way
to alleviate that risk is to own the bonds to maturity since doing so means an investor would be paid back in
full.
Peter Coffin, president of Breckinridge Capital, a Boston investment-management firm specializing in
municipal bonds, says he plans to buy some of the bonds for his national accounts if the yields are high
enough: "It's a volatile credit, and their fiscal house is not completely in order, but I'm not worried about
California defaulting at all."
---
California Dreaming?
Muni bonds are generally most advantageous to in-state investors who
don't pay state or federal taxes on the income. However, the yield on the
coming California bonds will appeal to investors in no-tax states and may be
high enough to attract investors in states with low personal income tax
rates. The state tax rates below are for residents with annual income
between $100,000 and $300,000.

THE NASDAQ COMPOSITE Index sagged to its first finish below 2000 since the day after Christmas, as
worries persisted about the economy's strength and about the risk that the stock market has become
overextended.
Treasury-bond prices, which benefit when the economy is soft and interest rates are low, continued to soar.
The yield of the 10-year Treasury note, which falls when the price rises, dropped to 3.718%, the lowest level
since July. Its three-day plunge in yield has been the sharpest since August 2002, a time when investors
were uncertain about the future of the economy and the stock market.
With the Nasdaq composite's decline of 0.68%, or 13.62 points, to 1995.16, that index now is down 0.4%
since the year began, the first time this year that it has been under water. The swoon comes as the Nasdaq
composite, which is dominated by big tech stocks, today marks the fourth anniversary of its record close of
5048.62, on March 10, 2000. That record, in turn, marked the bursting of the tech-stock bubble and, in many
investors' minds, the onset of the long bear market.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 72.52 points, or 0.69%, to 10456.96, its lowest close since Jan. 13. It
now is almost flat on the year -- up just 0.03%.
The immediate catalyst for the decline was Friday's disappointing report on February job creation. More
broadly, investors fret that with the Nasdaq composite up 50% last year and the Dow industrials up 25%, any
hint of weakness in the business climate raises questions about whether stocks are overdue for a pullback.
"People are saying, `If we aren't getting that payroll jump, what is the next push to get the economy going
again?' " said Todd Clark, head of listed trading at Wells Fargo Securities, a brokerage arm of Wells Fargo in
San Francisco.
"This is a pretty crucial area for the Nasdaq market to hold," he said, adding that if the Nasdaq continues to
sag, the tech-stock selling could accelerate as short-term investors cash in some of their big profits from last
year. If the Nasdaq holds its gains here, however, the rally could resume, as investors try to get in at levels
they haven't seen all year, Mr. Clark said.
Tomorrow is the first anniversary of the market's low point during the Iraq war, the most significant slump
since the current rally began in October 2002. Since that low last March 11, neither the Dow industrials nor
the broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index has fallen as much as 5% from a high. As the months have
passed, analysts have begun warning that a 5% or 10% pullback is inevitable, but the indexes have defied
such predictions.
The Dow now is down only 280.74 points, or 2.6%, since hitting a 2 1/2-year high of 10737.70 on Feb. 11.
The S&P 500, which fell 0.58%, or 6.63 points, to 1140.58, still is up 2.6% this year. It is just 1.5% off its own
recent high. The more-volatile Nasdaq composite, which has fallen more than 5% from a high more than once
in recent months, already is 7.4% below its 2 1/2-year high, hit in January.
Part of yesterday's problem was General Electric's sale of 119.4 million new shares, which raised $3.8 billion
but knocked down GE's price as the value of existing shares was diluted.
Texas Instruments, whose shares had more than doubled over the past 17 months, fell 1% even though the
chip maker offered an upbeat business outlook in comments that came after regular trading hours on
Monday.

Halliburton Warns About Liquidity --- Company Says It Could Face Billing Problems, Refunds Stemming from Iraq Work
Leading the News
Halliburton Warns About Liquidity --- Company Says It Could Face Billing Problems, Refunds
Stemming from Iraq Work
By Russell Gold and Neil King Jr.
774 words
9 March 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A3
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Halliburton Co., facing a barrage of questions about its handling of contracts to support the U.S. in Iraq, said
that refunding or withholding billing on substantial portions of its government work could "materially and
adversely affect our liquidity."
The disclosure, made in Halliburton's annual 10-K filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission,
comes as the Pentagon pressures the company to prepare final documentation for nearly $4 billion of work
the U.S. government already has paid for. Those payments may run afoul of government contracting rules
that limit how much can be paid before the work has been detailed.
According to Army officials, Halliburton could have to withhold billing on more than $400 million from other
work until it completes the accounting, a process that could take months. However, Halliburton spokeswoman
Wendy Hall said the company is ahead of schedule in finalizing details of the contracts.
The Houston energy-services and engineering company has two big contracts with the military to help rebuild
Iraq's oil infrastructure and to keep U.S. troops fed and housed. Through 2003, the company had booked
more than $3 billion in revenue for the work, under which it bills the government for its expenses and earns a
1% guaranteed profit.
The billing issue comes as federal officials investigate the company's performance on several fronts.
Halliburton last month agreed to withhold billing on $140 million for food services pending a Pentagon
investigation into how various subcontractors billed for meals served last year in Kuwait and Iraq. In addition,
Pentagon auditors suspended payment on more than $36 million in submitted food-service invoices at seven
dining facilities. The Pentagon also has opened a criminal investigation into a possible $61 million overcharge
for the delivery of fuel to Iraq from Kuwait.
Halliburton warned it may need to either make refunds to or withhold billing the government for an additional
"substantial" amount, which would materially hurt its liquidity, the company said.
Such a squeeze could require the company to borrow further. Its credit ratings remain at the low end of
investment grade. A Moody's Investors Service analyst said the credit-rating concern doesn't have any plans
to downgrade Halliburton's debt rating because the company had sufficient cash and receivables to pay its
near-term debt.
Because the company must pay its subcontractors before it collects from the government, it had racked up
about $800 million in out-of-pocket expenses at the end of December. Dave Lesar, chairman, president and
chief executive, says the company has issued debt to cover the outstanding receivables. "We've financed a
lot of this war," he said last week.
This rising debt is particularly troublesome for the company as it prepares to fully fund a trust to settle current
and future asbestos lawsuits and as it faces cash payments on a troubled construction project in Brazil.
Between the summer of 2003 and January, the company added $2.7 billion in long-term borrowings to fund
its asbestos trust and work in Iraq.
The company also acknowledged its internal-controls systems were overwhelmed by the amount of work the
Army assigned to it. The company says it has identified shortcomings and has taken steps to remedy the
problems, including enlarging the procurement staff and creating a "Tiger Team" to work with subcontractor
management and other issues.
Still, there are problems. Halliburton owes the Event Source, a Salt Lake City subcontractor, $87 million, of
which $50 million is past due, said Phil Morrell, Event Source chief executive. He said the company, which
has served 32,000 troops in seven dining facilities, hasn't been paid since mid-November. Ms. Hall said

--- Page 73 ---
Page 73 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Halliburton is reviewing all its dining-hall invoices and paying them once they are validated. She said 19
invoices from Event Source are being reviewed, of which seven are more than 30 days past due. She said
Halliburton understands the hardship on the subcontractors and is trying to speed the process along.
The company is being investigated on a number of other fronts, too: The Justice Department is reviewing
possible overcharges related to Halliburton's contract to provide logistics support for the Army in the Balkans,
according to the filing. The SEC has subpoenaed documents related to accounting for long-term construction
projects and is looking into alleged illegal payments related to a Nigerian plant. And a U.S. Treasury
Department office in January asked for more information about Halliburton's presence in Iran.

Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / What's Hot...and Not: Bets on Jobs Report Proved Perilous --- Employment Data Shock Investors...
Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / What's Hot...and Not: Bets on Jobs Report Proved
Perilous --- Employment Data Shock Investors, Currency Traders; Treasury Prices Skyrocket
By Justin Lahart
833 words
8 March 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A LOT OF TRADERS and professional investors spent much of last week placing bets that Friday's U.S.
employment report would be a barn burner. They ended up getting burned.
Economists had expected the economy added 125,000 nonfarm jobs in February, but trading-floor chatter
was putting payrolls as high as 200,000. When the number was released and hit at just 21,000, plenty of
people were caught wrong-footed.
"It's a shock," said Mike McGlone, Treasury analyst at ABN Amro. "People were not set up for this kind of
weakness.
The pumped-up expectations for the jobs number was due mostly to a report early last week from the Institute
for Supply Management that showed manufacturing employment rising to its highest level in over 16 years,
despite a slight slowdown in manufacturing activity in February from January. Plenty of traders and money
managers reasoned that information bode well for the main employment report from the U.S. Bureau of Labor
Statistics.
"People saw that strong employment component [of the manufacturing report] and thought there was a good
chance the jobs report would be better than expected," said Brian Reynolds, chief market strategist at M.S.
Howells &Co., which advises professional money managers. "Everybody got caught short here."
Treasurys were among the asset classes that saw the sharpest reversal off the jobs number. Down on the
week ahead of the employment report's release, the 10-year note rallied hard Friday, pushing its yield down
to 3.85% -- its lowest level since July. Bond yields move in the opposite direction of prices.
Mr. Reynolds believes yields have reached levels where they may spur another round of heavy mortgage
refinancing activity like that seen in mid-2003. That could prompt buying by mortgage lenders, which hold
Treasurys to hedge risk, and send yields even lower.
Meantime, Mr. McGlone said, the belief that the weak employment report has pushed any rate rise by the
Federal Reserve far into the future may embolden financial institutions to borrow at low short-term rates and
buy higher-yielding long-term debt. Such "carry trades," which tend to push long-term Treasury yields down,
can be highly profitable, but also carry heavy risks for players who don't get out when the Fed goes into
tightening mode.
"The carry trades should be piled on," said Mr. McGlone. "At some point that creates a massive correction.
But picking that point is like picking the top of the stock market in the 1990s." Carry trades based on bets
about U.S. rates have been a key play for global money managers for months now.
Currency traders were equally flummoxed by the jobs weakness. The dollar started moving higher against
other major currencies early in the week, pushing against, and breaking, technical levels that traders had
been keying off. Further adding to the dollar strength, Japanese officials stressed that they have no problem
continuing to buy the dollar to keep the yen week.
The yen weakness, seen as supportive of Japanese exporters, along with economic reports pointing to
continued business confidence and higher capital spending, helped push the Nikkei Stock Average up 4.5%
on the week -- the best performance of any of the major stock markets. The Tokyo stock market had closed
out the week, however, before the U.S. employment news came out.
The yen stayed weak against the dollar after the jobs report, thanks, according to currency traders, to
massive dollar buying by the Bank of Japan. But other major currencies reversed course and strengthened,
with the euro taking back nearly all of its losses against the U.S. currency for the week.

--- Page 76 ---
Page 76 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Meanwhile, chatter that China might move to push up its currency's peg against the U.S. dollar heated up
through the week, but officials moved to quash the speculation, and some observers noted that the weakness
in the yen against the dollar makes it less appealing for China to move up the peg in the near term.
Gold closed at its lowest level of the year midweek but then picked up Friday, mostly on the weakness in the
dollar. The dollar reversal pushed oil, too, into the winning side of the ledger by the end of the week -- even
amid speculation that the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries will cut crude protection by less than
planned when it meets April 1.
Finally, U.S. stocks were oddly unscathed by the jobs report, with the Dow Jones Industrial Average inching
up Friday to close out the week with a slight gain. The Wall Street view seemed to be with rates low, stocks
can still do OK. But the impetus for low rates -- a weak labor market -- is hard to bill as a positive for the
economy, and thus profits, in the long run.

Mutual Funds Pitch Commodities to Investors
Fund Track
Mutual Funds Pitch Commodities to Investors
By Christopher Oster
1,180 words
5 March 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
THERE'S GOLD IN them thar funds. And copper and oil and lumber . . .
So goes a current marketing pitch from the mutual-fund industry. With commodities prices soaring and
Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan concerned about inflation, some fund companies are urging
people to protect themselves against higher prices by putting money into mutual funds that specialize in
commodities.
Nothing new in that for investors who can tolerate a lot of volatility. There are dozens of gold and
natural-resources mutual funds that have been around for decades, most of which invest in stocks of
gold-mining firms, oil producers and timber companies. Gold funds in particular have been favorites of
investors who are alarmed about the economic future and see such funds as the next best thing to owning
the precious metal itself.
But several mutual-fund companies say commodities these days aren't just for the sky-is-falling crowd, and
that precious metals, oil and paper deserve a place in the portfolio of any investor who sees inflation ahead.
Further, more fund providers are introducing portfolios that invest not in the stocks of commodity-related
companies, but in the hard assets themselves through various financial instruments that track commodity
prices.
Merrill Lynch &Co. and Mellon Financial Corp.'s Dreyfus Funds each have filed plans with the Securities and
Exchange Commission in recent months to launch such funds. Two exchange-traded funds that will track the
price of gold bullion also are in the works.
The new entrants will join two commodity-tracking funds currently available to investors. The $472 million
Oppenheimer Real Asset Fund was launched in 1997 and has returned an average 18.1% a year during the
past five years, a period during which the overall market has been virtually flat.
Pimco Funds, a unit of Germany's Allianz AG, launched its Pimco CommodityRealReturn Strategy Fund in
November 2002 and the fund jumped 29.1% last year, slightly ahead of the S&P 500-stock index and the
Dow Jones Industrial Average. In little more than a year, the fund has grown to more than $2.1 billion in
assets.
Here is how the price-tracking commodity funds work: Rather than buying stocks of companies, or buying
actual hard assets such as gold, the funds purchase futures, options and other financial instruments in a mix
designed to mimic a broad commodities index. Since the outlay to buy the financial contracts is small, the rest
of the funds' assets are invested in fixed-income securities, which essentially serve as collateral accounts for
the highly volatile financial instruments. When the instruments lose or gain in value, the swings are tempered
by the bond holdings.
The renewed interest in commodities funds is little surprise to some. "There's always a market out there for
scare products, whether it's gold or defense stocks," says Don Cassidy, senior research analyst at Lipper
Inc. in Denver. "There are folks out there that are hard-asset folks, and at the merest whiff of inflation, they're
back after hard assets again."
Commodity-related funds have found buying assets directly poses numerous logistical problems, and buying
individual stocks when trying to take advantage of rising commodities prices can be trickier than it looks.
Some oil companies and gold-mining concerns hedge their exposure to commodities with the use of futures
contracts, for example, making it difficult for investors to know the true impact commodity prices will have on
those share prices.
"Every investor should have a 5% or 10% allocation to commodities," says Kevin Baum, manager of
Oppenheimer Real Asset.

--- Page 80 ---
Page 80 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The gains of commodity funds can be significant. Three resource-company-focused funds run by U.S. Global
Investors -- Global Resources Fund, Gold Shares Fund and World Precious Metals Fund -- were up 99.6%,
67.1% and 92.7%, respectively, last year.
But Frank Holmes, chief investment officer of U.S. Global Investors, which has about $1 billion under
management in funds focused on natural resources, says the downside can be equally dramatic. U.S. Global
Investors' Gold Shares and World Precious Metals funds were down 57.4% and 41.1% in 1997, and Global
Resources was down 38.5% in 1998.
The funds that track commodity indexes can be at least as volatile. Oppenheimer Real Asset, the first of the
group, has had big moves both up and down since its launch in 1997. It has gained as much as 44.4% in a
single year and lost as much as 44.9% -- and its single year loss or gain has never been smaller than 22%.
And not all of the funds are straightforward bets on commodities indexes. While Mr. Baum at Oppenheimer
says he keeps his cash account invested in high-quality, short-term, fixed-income securities to minimize the
fund's exposure to interest-rate swings, the Pimco fund invests its cash in TIPS, or Treasury Inflation
Protected Securities. But putting money into bonds carries its own risks as rising interest rates push bond
prices lower, including those of TIPS, since bond prices move in the opposite direction of yields.
Two exchange-traded funds linked to the price of gold, the first of their type, currently are awaiting approval
from the SEC. ETFs are similar to mutual funds but their shares trade throughout the day on exchanges like
listed securities.
Both planned offerings will attempt to give investors a means of betting on gold prices without actually going
out and buying bullion.
The World Gold Council, an association of gold-mining companies, filed to launch the Equity Gold Trust, and
Barclays Global Investors last month filed to launch its iShares Comex Gold Trust. Equity Gold Trust will set
its net asset value based on prices in the London market and the iShares fund will link its net asset value to
the Comex gold-futures contracts.
---
SPLIT DECISION: Research firm Morningstar Inc. said investors should consider selling Pimco stock funds
run by PEA Capital LLC following improper trading allegations, but noted that its recommendation didn't apply
to Pimco bond funds run by Pacific Investment Management Co.
New Jersey Attorney General Peter Harvey last month charged several Pimco units with civil fraud for
allegedly allowing certain investors to rapidly trade in and out of its funds, contrary to stated policies regarding
such trading. The Pimco units said the trading didn't violate the prospectus language for their funds.
Morningstar analyst Eric Jacobson said he found the charges and evidence against PEA Capital to be
"extremely troublesome," resulting in the recommendation that investors consider selling the funds after
reviewing their tax situation. But Mr. Jacobson said Morningstar made no change to its opinion on funds run
by Pimco's California-based fixed-income operation because the case presented by New Jersey "appears
less compelling" than with the stock funds.
A Pimco spokesman said the firm agreed with some of Morningstar's assessment regarding its bond
operations, but it disagreed with what was said about PEA Capital. He declined to elaborate.
-- John Shipman

February unemployment rate and the number of new jobs created, said stock trader Michael Driscoll at New
York brokerage firm Bear Stearns. "The fact that it is an election year adds additional importance," because
the numbers will be scrutinized to see whether government stimulus is creating new jobs, he added.
The dollar hit a three-month high against the euro amid speculation that the European Central Bank could
hint, at today's meeting, of plans to cut European interest rates. Gold declined again, to a new three-month
low. Oil prices, which had been rising, fell slightly, as did Treasury bonds.

expected last month. An economic report from Federal Reserve banks showed good overall economic growth
but only modest job creation.

moves inversely to price, rose to 4.059%. The 30-year bond was down 4/32 to yield 4.908%.

Stocks Decline on Fed Chairman's Remarks About Rates
Tuesday's Markets
Stocks Decline on Fed Chairman's Remarks About Rates
By E.S. Browning
434 words
3 March 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ALAN GREENSPAN raised the specter of higher interest rates, and investors didn't like it.
Already weakening, stocks sagged at about 1:30 p.m. EST yesterday, after the Federal Reserve chairman
told the Economic Club of New York that "at some point," the Fed's target short-term rate for loans between
banks, currently at a 45-year low of 1%, "will have to rise to a more neutral state."
The Dow Jones Industrial Average gave back most of Monday's 94-point gain, finishing down 86.66 points, or
0.81%, at 10591.48 -- its sharpest drop since January. The blue-chip average still is up 1.3% so far this year.
"It was a little profit-taking and a little squeamishness, with Greenspan talking about potentially raising rates
at some point," said John O'Donoghue, senior stock trader at New York brokerage firm Credit Suisse First
Boston.
At its weakest, the Dow was down more than 109 points, but it rebounded a bit as investors realized that they
already knew that a rate increase is inevitable at some point.
Stocks were affected by a sharp jump in the dollar, which hit its highest level against the euro since Dec. 11.
Shares that benefit from a weak dollar pulled back.
The euro fell 1.8% to $1.2217, its sharpest drop since July 23, 2002 -- 17 months ago. It was hurt by
concerns that the European Central Bank, which meets tomorrow, is facing pressure to lower interest rates,
which would hurt the euro. The yen hit its lowest level since November.
Gold and oil prices also fell, responding to the dollar's strength. Treasury bonds declined on fears of higher
rates.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.59%, or 6.86 points, to 1149.10, still up 3% this year. The
Nasdaq Composite Index fell 0.88%, or 18.15 points, to 2039.65, up 1.8% this year.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. On the Big Board, 1.45 billion shares traded, 1,440 stocks rose and 1,829 fell.
Bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 13/32, or $4.0625 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, rose to 4.041%. The 30-year bond was down 23/32 to yield 4.900%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 110.11 yen, up from 108.96, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.2217 from $1.2444.

Possibility of Increase In Rates Does Little To Upset Risk Forecast
Options Report
Possibility of Increase In Rates Does Little To Upset Risk Forecast
By Kopin Tan Dow Jones Newswires
332 words
3 March 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Concerns about rising interest rates spurred selling in the stock and Treasurys markets, but
did little to rile the option market's short-term risk forecast.
But while option traders don't anticipate big swings in the stock indexes over the next few weeks, they are
less confident about stocks' stability later in the year.
Take, for instance, popular options on the Standard &Poor's 500 Index; the Nasdaq 100 Tracking Stock, or
QQQ; or the Semiconductor Holdrs Trust, or SMH. Anticipated volatility of their March options remains
several percentage points below that of options expiring in May or after, said Wall Street Access's chief
market strategist Peter Dunay.
This is a sign some traders are selling short-term options, essentially betting that the indexes or
exchange-traded funds won't rise or fall beyond certain points soon. At the same time, they hedge their
exposure by buying longer-dated options that expire later this year.
Without much by way of economic data, traders took their cues from Federal Reserve Chairman Alan
Greenspan, who told economists how interest rates will inevitably have to rise.
The dollar rose and gold slipped, and gold-related puts traded actively. The Philadelphia Stock Exchange
Gold &Silver Index, or XAU, fell 3.24 points to 97.71. Its April 95 puts gained $1.10 to $3.50 as 2,580
contracts traded, compared with 717 contracts outstanding.
Meanwhile, option volume remained robust last month but cooled from January's frenzied levels, according to
the month-end data from the Options Clearing Corp. More than 86.3 million equity options traded in February,
a 73% jump from 2003, while index option volume rose 2.8% to about 6.1 million contracts. Daily average
option volume was about 4.86 million -- well ahead of 2003's record of 3.6 million but down from January's
5.72 million.

Investments in Rockville, Md. "The first day, the first couple of days or even the first week of the month can
be helped by cash flows into stock funds," he said.
Economic data were mixed. Manufacturing activity fell in February, but an index of employment in
manufacturing businesses jumped, fueling hope that broader job creation soon might rise. Construction
spending fell in January and personal incomes in January were weaker than expected. A Commerce

STOCKS MANAGED a second modest rebound as the dollar continued to recover and investors began
buying stocks that had suffered declines this month.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average did fall 21.48 points, or 0.20%, to 10580.14, held back by sharp declines
in Boeing and United Technologies, both Dow components. Worries circulated about the cancellation of a

falling close to 2000.
The Dow industrials are up 1.2% on the year; the Nasdaq is up 1.5%. Stocks had been falling since the
middle of this month. After rising almost 284 points, or 2.7%, for the year through Feb. 11, the Dow has given
back almost 158 points, or 1.5%, since then.
Stocks started yesterday with more declines, pushed down by a spate of mediocre economic news. They
turned up after some investors decided some shares had fallen low enough to be attractive.
"You did see people looking to pick up those names that they thought had fallen too far, too fast," said Jack
Caffrey, U.S. stock strategist at J.P. Morgan Private Bank in New York.
The dollar strengthened for a second straight day, hitting its highest level against the euro since Feb. 2, after
French Prime Minister Jean-Pierre Raffarin echoed German Chancellor Gerhard Schroder's call for a drop in
European interest rates.
The soft U.S. economic news included an eight-month low in demand for new homes in January, a January
decline in orders for durable goods such as appliances and cars, and a rise in new unemployment claims last
week. Still, the news fell only mildly short of expectations, and investors shrugged it off.
They also looked past a warning by Starbucks that sales growth will slow.

declined.

Stocks Climb As the Dollar Gains on Euro
Wednesday's Markets
Stocks Climb As the Dollar Gains on Euro
By E.S. Browning
633 words
26 February 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCKS FINALLY bounced after five days of declines, as the dollar surged against the euro and Federal
Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan offered upbeat comments on the economy.
The Nasdaq Composite Index, whose many tech stocks had been sagging for five weeks, rebounded 0.87%,
or 17.54 points, to 2022.98, now up 1% so far this year. The tech-heavy index again avoided finishing below
2000, something it hasn't done since Dec. 26.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 35.25 points, or 0.33%, to 10601.62, up 1.4% on the year.
Investors were reassured that "the dollar has stopped dramatically sliding lower," and that Mr. Greenspan
appears still to be vigilant on the economy, said Brian Pears, head stock trader at Victory Capital
Management, the money-management arm of KeyCorp in Cleveland.
But for several weeks now, he added, the main driver for the stock market has been its need to stop and
digest following the heavy gains staged last year and in January. The Dow industrials rose 25% last year and
the Nasdaq rose 50%.
Although tech stocks have been weak, "in general the market has been going sideways" and that's not bad
"given the gains we started the year with," Mr. Pears said.
The euro sagged and money moved toward dollar-denominated assets after German Chancellor Gerhard
Schroeder strongly urged Europe's central bank to step in and cut interest rates to stem the euro's rise.
The strong euro has helped boost sales of U.S. companies by making the prices of U.S. goods less costly
when converted into euros. It has raised the prices of European goods in international markets.
Yesterday, the euro fell 1.5% to $1.2496, its lowest level since the start of this month.
Mr. Greenspan, testifying before the House Budget Committee in Washington, repeated his earlier forecast of
a "vigorous" U.S. economy. He supported President Bush's call for taxes to remain low, and urged that, to
avoid spiraling deficits, government spending be held in check.
"We are vulnerable to bad news" after the sharp stock gains of the past 14 months, said Tim Swanson, chief
investment officer for wealth management at National City in Cleveland.
That could slow the market's progress. "But I am pretty comfortable over the next year that the market will go
up a little -- although probably not a lot."
Sales of existing homes slowed sharply in January compared with December, but January still was the sixth
strongest ever, the National Association of Realtors reported.
Investors were reassured that several companies, including Tiffany and Ingram Micro, a big computer
distributor, reported quarterly earnings above analysts' forecasts.
With the dollar rebounding, gold fell 2% to $395.80, its lowest level in three months.
Crude oil futures rose 3% in New York.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.40%, or 4.58 points, to 1143.67, up 2.9% this year.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 0.46%, or 0.70 point, to 150.91.

Lumber Prices Surge And May Boost Cost Of Homes Further
Commodities Report
Lumber Prices Surge And May Boost Cost Of Homes Further
By Jim Carlton
883 words
26 February 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
LUMBER PRICES ARE going through the roof again.
Prices have spiked the past few weeks amid unusually tight wood supplies and resurging housing demand.
The trend could add to the already torrid run-up in the price of homes in many parts of the country -- but not
enough, analysts say, to damp the nation's white-hot housing market.
Domestic lumber producers are in an enviable spot, analysts say, of having numerous orders to fill because
of a triple whammy that has crimped supplies: Foreign imports have cooled due to the weakened dollar, log
supplies are unusually low because of all the Western forest fires last year, and railroads are operating slower
than normal due to a strike in Canada and other factors.
"If you are a seller of lumber, it is a very, very busy business now," said Mark Donovan, a marketing vice
president for Forest City Trading Group Inc., a commodities trader in Portland, Ore.
The composite price for framing lumber in the U.S. jumped to $384 per thousand board feet as of last Friday,
from $327 in December, an increase of 17%, according to surveys by Random Lengths Publications Inc., a
market-tracking firm in Eugene, Ore. Many traders expect the price to quickly break through the five-year
peak of $386 reached in September, after prices began recovering last year. The composite price is a
weighted average of 15 different lumber products.
The imbalance between materials supply and housing demand is driving up prices even faster for plywood
and engineered wood panels. According to Random Lengths, the composite price of structural panels -- the
weighted average of 11 key products -- has jumped 60% to an all-time high of $571 per thousand square feet
from $356 in December. Like framing lumber, the prices for panels, which are used to make walls and
rooftops, had declined through much of the fall due to a seasonal housing slowdown.
Before that, lumber and panels pricing had shot up, ending a three-year slump in the wood-products industry.
That downturn was caused by, among other things, an excess of wood products brought on by increased
foreign imports drawn by the then-strong dollar. Composite lumber prices peaked at $408 per thousand board
feet in 1999, then fell back to $304 in 2002, according to Random Lengths. The lumber industry was hit so
hard that in the Southeast, for instance, about 50 of the region's 417 sawmills have had to close over the past
five years, according to Sara Baldwin, an editor for Timber Mart-South, a price report at the University of
Georgia.
The turnaround in pricing that began last year is benefiting the mills that were able to survive. "I wouldn't say
they are euphoric yet, but they are certainly relieved by the higher prices," Ms. Baldwin said.
After a holiday lull, industry executives say housing activity resumed in full stride, with higher-than-normal
sales in places such as Phoenix and Las Vegas offsetting most of the snow-related slowdowns in the East.
The price increases could put the squeeze on smaller builders, but they aren't likely to have much effect on
the operations of big builders. That's because many buy lumber and other wood products at volume
discounts, and lock in their prices as much as a year in advance. For instance, officials at Toll Brothers Inc., a
builder based in the Philadelphia suburb of Huntingdon Valley, say they signed a 12-month contract for
lumber in July, when composite lumber prices were at $302. "We all hate paying more, but this won't impact
our earnings," said Robert Toll, Toll's chairman and chief executive officer.
Still, the higher prices could eventually be passed on to consumers if the trend continues for very long.
Officials at Pulte Homes Inc., for example, said they might add $1,000 to $1,500 to the price of their homes;
lumber and paneling comprises about one-fifth the cost of construction. "That's maybe a half percent increase
in our prices, so it's not a gigantic item," said Richard Dugas Jr., president and CEO of Pulte, based in
Bloomfield Hills, Mich.

--- Page 98 ---
Page 98 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But officials at Toll Brothers say their home prices have already gone up so much because of the demand
around the country that the lumber increases can be easily absorbed into the total home cost. Toll's
single-family homes in Pennsylvania, for instance, have shot up more than 50% to an average price of
$589,000 from $383,000 in 1998. Company executives attribute the price increases to the same combination
of low interest rates and limited home supply that has fueled the nationwide housing boom.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: New York Mercantile Exchange futures rose as a bigger-than-expected draw in gasoline
inventories fed traders' fears that supplies would continue to fall. The April contract climbed $1.10 to $35.68 a
barrel.
GOLD: Futures prices fell sharply on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange as
speculative funds and bullion banks sold amid the dollar's advance. April futures dropped $8.70 to $396.10 a
troy ounce.
-- OsterDowJones Commodity News contributed to this article.

pressure doesn't build again."
Ingram Micro jumped $3.49, or 22%, to $19.55. The provider of technology products and services'

margins.
Rambus gained 2.29, or 7.2%, to 33.95 on Nasdaq. The administrative law judge who last week dismissed a
government antitrust suit against the computer-memory chip designer released a 334-page opinion, citing 12
key instances in which the Federal Trade Commission failed to prove its claims.
Intel helped the Nasdaq by rising 45 cents, or 1.5%, to 29.65. The semiconductor maker, which is also a
component of the Dow, outlined its wireless strategy at a conference in Cannes, France.
Dow component DuPont inched up 11 cents to 45.27. The research and technology company confirmed its
outlook for earnings of 65 cents to 75 cents a share in the first quarter and $2 to $2.20 a share in the full year.

search warrant and related grand-jury subpoenas by a federal court in Texas. The company said the
subpoenas sought placement, retention, graduation and attendance figures and other information. Other
education-related stocks fell, with Career Education losing 2.66, or 5.2%, to 48.80 and Corinthian Colleges
losing 1.82, or 3%, to 58.76, both on the Nasdaq.

same-restaurant sales growth at company-owned eateries, previously estimated at 2%, would be 3.5%.
United Rentals fell 3.68, or 18%, to 16.38. The equipment-rental concern's fourth-quarter loss widened
despite revenue growth of about 7%, and the company said it expects 2004 adjusted earnings of $1 to $1.10

markets during the California power crisis in 2000 and 2001. PG&E climbed 27 cents, or 1%, to 27.55 and
Edison International finished the session up 61 cents, or 2.7%, to 22.80.

Inflation Fears Fuel TIPS Popularity --- Treasury Issues Offer Hedge Against Consumer Prices; More Funds Enter the Fray
Fund Track
Inflation Fears Fuel TIPS Popularity --- Treasury Issues Offer Hedge Against Consumer Prices; More
Funds Enter the Fray
By Ian McDonald The Wall Street Journal Online
798 words
23 February 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C17
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Remember inflation?
Even though Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan sent reassuring messages on the subject in recent
testimony before Congress, many mutual-fund investors seem to have inflation on the brain. As a result, a
number of Main Street investors are hedging their bets against future price increases by putting money into
TIPS, or Treasury Inflation Protected Securities.
TIPS are bonds issued by the Treasury Department, meaning their repayment is promised by the federal
government. As with other Treasury bonds, income from TIPS is exempt from state and local taxes, though
taxable at the federal level.
Unlike regular Treasury bonds, TIPS are designed to beat inflation by paying holders a fixed interest rate on
top of any acceleration in inflation as measured by consumer prices. The Treasury adjusts the face value of
TIPS in lockstep with the consumer-price index, ensuring that investors are protected against rising prices (or
that they will get back their original investment if prices were to fall).
Inflation is back on investors' list of worries after a long absence. Many fear that the Fed's steady stream of
interest-rate cuts designed to stimulate the economy during its downturn may work too well, triggering higher
prices.
Though it has been far lower during recent years, 1.9% in 2003 for example, the annual inflation rate in the
U.S. has averaged about 4% during the past 50 years, according to the Labor Department. If your assets
don't keep pace with inflation, you are losing purchasing power -- and getting poorer -- day by day.
"We'll see higher inflation going forward, with the only question being how much," says Dan Shackelford,
who, as manager of the $34.2 million T. Rowe Price Inflation-Protected Bond Fund, stands to attract more
business if more people are worried about inflation.
Mr. Shackelford is hardly alone in advocating TIPS, which the Treasury began issuing in 1997. After the
largest TIPS auction ever last month, the U.S. TIPS market tops $200 billion in bonds outstanding, and the
Treasury is considering adding a TIPS issue with a 20-year maturity to complement its current 10-year TIPS
bonds. Some municipalities and companies are issuing bonds with inflation protection as well.
And TIPS-focused mutual funds are in vogue as well. At the start of this year, there were about 20
TIPS-focused mutual funds. After the sector took in more than $5 billion a year in 2002 and 2003, according
to fund tracker Lipper Inc., at least six other TIPS funds either have launched or have filed plans with
regulators during the past 90 days.
It doesn't hurt that large TIPS funds such as the $8.9 billion Pimco Real Return Fund and the $4.9 billion
Vanguard Inflation-Protected Securities Fund boast returns of more than 10% annually during the past three
years.
To be sure, solid recent gains, a spate of new offerings and healthy inflows in a given fund category are often
a contrarian indicator. But TIPS funds may be a sensible, long-term addition for many investors' portfolios.
During a period of accelerating inflation they should post stock-like gains, for instance, but they also are a
solid diversification tool for a stock-heavy portfolio, since their prices have tended to move in different cycles
than those of stocks.
"They're a good option for investors who are worried about inflation," says Lipper fund analyst Andrew Clark.
With lower potential volatility than other inflation hedges such as gold or commodities, "they're really one of
the few good tools out there for hedging against inflation," he says.

--- Page 104 ---
Page 104 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
TIPS pay the same interest rate semiannually even if their face value rises, providing a higher income stream
as inflation accelerates. So, if inflation steadily quickens, each income payment will be higher than the last.
There are risks to TIPS, of course. Rising interest rates ding bond prices, including those of TIPS, since
bond prices move in the opposite direction of yields. And if inflation is modest, investors would earn more
from regular Treasury bonds than from TIPS.
How do you know when you would be better off with one or the other? Today a 10-year TIPS bond pays
about 1.8% above inflation, compared with about a 4.1% yield for standard 10-year Treasurys. So, if
inflation is more than 2.3% over the life of the bond, TIPS holders earn higher returns than holders of
Treasurys. If inflation is lower, they will earn a bit less.
Funds likely will provide a better route for many investors wanting greater diversification and professional
management, rather than buying TIPS directly.

Cyclicals Slump, and Caution Rules --- Despite Markets' Run-Up, Big Investors Spurn Sector And Buy Defensive Stocks
Going Global
Cyclicals Slump, and Caution Rules --- Despite Markets' Run-Up, Big Investors Spurn Sector And Buy
Defensive Stocks
By Michael R. Sesit
764 words
18 February 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C18
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Paris -- LIKE DRIVING A car, investing is all about understanding when to accelerate and when to apply the
brakes.
With global stock markets' recent run-up, you might think many investors would be putting their foot on the
gas and buying cyclicals, or companies best poised to benefit from an economic upturn.
Not so. During January, the worst-performing industry groups of the Standard &Poor's 500 stock index were
mostly cyclicals -- led by gold mining, metals mining, airlines, construction and autos.
"Cyclicals have become sicklicals," says Michael Belkin, president of Belkin Ltd., a Bainbridge Island, Wash.,
investment-advisory firm that forecasts financial-market trends. Noting how investors last year reaped the
rewards from betting big on high-risk/high-return assets, he says, "The year 2004 theme should be the return
of risk. Last year was reward."
In other words, many big investors think it is time to put on the brakes.
In stock-market parlance, it is known as buying defensive companies -- such as food and beverage concerns,
supermarkets, utilities, health care -- stocks that lag behind the market during rallies but don't fall as much
during downturns.
So far this year, consumer staples are up 2.8%; health care has risen 4.7%; energy is up 3.9%; and
insurance companies are 9.3% higher. That compares with a 4% rise in the S&P 500 and 2.5% for the Dow
Jones Industrial Average. As for cyclicals, materials are down 1.7%, and industrials are up about half the
index at 1.4%. Consumer discretionary stocks -- things like autos, retailers and airlines -- are 0.7% higher.
Technology stocks are up 2%, with semiconductor shares down 1.6%.
The rewards of 2003 were indeed plentiful. After three years of negative results, developed-world stock
markets rallied 23% in local-currency terms, according to Morgan Stanley Capital International. German
stocks rose 33%; Canadian equities were up 25%; and markets in Spain, Hong Kong, Singapore and Sweden
climbed 28% to 33%. Japan ended the year 22% higher, while in the U.S., the Dow rose 25%, the S&P 26%
and the Nasdaq 50%.
To an American investor, who tallies wins and losses in dollars, the sinking greenback was an added bonus.
It transformed a 33% Swedish-market advance into a 61% rally, Japan's 22% gain into one of 35% and
boosted the MSCI World index's increase to 31%.
Meanwhile, MSCI's Emerging Markets Free index rose 52% last year, measured in dollars. Leading the index
were Thailand, Turkey and Brazil -- which, all more than doubled in valuerespectively, soared 134%, 122%
and 103%. As for bonds, developed-country government debt struggled to perform. Not so for
emerging-market government bonds, which returned 27%, according to Lehman Brothers. Ditto U.S. and
euro-area high-yield -- or junk -- bonds, which returned 29% and 30%, respectively.
But that was all, well, last year.
Now "stocks and bonds remain stuck in a state of stasis," while investors await stronger signals about the
global economic recovery and major central banks' intentions concerning interest rates, says David Brown,
chief European economist at Bear Stearns in London.
At UBS, economists acknowledge that economic growth is going gangbusters and that 4.5% world growth in
2004 will be the best since 2000. But they warn of sizable financial imbalances: For one, the U.S. and China,
which account for an inordinately high 55% of global GDP growth, could slow during the second half. And

--- Page 106 ---
Page 106 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
within the U.S., a budget deficit bordering on more than 4% of GDP -- and which is mostly financed by Asian
central banks -- hangs over markets like a sword of Damocles.
Clive Mc Donnell, European strategist at S&P in London, says one of the key issues in 2004 will be managing
risks -- or knowing when to apply the brakes -- whereas in 2003, "it was simply a matter of increasing
exposure to high-beta sectors." (High-beta is professional lingo for stocks that tend to rise -- or fall -- more
than the market as a whole.) He observes that bond investors are demanding higher returns on
telecommunication-company bonds relative to government securities, while in equity markets, telecoms are
underperforming the S&P 350 European stock index by one percentage point.
Mr. Mc Donnell's advice to investors: "Lock in profits and adopt a more-defensive short-term asset-allocation
strategy." Even so, he believes European shares could climb 6% to 8% this year.
