Blue Chips, Bonds Pull Back in Wake of Weak Economic Data
Thursday's Markets
Blue Chips, Bonds Pull Back in Wake of Weak Economic Data
By E.S. Browning
406 words
13 February 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AMID SOME MILDLY disappointing economic news, investors pulled back from stocks a day after the Dow
Jones Industrial Average soared to a 32-month high.
But the declines were modest. The blue-chip average fell 43.63 points, or 0.41%, to 10694.07, still up 2.3%
so far this year.
Treasury bonds, which also rose strongly Wednesday in response to dovish comments on interest rates
from Federal Reserve Chairman Alan Greenspan, also gave back some gains. The dollar, which in contrast
had hit a 41-month low against the yen on Wednesday, rebounded slightly.
Technology shares led the stock declines, and the Nasdaq Composite Index, heavy in tech stocks, fell 0.77%,
or 16.05 points, to 2073.61, still up 3.5% on the year.
"The markets just felt a little bit tired," said Tim Heekin, director of trading at San Francisco brokerage firm
Thomas Weisel Partners. "There was pretty steady selling most of the day."
The main news was a surprise jump in last week's new unemployment claims to the highest level since early
December. January retail sales were a bit below expectations, mainly due to auto sales.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.49%, or 5.65 points, to 1152.11. It still is up 3.6% on the
year, more than the Dow or the Nasdaq, but less than the Russell 2000 small-stock index, which is up more
than 6% this year. The Russell yesterday fell 0.72%, or 4.32 points, to 592.75.
Gold prices rose; oil prices were little changed.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 0.99%, or 1.49 points, to 152.6.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. On the Big Board, where 1.44 billion shares traded, 1,426 stocks rose and 1,827 fell.
Bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 7/32, or $2.1875 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, rose to 4.052%. The 30-year bond was down 19/32 to yield 4.937%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 105.39 yen, up from 105.37, while the euro fell against
the dollar to $1.2805 from $1.2826.

Rational Exuberance
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Rational Exuberance
598 words
13 February 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A12
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The usual media suspects are treating Alan Greenspan's Congressional testimony this week as one more
opportunity to rumble over fiscal policy -- never mind that taxes and spending aren't the Fed Chairman's job.
We see his remarks, instead, as the first salvo in a debate over who'll take the heat when interest rates start
to rise later this year or next.
The folks who want to repeal President Bush's tax cuts are typically broadcasting the Chairman's familiar
distaste for budget deficits. Yawn. Most of them ignored his other comment that "I've always argued the
problem is on the expenditure side," which is to say the inability of the political class to slow the growth of
spending. "I am in favor, as I've indicated in the past, for continuing the tax cuts that are in dispute at this
particular stage," Mr. Greenspan added.
The Fed chief knows that the incentives provided by those tax cuts have helped put the economy in its
current rationally exuberant mood. He said that the pace of growth may reach 5% this year, that individual
and business spending are rising, that productivity is surging, and that while net new job creation has been
slow it too will start to increase soon.
"Last year appears to have marked a transition from an extended period of subpar economic performance to
one of more vigorous expansion," he declared. No wonder liberals are pouncing politically on "deficits" and
the "outsourcing" of service jobs overseas; everything else is going too well. They're confounded by
prosperity they never expected, so they are left predicting disaster just around the next corner.
We thought the real news in Mr. Greenspan's testimony was on monetary policy. He blew away any thought
that the Fed is worried about the dollar or inflation. The obvious message is that the Greenspan Fed isn't
going to raise interest rates before the election if the Chairman can possibly avoid it. Mr. Greenspan seems
to think, and for now the markets agree, that he can keep monetary policy as wide open as it now is through
November without rekindling inflation.
We wish him luck, but those of us who look at commodity markets for real price signals have watched the
dollar fall and gold and commodities rise. Economist Brian Wesbury, of Griffin, Kubik in Chicago, adds that a
4% year-over-year increase in the producer price index "is a clear sign of inflationary pressures."
Sooner or later these will build and force the Fed to tighten. And here's where Mr. Greenspan's "deficit" worry
is relevant. The Fed chief is more or less saying that if he and his fellow governors do have to raise interest
rates smartly next year, don't blame them. The deficit will have made them do it. Mr. Greenspan doesn't have
a reputation as a savvy Beltway player for nothing.
We aren't about to let the Chairman off so easily. If inflation rises faster than expected, and interest rates
rise along with it, the responsibility will rest squarely with the Fed. Congress and the President set fiscal
policy, and their cuts in dividend and income-tax rates have helped lift the economy after a rough patch.
Given even modest spending restraint, the deficit will fall as growth spins off more revenue. The Fed's job, on
the other hand, is to keep a firm hand on the monetary tiller to maintain a stable price level. If that means
raising interest rates in an election year, so be it.

Travel: Trouble in Low-Fare Land --- Will Airlines' Me-Too Perks Fly With the Customers? New Drinks, Old Planes
Travel:
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Travel: Trouble in Low-Fare Land --- Will Airlines' Me-Too Perks Fly With the Customers? New Drinks,
Old Planes
By Paula Szuchman and Susan Carey
1,397 words
13 February 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
W1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
JEFF HAUSMAN was looking forward to his first trip on Song, Delta's discount airline that advertises unusual
touches like in-flight Pilates exercises and attendants outfitted in Kate Spade uniforms. But on board his flight
to Los Angeles, he found the promised seat-back TVs were missing, and the bottled water ran out halfway
there. "It felt like the Partridge Family bus," says the Tampa, Fla., marketing executive.
With the nation's big airlines increasingly trying to mimic the low-cost carriers, travelers and airline experts are
starting to ask whether the new generation of me-too airlines is misjudging what customers really want. Song
advertises fresh-mixed Cosmopolitans, but puts travelers on hand-me-down planes from Delta. United
launched its low-cost carrier yesterday with club-style "trance" music and the playful name of Ted, but also
limited schedules (one flight per day from Denver to Fort Lauderdale, Fla.). And with Delta conducting a
companywide strategy review, Song is already putting its expansion plans on hold. "No one cares about
designer flight-attendant uniforms," says aviation consultant Michael Boyd of the Boyd Group.
It comes at a critical point for the industry, with two major carriers rolling out their low-cost branches, even as
new concerns arise over the low-fare strategy. JetBlue, for instance, has recently seen its operating margins
squeezed. And of course historically, apart from the success of Southwest Airlines, most attempts to
challenge traditional carriers have failed, from People Express in the '80s to Pro Air. Indeed, now that the
economy and travel are improving, many big carriers are mobilizing against the upstarts as they have so
many times in the past, playing to strengths like elite flier perks and more frequent flights. American Airlines,
for example, just added two new daily nonstops between New York's JFK and Phoenix, where it competes
with discounter America West.
And now come signs that many of these carriers may be losing their big advantage -- the low fares
themselves. BACK Aviation Solutions surveyed 15 major routes over three periods during the upcoming
spring-break season, and found that majors undercut the discounters some 55% of the time, based on an
analysis of lowest-available fares. Travelers planning to fly between New York and Fort Lauderdale in
mid-April, for example, could have paid $196 this week on United -- while AirTran was asking $401. "The
majors really don't have a choice," says BACK's director, David Beckerman. "They don't want to lose out on a
paying passenger."
To be sure, the traditional carriers are up against some big obstacles in their efforts to compete, with higher
costs due to pilot contracts. And for their part, low-cost carriers say they continue to offer the cheapest flights
in many cases. While many of the perks they're emphasizing seem offbeat, these airlines say they build
loyalty that will help them fend off big rivals, often following the advice of brand consultants to stand out from
the crowd. For Ted, United hired Pentagram, the New York-based image-maker behind Godiva's gold
chocolate boxes and the Citibank logo (Pentagram consulted baby-name books to help come up with the
moniker). "We're trying to create a personality," says Ted Vice President Sean Donohue. The company's
research, he adds, shows that customers want "a little bit of fun on board."
Still, some fliers say the newcomers are trying to copy some of the more successful low-cost airlines' attitude
on the cheap. While flashy color schemes abound, for instance, so far only Frontier has followed JetBlue by
adding live seat-back TVs on every aircraft; installation alone can cost $500,000 or more per plane, in-flight
entertainment experts estimate. (Song plans to have them on all of its planes by the end of March, it says.)
Ted, meanwhile, shows taped programming on overhead screens.
For Gary Leff, it's no contest. Low-fare carriers rarely match the majors on offerings that matter most to him,
the 29-year-old finance director says, and he won't be tempted when Ted expands flights to his hometown
airport, Washington's Dulles. He'll fly Continental to earn flier miles and get business-class upgrades not only

--- Page 114 ---
Page 114 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
for work trips, but for vacations, too. "I'm not interested in having fun," he says. "I'm interested in getting
where I'm going with the least amount of discomfort."
To be sure, over the past few years, low-cost carriers have been among the travel industry's relative
successes. JetBlue and AirTran have both been profitable. Southwest has emerged as one of the big
winners, with its extreme no-frills approach -- open seating plans and a focus on direct ticket sales and short,
nonstop flights. Southwest alone now controls half of the $12 billion low-cost airline market.
Low-cost carriers got a particular boost following Sept. 11. Major airlines were cutting routes and staff, with
American fighting to avoid bankruptcy court and United filing for Chapter 11 protection. As Southwest and
JetBlue remained profitable and European discounters provided another attractive model, still more low-fare
carriers started doing business. Discount carriers' growth is expected to continue, with J. P. Morgan
predicting they'll control 40% of domestic departures by 2006, up from 32% currently.
And for many fliers, the perks are pretty attractive. Bruce Schobel says he prefers Song for its TVs, and for
the interactive music-trivia games he can play with other passengers. "It was so cool," the 52-year-old actuary
in Princeton, N.J., says about a recent flight to Florida. "I got to see the Fed interest-rate announcement live."
(As for Mr. Hausman's trip, Song says it has increased each flight's water allotment since he flew.)
But strains are beginning to show even on the more successful low-cost carriers, including JetBlue, the
country's No. 2 airline in customer satisfaction, according to market researcher Resource Systems Group.
Though it increased its capacity by nearly half in the past year, it fell on one key measure of airline success;
its planes flew 78% full in January, down from 82% a full year ago. (The majors, by comparison, have seen
load factors increase slightly, though they remain lower than JetBlue's, with United, American and Continental
all flying less than 70% full.)
In the end, for a look at how the low-fare push may play out, consider yesterday's launch of Ted. On an
inaugural flight from Denver to Fort Lauderdale, flight attendants wore United uniforms, with bright orange
"Ted" buttons. Seats were United blue, with a United in-flight magazine in the pocket. The biggest change,
perhaps, was the all-coach cabin -- and the announcement by the co-pilot just after takeoff. "We are exci-TED
that we have depar-TED," he said. "And we are motiva-TED to serve you."
---

Comcast's Bid And Greenspan Energize Stocks --- Blue Chips Climb 123.85 Points As Testimony Points to Low Rates; Bonds Rise and Dollar...
Wednesday's Markets
Comcast's Bid And Greenspan Energize Stocks --- Blue Chips Climb 123.85 Points As Testimony
Points to Low Rates; Bonds Rise and Dollar Slides
By E.S. Browning
858 words
12 February 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
RARELY DO Mickey Mouse and Alan Greenspan work together, but yesterday they combined to send the
Dow Jones Industrial Average up 123.85 points to a new 2 1/2-year high. Treasury bonds also rose sharply,
but the dollar hit a 41-month low against the yen.
The Federal Reserve chief told a congressional committee that the economy is managing a "vigorous"
expansion without obliging him to raise interest rates anytime soon. That news came just after Walt Disney
found itself the object of an unexpected takeover offer, at a premium price, from Comcast.
Together, the two events reinforced the belief that stocks will continue to benefit from a robust economy with
low inflation and low interest rates, which many consider a near-perfect situation for stocks. Stock-based
takeovers are evidence of that kind of environment, fostering hope that companies will continue to feel
stronger and to behave daringly.
All of that pushed the Dow industrials up 1.17% to 10737.70, a gain of 2.7% so far this year. Walt Disney,
which is a component of the Dow industrials, accounted for just over 26 points of the gain -- about a fifth of
the total.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 1.07%, or 12.22 points, to 1157.76, a gain of 4% for the
year and its own 23-month high.
"The market started taking off when Mr. Greenspan started speaking," said Jon Brorson, head of
growth-stock investing at money-management group Neuberger Berman. "He was very optimistic on the
economy but very patient with interest rates."
The 10-year Treasury note rose 21/32, or $6.5625 for every $1,000 invested. Its yield, which falls when the
price rises, dropped to 4.025%, near the closely watched 4% level again. Low yields reinforce economic
strength because many lending rates, including many mortgages, are pegged to them, and they keep
borrowing costs down for both companies and individuals.
The dollar, however, declined on the prospect that interest rates won't rise soon. It hit 105.37 yen, its lowest
level against the Japanese currency in 41 months. The euro, Europe's five-year-old common currency, rose
to $1.2826, just below the record high of $1.2843 hit last month.
Gold rose, as did oil, which continued to react to the news that the Organization of Petroleum Exporting
Countries plans to reduce production quotas in order to keep prices up.
Coca-Cola fell after quarterly unit sales missed the company's expectations. But the day's big corporate news
was the Comcast bid for Disney. Coming on top of the J.P. Morgan Chase plan to acquire Bank One and the
expectation that a rival company will bid for AT&T Wireless, the news boosted hopes that more takeovers
could be on the horizon.
"Comcast-Disney really set the tone for the morning, and then, with the Greenspan testimony, it was off to the
races," said John O'Donoghue, senior stock trader at brokerage firm Credit Suisse First Boston in New York.
The main motor for stocks now is the continuing flow of money into mutual funds, which stock traders can see
in buy orders every day, Mr. O'Donoghue said. But the Comcast bid "does give you an indication that there is
confidence within the corporate world that there are strategic alliances or acquisitions to be done there, even
though people feel that stock valuations are quite high."
On top of that, he noted, many short sellers -- investors who borrow stock and sell it in hopes of buying it
back cheaper after a decline -- had been betting that Disney would decline. The Comcast bid forced the short

--- Page 119 ---
Page 119 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
sellers to unwind their bets by hastily buying back the stock they had sold, and that had the effect of pushing
Disney still higher.
Many on Wall Street still worry that stocks have come too far, too fast, but the Greenspan testimony
convinced many people that there are few problems lurking in the economy or in interest rates that are
poised to knock stocks down right away.
"Last year appears to have marked a transition from an extended period of subpar economic performance to
one of more vigorous expansion," Mr. Greenspan said in testimony to the House Financial Services
Committee. "Looking forward, the odds of sustained robust growth are good, although, as always, risks
remain."
One of the risks he mentioned was the increase in federal budget deficits, but that concern didn't seem to
affect stocks.
"Bottom line: In his congressional testimony, the Fed chairman continued to signal that `with inflation very
low and substantial slack in the economy, the Fed can be patient in removing its current policy
accommodation' " -- meaning it can keep interest rates low, noted economist Maury Harris at brokerge firm
UBS, in a note to clients.
Investors also looked past news that mortgage applications fell again last week, a sign that consumer
spending may be slowing.

Copper Surges on Chain Reaction From Fed Chairman's Testimony
Commodities Report
Copper Surges on Chain Reaction From Fed Chairman's Testimony
By Allen Sykora
291 words
12 February 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A chain reaction sent high-grade copper futures to their strongest levels since the summer of 1997.
"The real tempest in the teapot is [Fed Chairman] Alan Greenspan's testimony, which is basically confirming
expectations that interest rates are going to stay low," said Tim Evans, analyst with research firm IFR
Pegasus. "That is causing a sharp decline in the U.S. dollar, which is in turn boosting all of the metals -- gold
and silver as well as copper."
March copper futures surged 3.80 cents to $1.2160 a pound on the Comex division of the New York
Mercantile Exchange. The contract hit a life-of-contract high of $1.2180.
The euro was around $1.2800 as copper was closing, up from $1.2696 in New York late Tuesday. A weaker
U.S. currency makes copper cheaper overseas in foreign currencies and thus can help stimulate demand.
As March copper rose, purchase orders placed in advance -- known as buy stops -- were triggered at $1.20,
said an analyst who frequently visits the trading floor. He also reported that buy stops were triggered in
London Metal Exchange trading as three-month copper there moved through $2,600 a metric ton.
In other commodity markets:
CATTLE: Chicago Merc live-cattle futures rose by their limit after aggressive early buying by commercial
accounts indicated beef packers badly needed supplies, according to traders connected to feedlot accounts.
February futures rose 1.50 cents to 75.10 cents a pound.
ORANGE JUICE: Futures fell on the New York Cotton Exchange after reaching a 27-year low as speculators
sold. March futures dropped 1.05 cents to 60.05 cents a pound.

Mouse Trap: Disney, Struggling to Regain Glory, Gets $48.7 Billion Bid From Comcast --- Already on Defense, Eisner Faces Biggest Challenge...
Mouse Trap: Disney, Struggling to Regain Glory, Gets $48.7 Billion Bid From Comcast --- Already on
Defense, Eisner Faces Biggest Challenge Of Nearly 20-Year Reign --- Cable Giant's Huge Ambitions
By Bruce Orwall and Peter Grant
2,946 words
12 February 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
For the past few weeks, Michael Eisner has been bashed like a Mickey Mouse pinata by critics who
questioned his leadership of Walt Disney Co.
Now the question is: Did Roy E. Disney, Steve Jobs and Mr. Eisner's other antagonists soften up Disney for
Comcast Corp. to crack open and grab the candy?
Comcast, the nation's largest cable company in terms of both subscribers and revenue, yesterday launched
an unsolicited $48.7 billion stock offer to acquire Disney, the entertainment giant that is perhaps Hollywood's
best-known brand. Coming close on the heels of Comcast's $51 billion acquisition of AT&T Broadband in
2002, the offer reflects the company's hunger to own some of the movie and television programming that
flows through its cable-television pipes to about 21 million households.
The timing of the offer seems designed to exploit one of the weakest moments in the career of Mr. Eisner,
Disney's chairman and chief executive of nearly 20 years. Mr. Eisner is already fending off a campaign to
oust him led by two dissident ex-directors. The pair, Roy E. Disney and Stanley Gold, say his leadership has
drained the life from a once-great company. The company's stock is trading where it was six years ago; its
earnings, after several years of declines, rebounded last year but remain below 1999 levels.
Mr. Disney's efforts have in turn emboldened other critics -- such as Pixar Animation Studios Chief Executive
Steve Jobs, who recently terminated a key negotiation with Disney -- to launch their own broadsides.
Now Comcast, controlled by Philadelphia's Roberts family, is trying to move in for the kill shot. Comcast
President and Chief Executive Brian Roberts made public a letter to Disney saying that Mr. Eisner earlier this
week rebuffed his suggestion that their companies begin talks to combine. Disney's board met and issued a
statement saying that it "has received and will carefully evaluate" the proposal.
The deal initially offered a 7% premium for Disney shares, plus the assumption of Disney's $11.9 billion in
debt. But after Disney's shares jumped $3.52, or nearly 15%, to $27.60, and Comcast's class A shares fell
$2.70, or nearly 8%, to close at $31.23, the offer price was $48.7 billion, which is below Disney's current
market value of $56.5 billion. That means investors are betting that another bidder will emerge or that
Comcast will sweeten its offer.
If the Comcast deal succeeds, it would create one of the world's top media and entertainment companies with
$45 billion in annual revenue, a market value of $125 billion and 179,000 employees. Disney brings to the
table its famous theme parks, its movie studio, its ABC network and ESPN sports network. Comcast, with the
vast reach of its cable system can provide service to up to 40 million homes; it also owns stakes in everything
from E! Entertainment Television to the Philadelphia 76ers.
But the bid calls into question Mr. Eisner's ability to survive yet another storm without a strong bench of allies
to rely on. Mr. Eisner's direct, often-confrontational style has made him one of the least-loved moguls in
Hollywood. After taking the reins at Disney in 1984, he was one of the superstars of the corporate world for
the next decade, as Disney's theme parks drew big crowds and its animated films became beloved, profitable
franchises. Yet he has been increasingly under siege in recent years as the company's performance has
tailed off. Key executives such as former studio head Jeffrey Katzenberg departed. Mr. Eisner made missteps
such as handing the company's presidency to agent Michael Ovitz, who only lasted 14 months in the job.
The first suggestion that Comcast might be planning something, according to a person familiar with the
matter, came when Mr. Roberts recently began trying to contact Mr. Eisner through an intermediary -- George
Mitchell, the former U.S. senator who is Disney's presiding director. Mr. Roberts reached Mr. Eisner directly
on Monday, but their talk was very brief with Mr. Roberts asking to discuss a merger and Mr. Eisner declining.

--- Page 122 ---
Page 122 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Disney executives on Tuesday discussed how they would respond if Comcast came back with something
more specific.
But Disney was clearly in scramble mode yesterday, responding to the public unveiling of the Comcast offer
just hours before it was to begin a three-day analysts meeting at its Walt Disney World Resort here in
Orlando.
Comcast's initial offer might have been laughed off in the days when Mr. Eisner was seen as a stronger
leader. And even now people close to Disney's board say it isn't likely to leap at yesterday's Comcast
proposal. But the Disney board is on a crusade to demonstrate its independence from Mr. Eisner, and these
people say that richer offers -- from Comcast or anyone else -- will be taken seriously, regardless of Mr.
Eisner's feelings. That means the legendary Disney empire is in play.
At the front of the list of prospective rival bidders are two acquisitive media moguls: John Malone and Barry
Diller. Both have the reputation and financial heft to be taken seriously as bidders. Mr. Diller, who is close to
Mr. Eisner, is chairman and CEO of InterActive Corp., which owns Expedia, Ticketmaster and the Home
Shopping Network. Mr. Malone is chairman of Liberty Media Corp., which holds interests in entertainment
networks including the Discovery Channel and QVC. Both declined to comment.
Other media companies are less likely to emerge with rival bids. Rupert Murdoch said yesterday his News
Corp. wouldn't make a bid. Viacom Inc., which owns the CBS television network, would have regulatory
problems buying Disney, owner of ABC. Time Warner Inc. is still mired in financial and accounting problems
from its ill-fated merger with America Online Inc.
Disney plans to show its resolve in part by continuing with the conference it is conducting here, meant to
showcase the company's much-improved financial performance in the last year. The company released its
fiscal first-quarter earnings, which were especially strong, earlier in the day than expected yesterday. Disney
believes that any potential deal could face tough regulatory scrutiny from the Federal Communications
Commission.
Comcast, which is also one of the country's leading broadband providers, believes it can increase profits of
the combined companies partly by coming up with new ways to put Disney's content on TV and the Internet.
"The bottom line is that we think it will accelerate the digital future," said Mr. Roberts, the Comcast chief
executive, at a press conference yesterday in Manhattan.
The offer once again presses the issue of whether so-called content creators such as Disney should be
owned by the same companies that distribute news and entertainment to the public through cable systems,
satellite services and broadcast networks. Numerous other such combinations have failed -- notably the
disastrous marriage of America Online and Time Warner. But Comcast is ravenous for such a deal. Having
grown from a small family business in Tupelo, Miss., into one of the media world's most feared players,
Comcast believes it needs such a deal to level the playing field against rivals such as Mr. Murdoch's News
Corp., which owns satellite systems all over the world that are fed by the company's Fox television networks
and movie studio.
Disney, however, has avoided further forays into distribution after its mid-1990s purchase of Capital Cities
ABC Inc., which gave it the successful ESPN cable networks but also saddled it with the struggling ABC
network. As he opened the analysts meeting, Mr. Eisner reiterated his view that Disney's content will prove
more valuable than the commodity of distribution. "We love being a content company," he said. "We've been
a content company for many years. We do think that content is, if not king, [then] king and queen."
For consumers, a marriage of Disney and Comcast would likely mean new cable networks and
entertainment-oriented Web sites targeted at people with high-speed Internet hookups. Comcast executives,
for example, suggested that they might try to sell a package of Disney Web sites to broadband users in the
same way that HBO is now marketed as a premium cable channel.
Although Comcast has coveted Disney for years, the takeover plan moved to the front burner in the first week
of January when Comcast met with its longtime advisers at a hotel near the Philadelphia airport. The meeting
ostensibly was to determine the company's strategic direction, but "everyone knew what we were really
meeting about," said a person who attended the meeting -- and that was Disney.
Former AT&T Corp. Chairman and Chief Executive C. Michael Armstrong has served as chairman of the
board of Comcast since the AT&T Broadband merger. People close to Comcast board members say Mr.
Armstrong in recent weeks has been among those board members supportive of the charge to buy Disney.
The plan was officially hatched and advisers spent the next month putting it together. By last week, most of
the plans were complete and Mr. Roberts called Mr. Eisner on Monday.

--- Page 123 ---
Page 123 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The call, according to two people familiar with the matter, lasted less than five minutes. Mr. Eisner told Mr.
Roberts that he liked Disney's strategy "just fine" and wanted to keep it "as is," according to people briefed on
the call.
When it became clear that Mr. Eisner wasn't interested, the Comcast team prepared a "bear hug" letter -- a
traditional takeover strategy in which an unsolicited suitor publicly makes a bid.
In the foreground of the Comcast move has been its president, Stephen Burke -- whose father was a top
Disney executive, and who himself worked as a senior executive at Disney for 12 years and was considered
by some a possible heir to Mr. Eisner. In presentations yesterday, Mr. Burke criticized the performance of a
number of Disney divisions and predicted that Comcast would be able to increase the company's cash flow
by $800 million to $1.3 billion within three years.
(MORE)
For example, Mr. Burke noted that Disney's ABC Network "is a weak No. 4" compared with the other major
broadcast networks. He also criticized Disney's animation division and said if Comcast succeeds in its
takeover, it would once again become "absolutely central to the heart" of Disney, he said.
The offer spotlights the shifting fortunes of two media players: Comcast's ascent from humble roots to
become a media powerhouse and Disney's slide from its position as one of Hollywood's most lucrative
goldmines.
Comcast got its start in 1963 when Ralph Roberts and two partners bought a cable system in Tupelo, Miss.
Since then the company has grown steadily, becoming the country's largest cable operator in 2002 when it
acquired the cable division of AT&T Corp.
During the company's rise, the Robertses developed a reputation for strong management, tough deal making
and the habit of pouncing on companies when they were vulnerable. For example, Comcast launched its
hostile bid for AT&T Broadband in 2001 when the unit was suffering from high debt and weak margins, and
AT&T was planning to spin it off as a separate company.
The timing of Comcast's bid for Disney isn't optimal for the cable operator, coming less than 18 months after
its buyout of AT&T Broadband. But people close to Comcast say the timing was partly motivated by the
turmoil within the Disney boardroom.
Comcast also has succeeded in merging its cable systems with those owned by AT&T Broadband faster than
expected -- giving some analysts hope that it could also handle the more difficult task of absorbing Disney. At
the time of the merger, AT&T Broadband's operating margins were just under 20%, one of the lowest in the
cable industry. Yesterday, Comcast released its earnings for the fourth quarter of 2003, showing margins of
38%, well on their way to 40%, the company's goal for the end of the year. Revenue and subscribers also
were up, while the company's debt was down to close to $23 billion, or about $7 billion less than when the
merger took place. Most important, the company projected that this year it will have $2 billion in so-called free
cash flow, earnings before taxes, amortization and depreciation.
With its debt down and cash reserves growing, Comcast late last year began thinking about what to do next.
On the top of the list was buying new content to put out on its cable network that already has more than 21
million subscribers and its high-speed data network that now has 5.3 million subscribers.
Last summer, Comcast considered buying the U.S. entertainment assets of France's Vivendi Universal but
dropped out of the bidding because it felt the price was too high. With Vivendi no longer an option, Comcast
set its sights on Disney. The company's cable networks, particularly ESPN, are especially attractive to the
cable operator which, along with the rest of the industry, has seen its programming costs skyrocket in recent
years. Most cable operators had to pay ESPN a 20% rate increase last year. Comcast executives also would
love to have Disney's extensive library of programming to sell on its cable and broadband pipes.
With a strong survival instinct and gift for corporate gamesmanship, Mr. Eisner has until now deflected every
challenge. But his skill as a corporate warrior is today being put to its stiffest test. In recent weeks, he has
been consumed with fighting the insurrection that began when Messrs. Disney and Gold resigned from the
company's board after Mr. Disney was notified that a mandatory retirement policy would force him off the
board. Stunned that he had been pushed out of the company bearing his family name, Mr. Disney launched a
public campaign seeking Mr. Eisner's ouster.
The movement to replace Mr. Eisner comes at an odd time, given that Disney's financial performance steadily
improved last year after several years of declining earnings. The company is projecting 30% earnings growth
this year, barring unforeseen circumstances. Its movie studio had one of its biggest years ever year in 2003
with hits such as "Pirates of the Caribbean: The Curse of the Black Pearl."

--- Page 124 ---
Page 124 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But Disney's theme parks are still struggling to regain the success they enjoyed before the Sept. 11, 2001,
terrorist attacks. Disney's ABC network has made improvements, but not the full turnaround that Mr. Eisner
and Disney President Robert Iger have promised.
It's not yet clear whether Messrs. Disney and Gold have generated much support among institutional
investors that hold the company's stock. Yesterday, however, the closely watched proxy advisory firm
Institutional Shareholder Services recommended that its members follow Mr. Disney's lead and withhold
support for Mr. Eisner at the Disney annual meeting next month.
In a statement, Disney called the ISS recommendations "inexplicable and unjustified" since Mr. Eisner "led
the very changes that resulted in a board dominated by independent directors."
Regardless of how much support they ultimately attract, though, Messrs. Disney and Gold have created an
unstable environment that affords an opportunity for others to take shots at Mr. Eisner. Mr. Jobs, for example,
used the moment to abruptly end negotiations to extend Pixar's lucrative partnership with Disney, under
which Disney co-finances and distributes Pixar's popular computer-animated films. That deal has generated
hundreds of millions of dollars in profits for Disney at a time when its own animated movies were faltering.
Even a few executives within Disney have felt comfortable carping about their boss. Harvey Weinstein,
co-chairman of the company's Miramax Films unit, was recently quoted in Time magazine complaining that,
while Mr. Eisner received a bonus for his work last year, he didn't. However, Mr. Weinstein and his brother
Bob, also the unit's co-chairman, were paid a collective bonus of about $18 million last year, according to
Disney. A Miramax spokesman says Mr. Weinstein was actually referring to outstanding compensation he is
owed from 2002.
Messrs. Disney and Gold amplified their criticisms yesterday. In a forum with the proxy advisory firm Glass,
Lewis &Co., Mr. Gold launched bitter attacks against several Disney board members, at one point asserting
that Mr. Mitchell "talks out of both sides of his mouth." He tore into Disney's Mr. Iger, also on the board,
claiming that "he has failed" in his efforts to revive ABC, and adding: "There's nothing more outrageous than
giving Bob Iger a bonus."
But Mr. Disney saved his most searing remarks for Mr. Eisner, whom he helped install as chairman back in
1984, when corporate raiders were circling the company during a fallow period.
Citing "The Wizard of Oz," Mr. Disney said: "Remember when the witch was killed and all the little winged
monkeys stood up and started singing `The witch is dead'? That's what happened in '84, and that's what will
happen this time."

The Willy-Nilly Dollar
The Willy-Nilly Dollar
By David Malpass
1,291 words
11 February 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A18
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Since the 1970s, currency values have been swinging wildly, with immense impact on economies and lives.
Just since 1999, the dollar has moved from $1.17 per euro to $0.83 in 2001, and to $1.27 today, matching
moves in the dollar's value in terms of gold and commodities. The impact of this volatility on U.S. farmers and
manufacturers, developing countries, tourism, and investment flows has been immense and disruptive.
At last weekend's G7 meeting, the powerful group of finance ministers and central bankers accepted this,
asserting that exchange rates should be flexible and reflect economic fundamentals. The U.S. hopes the G7
language will form a lasting consensus and be viewed as a set of long-term currency principles. But there's a
hitch: Rather than "reflecting" fundamentals, the exchange rate is itself a key fundamental. It affects capital
flows, jobs, inflation, and interest rates. Currency changes harm the relationship between debtors and
creditors and dominate the profitability of many companies.
Without considering the exchange rate, there is not even a ballpark economic definition of a country's
fundamentals -- one can't tell whether monetary policy is tight or loose, or inflation likely to rise or fall. If the
idea is that strong economic fundamentals cause a strong currency -- as the Clinton administration argued --
it is unclear why the Bush administration seems so comfortable with the two-year weakness in the dollar. It
surely doesn't agree with the dollar-bears' claim that U.S. fundamentals have deteriorated substantially due to
the tax cuts and fiscal deficits.
My view is the opposite. U.S. fundamentals are much better now than in 2000, in large part due to the change
in the value of the dollar and the improved tax structure. The fundamentals are reflected in important new
records in GDP, total employment according to the household survey, corporate profits, productivity and
disposable personal income. The U.S. potential growth rate in coming years is one of the best on record, and
hard to explain if the current value of the dollar is thought to "reflect" economic fundamentals.
The irony in the fascination with the G7's exchange-rate language is that the causal relationship from
economic fundamentals to currencies works so poorly. The dollar reached its peak value in 2001 after the
recession started. Dollar strength wasn't the sole cause -- lax regulation plus high tax rates, interest rates,
and oil prices contributed -- but the dollar certainly didn't reflect U.S. fundamentals.
Japan's experience also supports this "reverse-fundamentals" view. The yen strengthened in 1990-1995 as
Japan created a multi-year stagnation, record unemployment, and a decade-long stock-market downturn. Not
only was the yen not reflecting fundamentals, it was the key negative fundamental.
At the weekend's G7 meeting, participants added the idea that excess volatility or disorder in exchange rates
was undesirable for economic growth. Good point, except the U.S. put very wide boundaries on the
definitions of "excess" and "disorder." It signaled that the euro's movement against the dollar -- up 53% since
2001 -- has not qualified as undesirable, nor did one-month moves of over 5% (September and December)
constitute excessive volatility. In the same vein, the U.S. has set very wide boundaries in defining its "strong
dollar" policy, asserting that it is still in place even after the dollar has lost 35% of its value.
I don't think the G7's formulation for currency policy -- flexible, reflecting fundamentals -- is good for global
growth. As a currency moves, the demand for it moves in the same direction causing momentum to take over.
This momentum nature of currency movements leads to wide, anti-growth swings in exchange rates. A
pro-growth stance would be for the G7 to prefer less currency instability in order to create better economic
fundamentals.
The U.S. has shown it can withstand the damage from wide swings in the value of the dollar. The strong
dollar reached an extreme in 2000 and 2001 causing crises world-wide, a perfect storm, yet the U.S.
recession was milder than normal and unemployment peaked at 6.3%. The U.S. economy is big and flexible,
with multinational corporations well positioned for currency chaos.

--- Page 129 ---
Page 129 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The brunt of the damage from currency volatility falls abroad. Latin America suffered whiplash when the dollar
levitated, then crashed, with serious aftershocks still showing up almost daily. Though I'm optimistic about
Japan, its years of deflationary yen strength constitute a massive headwind to its recovery.
A better system would be one in which governments and their central banks preferred currency stability over
instability and used exchange rates as a useful signal for monetary policy. If the currency is strong and
strengthening, it means that monetary policy should be loosened to meet rising demand, and vice versa if
the currency is weakening. Currencies would still be flexible and would trade actively. Currency interventions
would be unnecessary and unwelcome. For the U.S., the result would be a "strong and stable" dollar and
relatively steady interest rates rather than the present willy-nilly dollar and wide swings in interest rates.
Europe, Japan and many other countries are yearning for less instability in the dollar, and would grow faster if
it occurred.
---
As one of the least regulated markets, foreign-exchange trading has moved to new peaks of profitability on
the uncertainty about future exchange rates. George Soros is famous for making a royal fortune breaking the
pound in 1992. But even bigger bucks were made by those who broke the euro in 1999 and 2000 and then
broke the dollar in 2002 and 2003. For foreign-exchange markets, the game never stops, there's nothing to
go bankrupt, and no pretense of fundamental value.
The financial stakes are also high for governments. Japan's Ministry of Finance lost nearly $40 billion after
the September G7 meeting in Dubai when the dollar weakened precipitously and the value of Japan's official
dollar holdings shrank. The MOF has been working overtime to make a small fraction of that back through the
risky carry trade, lengthening the maturity and raising the yield on its U.S. bond holdings despite the grim
outlook for U.S. bonds.
China and its neighbors are enjoying a boom in investment, in large part due to good fundamentals provided
by currency stability. Investor logic is that the G7 wants Asia's currencies to strengthen, so investment in Asia
might also gain even more than its fundamentals suggest. Asia will likely reap tens of billions in extra
investments if a currency-appreciation cycle builds, a parallel to Japan in the 1980s.
This raises another major currency-related problem for the U.S. If currencies are to reflect economic
fundamentals, neighboring Latin America will see itself decapitalized while Asia booms. Rather than Mexico
joining China in currency stability and fast growth, recent years have seen investment dollars redirected from
Mexico to China in search of China's yuan stability and future strength. Rather than reflecting fundamentals,
currencies are causing them.
On this page in June 2002, I explained my "Bullish on America" views after years of concern. I'm still bullish
on America, but my latest concern is that we have swapped an overly strong dollar for an overly weak dollar,
neglecting its value in both directions. The U.S. can survive these currency swings, but the costs are high and
unnecessary. The opportunity to improve the currency system is palpable. Around the world, countries yearn
for less instability, leaving the U.S. government and currency traders the principal advocates of the status
quo.
---
Mr. Malpass is chief global economist at Bear Stearns.

Credit Where It's Not Due
Credit Where It's Not Due
By Howard Husock
1,128 words
10 February 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
When the Senate Banking Committee holds its hearings today on regulation of Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac,
members should keep foremost in mind the determination by a December Federal Reserve study that the
secondary mortgage giants provide little or no benefit to homebuyers, while costing taxpayers billions.
This must now serve as the first argument for their eventual privatization. But it is not the only argument.
Public support has not only enriched stockholders at taxpayer expense but has provided the rationale for a
system of regulatory mandates which seek to use the vast mortgage capital pool as an instrument of unwise
social policy. In the name of expanding home-ownership and assisting low-income and inner city households,
these mandates risk undermining the crucial habits of thrift and frugality so important to upward mobility --
and replacing them with the fools' gold of easy credit.
Here's how the system works. In exchange for their status as "government-sponsored enterprises" -- and the
credit market advantages which come from the perception that the government will not allow them to fail --
Fannie and Freddie are subject to so-called "affordable housing goals." These mortgage purchase quotas,
set by the Department of Housing and Urban Development (HUD), require that no less than 50% of the
mortgages purchased by Fannie and Freddie be for low and moderate-income households, that 20% be
specifically for those of low income, and that 31% come from "geographically targeted underserved areas."
These figures represent substantial increases adopted in the waning days of the Clinton administration.
What could be wrong with what seem to be laudable goals? A great deal. They are based in a big lie which a
national coalition of anti-banking groups has circulated for years: that the private mortgage markets would,
left to their own devices, deny credit to those of modest means in less than affluent neighborhoods -- a
charge known as red-lining when it first surfaced in the 1970s. The best that can be said for this is that there
was a time, before the bank deregulation of the early '80s, when banks had little incentive to serve riskier
markets and often did not. But that is ancient history in the mortgage industry -- which now uses
computerized credit scoring, flexible interest rates and mortgage-backed securities to serve almost anyone.
The advent of sub-prime -- read higher-interest -- lending has brought credit to higher-risk households once
outside the mainstream financial system. They have helped push up the American rate of home-ownership to
its record high of 68.4%. Indeed, between 1993 and 2001, 1.4 million Americans bought homes with
sub-prime loans.
But the bank-haters -- including activist groups like ACORN, the Greenlining Institute, and National
Community Reinvestment Coalition, whose collective raison d'etre lies in the supposed market failure of the
mortgage lending industry -- don't see all this as an advance at all. With HUD oversight, they pressure Fannie
and Freddie not to purchase sub-prime loans, which are denounced as "predatory." They have pushed a
wave of "anti-predatory" lending legislation through state legislatures, based in such vague criteria that rating
agencies, fearful of potential liability, have said they can't rate the risk of mortgage bonds which include
sub-prime loans. Were it not for the courageous decision by Comptroller of the Currency John Hawke to
exempt nationally chartered banks from these state laws, sub-prime mortgage lending might well dry up. Mr.
Hawke was undeterred by threats of lawsuits by Eliot Spitzer, and his Connecticut counterpart Richard
Blumenthal.
The agenda here is a demand for credit on easy terms for areas and households which are, in the liberal
imagination, uniformly victims of an unjust economic system -- rather than being seen as individuals, some of
whom deserve credit on favorable terms, some of whom don't. With HUD pressure to meet the affordable
housing goals, and high-interest loans castigated as predatory, both the banks -- which want to sell
mortgages to Fannie and Freddie -- and the secondary giants themselves, which want to retain government
support, have reason to let easy credit flow, willy-nilly, to target populations.
Therein lies the real danger of the affordable housing goals. They provide a powerful incentive to say yes to
mortgage applications to which it might be better for lenders to say no. It is, after all, better to say no when a
household has not saved a sufficient down payment or lacks a reliable income stream. "No," under such

--- Page 132 ---
Page 132 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
circumstances -- and said with an explanation -- is a way of saying, If you improve your creditworthiness, you
can get a mortgage. Saying no is also a protection for those who already own homes in a neighborhood.
Foreclosures -- the bitter fruit of easy credit -- are bad news for neighboring homeowners who are making
their mortgage payments and hoping the value of their home will increase. This is the virtuous circle that
arises from traditional credit criteria, and is threatened by "affordable housing goals."
That's been the story with another easy-credit mortgage program, that of the Federal Housing Administration,
notable for their "flexible qualifying guidelines" designed to help low-income households. Down payments are
3% or less, with loans insured by the federal government. The delinquency rates for FHA loans runs over
12%, more than four times the 2.93% rate for prime rate loans. And problematic FHA loans have been shown
to be concentrated in lower-income urban neighborhoods.
The Fed has found that higher-than-conventional foreclosure rates also typify so-called Community
Reinvestment Act (CRA) "special lending programs" -- a geographically targeted program which banks adopt
to fulfill the Act's lending mandates. Like the Fannie and Freddie affordable housing goals, the CRA is built on
the false premise of an anti-poor conspiracy by the financial industry; and it allows non-profit mortgage
lenders like the Neighborhood Assistance Corporation of America to gain the right to administer huge pools of
mortgage money on behalf of fearful banks.
It's true that the delinquency rates for sub-prime mortgages are also high (11+%); but at least these loans
hold the prospect of encouraging households to learn from their mistakes and qualify for better credit rates
next time. The easy credit of the Fannie and Freddie affordable housing goals breaks the link between
personal habits and creditworthiness. Public subsidy for Fannie and Freddie merely gives those who would
pervert mortgage markets the chance to make credit seem like an entitlement.
---
Mr. Husock, a contributing editor to City Journal, is the author of "America's Trillion-Dollar Housing Mistake"
(Ivan Dee, 2003).
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Mortgage Lending Bias Can Create `Undeserving' " -- WSJ Feb. 23,
2004)

Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / What's Hot...and Not: Overheated Sectors Cool Off Amid
Rate Chatter --- It Was a Negative Week For Commodities and Tech; Mexico Continues Rebound
By Craig Karmin
692 words
9 February 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
GLOBAL INVESTORS expressed renewed concern over the strength of the U.S. economic rebound last
week, despite a broad stock-market rally Friday.
The Federal Reserve's hints late last month that it soon may raise short-term interest rates again
reverberated in financial markets, though the fears of an imminent rate increase subsided after a report
showed U.S. labor-market growth remained modest. Still, the rate chatter early in the week helped bring
some of last year's biggest winners back to Earth while drawing fresh attention to some of the former
laggards.
Technology stocks were among the bigger losers. The Dow Jones Japan Technology Index fell 4.7%, while
the Nasdaq Composite Index edged down 0.1%. Nasdaq stocks had been doing even worse. But the U.S.
employment report on Friday, showing that nonfarm payrolls expanded by only 112,000 during January,
muted some fears of a near-term interest-rate increase and helped tech stocks regain some lost ground.
Even so, many global fund managers say that the securities that have benefited from low interest rates and
higher investor tolerance for risk aren't about to reignite for another sustained run.
"You're seeing many of the hot markets cool down," says Peter Marber, president of Trust Co. of the Atlantic,
a money-management firm in Greenwich, Conn. "Investors are increasingly aware of the froth."
Commodities, another big winner from last year, resumed their recent retreat. Reuters CRB
Commodity/Futures Index edged down 0.6%, though gold edged up 0.4%. Japanese assets were hit
particularly hard, with the Japan Basic Resources Index and Basic Materials Index among the bigger
decliners.
As happened during the period of economic stagnation two years ago, bonds and some of the more
conservative stocks saw gains. The Dow Jones Europe Utilities Index advanced 3.9%, while Europe's
Noncyclical Goods &Services rose 3.6%. Global bonds, in particular those in Europe, also generally
outperformed more speculative assets. Riskier U.S. high-yield and emerging-market bonds, meanwhile, fell
back.
Donald Straszheim, global economist and principal at the economic consulting firm Straszheim Global
Advisors, considers recent trading a sign that last year's buoyant mood has passed as investors take a more
sobering look at the sustainability of a U.S. economic rebound.
"We're going to see a lot more caution and skepticism over the next few quarters," he predicts. "It's a step
back toward reality."
The dollar flirted with a new low against the euro on Friday, but for most of the week had leveled off from its
free-fall against a basket of currencies: the J.P. Morgan U.S. Dollar Index was up 0.1% over the first four
days last week. Analysts said the prospect of higher U.S. interest rates would be bullish for the dollar,
especially if, as many say, the European Central Bank is leaning toward a rate cut by midyear.
Not all of the 2003 winners had setbacks last week. The Hang Seng China Enterprises Index of mainland
shares that trade in Hong Kong rose 3.7%, rebounding after a January selloff amid talk of a Chinese
stock-market bubble and fears about bird flu spreading in China and other parts of Asia.
And the U.S. payroll report and hopes that tighter U.S. monetary policy may be stalled helped Brazilian
stocks shake off a tough week. After falling 3.5% over the first four days, the Bovespa Index advanced 4.2%
on Friday.

Bilbao Vizcaya Argentaria's unexpected tender offer for the remaining 41% of Grupo Financiero BBVA
Bancomer that it already doesn't own. The news about Mexico's largest bank sparked talk of further
consolidation in the Mexican banking industry.

J
C3
English

NEW YORK -- Struck down by concerns about higher interest rates, small-capitalization stocks continued to
slump, underperforming the broad market for a second straight session.
The Russell 2000 Index fell 4.05, or 0.69%, to 579.86, and the Standard &Poor's SmallCap 600 Index shed
1.34, or 0.48%, to 277.50.
Communications-technology stocks, which have been among the month's strongest performers, didn't help.
Large-cap Foundry Networks said rivals are seeking market share by introducing products and lowering
prices, and the news led the sector lower. Powerwave Technologies fell 67 cents, or 6.9%, to $9.03,
Mindspeed Technologies slid 56 cents, or 5.7%, to 9.30, and Packeteer shed 44 cents, or 2.3%, to 18.96.
Sonicwall jumped 38 cents, or 4.5%, to 8.84, as the provider of computer-network-security products sharply
narrowed its fourth-quarter net loss to two cents a share from $1.33 a share the year before.
Gold sank on a strong U.S. dollar, and stocks related to the precious metal also gave a poor showing. Coeur
D'Alene Mines fell 42 cents, or 7.1%, to 5.51, and Meridian Gold sank 60 cents, or 4.7%, to 12.11, both on
the New York Stock Exchange. Randgold &Exploration fell 68 cents, or 5%, to 13.02.
Small stocks overall continued to slide on concerns that the Federal Reserve may raise interest rates sooner
than expected. Still, some analysts said the stock market is using the Fed's comments Wednesday as an
excuse to pause after a run-up in stock prices.
"The Fed's change of language is more of an absence of a positive than a negative," said James Furey, head
of small-cap strategy at Lehman. "Were they to move to a tightening bias, that would be a negative, but they
haven't."
Strong earnings from diversified manufacturer Leggett &Platt appeared to kindle interest in some furniture
makers. Interface, a provider of fabrics and floor coverings, jumped 45 cents, or 6%, to 8.
Restaurant stocks edged up after Ryan's Family Steak Houses defied expectations by posting substantially
higher fourth-quarter earnings, despite rising beef prices. Ryan's rose 1.15, or 7.6%, to 16.28, Papa John's
International gained 1, or 3%, to 34.03, and Jack in the Box (NYSE) added 30 cents, or 1.3%, to 23.75.
Fitness-equipment maker Nautilus Group (NYSE) fell 2.46, or 16%, to 13.37, bucking gains in the
recreational-services group, after posting a sharp decline in fourth-quarter income.
Software stocks struggled after large-cap Veritas Software set disappointing first-quarter targets. For its part,
DigitalThink plunged 38% to 1.86 amid fears the educational-software maker may lose business from
Electronic Data Systems because of a contract dispute.

INVESTORS CONTINUED to wring their hands over Wednesday's subtle shift in Federal Reserve
interest-rate policy.
Blue-chip stocks rebounded after sagging Wednesday, but technology stocks and small stocks kept falling.
Many more stocks fell than rose on both the New York Stock Exchange and Nasdaq Stock Market.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average, which surged Monday and dropped Tuesday and Wednesday, rose 41.92
points, or 0.40%, to 10510.29. The gyrations left the blue chips down 58 points, or 0.5%, on the week -- and
up only 0.5% since the year began.
Volume on the Big Board was the highest of the new year, surpassing the previous high on Wednesday.
The Nasdaq Composite Index, whose tech stocks have led the year's gains, fell 0.44%, or 9.14 points, to
2068.23, down 2.6% on the week but still up 3% on the year.
The declines "have blown a little foam off the beer. It was getting a little frothy out there," said David Briggs,
head of stock trading at Pittsburgh mutual-fund group Federated Investors. "Whoever is in the market buying
has gone with the safer stuff."
A subtle shift in language in the statement following Wednesday's Fed policy meeting sowed worry that the
Fed could raise target interest rates by spring or early summer. Treasury-bond prices fell mildly after
Wednesday's sharp drop. Gold fell under $400 an ounce as the dollar again rebounded against the euro. Oil
prices fell.
The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.50%, or 5.63 points, to 1134.11, up 2% on the year.
Investors will be closely watching fourth-quarter economic-growth numbers due out today.

Bonds declined. The 10-year Treasury note fell 2/32, or $6.25 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, rose to 4.186%.

Gold Falls as Stronger Dollar Prompts Speculators to Sell
Commodities Report
Gold Falls as Stronger Dollar Prompts Speculators to Sell
By Gavin Maguire
381 words
30 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Gold prices on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange crumpled more than
$16 a troy ounce, or 4%, as a rebounding U.S. dollar sparked heavy selling by speculative funds, which
previously had been the big buyers.
The most active April contract fell $16.40 to $399.40 an ounce -- its first close below the $400 mark since late
November.
Silver, platinum and palladium stampeded lower as buyers shunned precious metals.
The dollar's upswing has come on the back of a perceived shift in the Federal Reserve's stance toward
interest rates. A stronger U.S. currency renders dollar-denominated gold more expensive and a more
attractive sale in other currencies.
"By hinting that [interest] rates don't have to stay low forever, the Fed removed in part one of the big factors
pressuring the dollar lower these past few months. And that's what sent it higher today and gold lower,"
argued Dave Rinehimer, director of futures research at Citigroup Global Markets.
Tom Boustead, precious-metals analyst at Refco LLC, agreed, and allowed for an additional $5 to $10 of
price erosion for April gold over the coming days.
Longer term, Messrs. Boustead and Rinehimer agreed that the dollar's tone and direction were critical. "This
leads me to believe that what we are seeing in gold is just a short-term correction rather than a trend
change." said Mr. Boustead, adding that "there are many things to look out for over the near term that could
determine whether this dollar rally will continue or not," including U.S. gross-domestic-product figures for the
fourth quarter, which are due today, and the coming Group of Seven meeting. "These things pose risks for
gold in the meantime, so I'm allowing for more weakness and more wide price movement," he said.
Mr. Rinehimer added that concern for certain areas of the economy as well as geopolitical uncertainty were
likely to hobble the U.S. currency over the longer run and thereby aid gold.
In other commodity markets:
COTTON: March futures shed 2.10 cents to 68.66 cents a pound.
CRUDE OIL:March futures dropped 81 cents to $32.81 a barrel.

Hands on the Wheel
REVIEW &OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Hands on the Wheel
559 words
30 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A12
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Financial markets have given a muted raspberry to the Federal Reserve's hint on Wednesday about an
earlier increase in interest rates than many have expected. Markets always hate surprises, but this looks to
us like good news if it means that Chairman Alan Greenspan and comrades are finally putting their hands
back on the wheel.
For some time now the Greenspan Fed has been driving monetary policy like a daredevil, even as future
price signals have been flashing yellow. The dollar has been falling against the price of gold and against
other major currencies, oil has been as high as $35 a barrel in recent weeks, and commodity prices in
general are in a reflationary boom. The pertinent question isn't whether the Fed might tighten too early but
whether it may already be too late to head off inflation that leads to even higher interest rates in 2005 and
2006.
The Fed may have finally begun to wonder this itself. Its Wednesday statement dropped its famous pledge to
maintain easy money for a "considerable period." In its place was language declaring that the Fed's Open
Market Committee believes "it can be patient in removing its policy accommodation." Everyone is trying to
figure out what difference this represents, a la Kremlinology. But our reading, or at least our hope, is that it is
a sign that Mr. Greenspan is reasserting some leadership over his fellow governors who have talked as if
inflation can't possibly come back.
We don't think that modestly rising interest rates this year are a danger to an economic recovery that looks
strong on nearly all counts. Today's fourth-quarter GDP figure is expected to fall from the third quarter's
blowout 8.2% but still be robust. The early evidence in January is also on the bullish side.
Housing, business spending, corporate profits and consumer confidence are all up, while jobless claims have
been falling. For a change, even much of the rest of the world is growing. In this environment, and especially
with the dollar weak, maintaining the 1% fed funds rate offers more long-term risks than short-term benefits.
Yes, the Fed is always wary of raising rates in an election year. And yes, job creation isn't yet as rapid as
anyone would like. The Fed's Wednesday statement did say, however, that "although new hiring remains
subdued, other indicators suggest an improvement in the labor market."
As Mr. Greenspan recently noted, the jobs that will be created from innovation and risk-taking are always
harder to discern than the jobs being extinguished because of new competition or technological change.
Kodak's decision to shed 15,000 jobs over three years because of competition from digital cameras makes
headlines, but the thousands of new jobs created by advances in digital technology don't.
In any event, the Fed's job is not to get the jobless rate down. The Bush tax cuts in marginal income rates
and on dividends have turned out to be extremely well timed and have improved incentives to invest and take
risk. New business investment and profits will eventually produce new job growth. The Fed's job is to
maintain price stability, and that means preventing future inflation even if it requires tightening money in an
election year.

Page 145 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Stocks, Bonds Drop on Fed Move
Wednesday's Markets
Stocks, Bonds Drop on Fed Move
By E.S. Browning and Aaron Lucchetti
1,159 words
29 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
STOCK AND BOND prices fell heavily after the Federal Reserve surprised investors by hinting that it may
raise its target short-term interest rates sooner than many had expected.
The drop of 141.55 points, or 1.33%, in the Dow Jones Industrial Average yesterday, to 10468.37, was the
heaviest decline in both point and percentage terms since October. The bond-market rout sent yields, which
move opposite to price, sharply higher.
But the prospect of higher U.S. rates helped the sagging dollar, which rebounded on the day, notably against
the euro, Europe's common currency.
The decline left the Dow industrials up a bare 0.1% since the year began. After soaring 134 points on
Monday, to their highest level since well before the terrorist attacks of Sept. 11, 2001, the blue chips have
given back all of that and more. They are down 1.2% for the week so far, facing the prospect of a second
consecutive weekly decline, after eight straight weeks of gains.
Trading volume on the New York Stock Exchange was the heaviest of the young year.
A big part of the problem was the surprise. Normally, when Fed officials plan a switch in their message to
investors, they telegraph it, but this time there was little warning and investors were caught off-guard.
Fed target rates themselves didn't change. Fed officials, at their regular January policy meeting, left their
target rate for overnight lending among banks at a 45-year low of 1%. But they changed the wording of their
commentary, and that is what surprised people. Instead of stressing that they didn't plan to change their
target "for a considerable period," they wrote that they "can be patient" about changing rates.
The words were little different, but investors interpreted the switch as a hint that a rate increase is in the
cards. Because low interest rates have been the primary driver behind a strong bond market, and one of the
main supporters for stocks, investors flinched.
"The Fed has blindsided the markets -- again. Take an `F' for communication skills," complained Ian
Shepherdson, chief U.S. economist at High Frequency Economics in Valhalla, New York, in a note to clients.
Almost all of the day's main moves in the financial markets came after the Fed news, as investors, who had
been expecting nothing new from the Fed, suddenly shifted their attention away from the relatively positive
corporate earnings news and the economy, and to interest rates.
Bond investors were particularly unnerved, because interest rates are the primary driver of their market, and
because a shift in Fed interest-rate policy changes everything. The benchmark 10-year Treasury note, whose
yield earlier this month had dipped below 4% based on hopes that the Fed would leave rates unchanged for
months to come, jumped to 4.177%. The note's price sagged by 26/32, or $8.125 for every $1,000 invested.
The shift in expectations could be seen in futures trading. At the Chicago Board of Trade, futures on the
federal-funds rate -- the rate for which the Fed sets its target -- moved sharply. Before the Fed news, traders
were pricing in only a 44% chance that the Fed would raise its target rate by June. After the announcement,
trading indicated a 72% chance of a quarter-point rise, to 1.25%, in June. Futures trading indicated that
traders think a quarter-point rise by August is a near certainty.
The change in language was surprising because employment growth has been so weak recently, said
Vincent Boberski, managing director of fixed-income research at RBC Dain Rauscher. "I'm trying to decide
whether I should rethink my outlook" and factor in an interest-rate increase earlier than previously anticipated,
he said.

--- Page 146 ---
Page 146 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Many in the bond market had been expecting a shift in Fed body language, although not so soon.
"I can't say I thought they'd drop it today, but it was in the cards" over the next couple of months, said William
Gross, chief investment officer at Pacific Investment Management Co., or Pimco. Mr. Gross says he still
expects the Fed to hold off on raising rates until the second half of the year -- probably until the fourth quarter
-- but adds the Fed's statement yesterday was a small step toward making an interest-rate increase "easier to
swallow" for the bond market.
The yield of the 10-year note still is below the 4.5% range that it hit over the summer, and stock investors
noted that they won't get too nervous about rising interest rates until that yield moves closer to 4.75% or 5%,
which many consider still some way off. But unless the Fed changes its tune, many in the bond market
believe, bond yields are likely to move up now, and that is bad news for stocks and bonds.
Rising yields also are negative for the economy because other market rates, such as the rates of fixed-rate
mortgages, sometimes are pegged to the yield of the 10-year note. Rising rates could put a damper on
demand for new mortgages.
Some analysts have been warning for weeks that stocks have risen so much -- the Nasdaq Composite Index
was up 50% last year and the Dow industrials 25% -- that they are overdue for at least a temporary pullback.
But others point out that the Fed's willingness to begin raising rates could be viewed as a positive for stocks.
The change in language suggests that the Fed sees the economy and corporate profits more solidly back in
the saddle.
Still, the initial reaction was negative. The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 1.36%, or 15.57
points, to 1128.48, down 1% this week and up only 1.5% for the year so far.
The Nasdaq composite, whose many technology stocks have led the gains this year, fell 1.83%, or 38.67
points, to 2077.37, down 4% this week and now up only 3.7% on the year.
Small stocks fell even harder, with the Russell 2000 small-stock index declining 1.89%, or 11.26 points, to
583.91.
Some analysts consider that small stocks, which do more of their borrowing directly from banks than do larger
companies, would be hurt proportionately more by rising rates.
Gold rose $4.50 to $414.50, while oil fell 50 cents to $33.62.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. On the Big Board, where 1.84 billion shares traded, 953 stocks rose and 2,367 fell.
Bonds declined. The 30-year bond was down 31/32 to yield 5.006%.
The dollar strengthened. Late in New York, it traded at 106.02 yen, up from 105.64 yen, while the euro fell
against the dollar to $1.2488 from $1.2640.

Dow Industrials Give Back Gains Of Previous Day
Tuesday's Markets
Dow Industrials Give Back Gains Of Previous Day
By E.S. Browning
639 words
28 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
A DAY AFTER hitting its highest level since before the terrorist attacks of Sept. 11, 2001, the Dow Jones
Industrial Average gave back most of the previous day's gains.
The up-and-down market reflects the combination of hope and fear that dominates stock trading these days.
Although powerful earnings news and strong economic gains have persuaded many investors that stocks are
due to rise further, worries are spreading that the market is overdue for at least a temporary pullback.
Yesterday, the Dow industrials fell 92.59 points, or 0.87%, to 10609.92, giving back much of Monday's
134-point gain. The industrial average still is up 1.5% this year, after rising 25% last year, but it is facing head
winds.
The Nasdaq Composite Index, whose many technology stocks have led the market's gains for more than a
year, gave back all of Monday's advance and more. Up 1.41% on Monday, it fell 1.75%, or 37.79 points, to
2116.04, still up 5.6% for the year. The Nasdaq rose 50% last year.
The problem lately has been that earnings and the economy have been so strong that anything short of
spectacular news can be viewed as a disappointment.
Yesterday, the Conference Board's consumer-confidence report showed a strong increase to the highest
level in 18 months. But some forecasters had expected an even steeper advance.
Texas Instruments fell despite reporting strong quarterly results, as some analysts complained about its profit
margins. Novellus Systems also declined despite good profits because its performance forecast was
considered uninspiring. Caterpillar reported strong profits and sales but fell anyhow, as analysts had
expected stronger operating margins.
"The earnings news was fairly good, but there were high expectations," said Taai Izushima, head of U.S.
stock trading at brokerage firm Daiwa Securities America in New York. "When people don't see spectacular
results, the stock gets sold off a little bit. But it looks like a temporary setback" for the market as a whole.
One reason for nerves is that Federal Reserve policy makers today end a regular two-day meeting and
announce their decision on interest rates as well as their outlook for the future. Investors widely expect the
Fed target rate for overnight interbank rates to stay at 1%, a 45-year low. They think the Fed will indicate that
it still doesn't plan any changes in rates "for a considerable period." But as they wait, some investors are
nervous, which makes them more likely to make short-term sales.
The dollar resumed its decline, falling to 105.64 yen, its lowest point in more than three years. The dollar also
fell against the euro. Treasury bonds, which had declined Monday amid the stock surge, regained most of
their lost ground. Gold rebounded but crude oil declined.
The broad Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.98%, or 11.32 points, to 1144.05, still up almost 3% so far
this year.
Outside the U.S., stocks advanced in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
rose 1.04%, or 1.56 points, to 151.43.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks retreated. On the Big Board, where 1.59 billion shares traded, 1,412 stocks rose and 1,866 fell.
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 15/32, or $4.6875 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which
moves inversely to price, fell to 4.078%. The 30-year bond was up 22/32 to yield 4.943%.
