Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / Who's Hot...And Not: Correction for Technology Stocks --- Microsoft's Record Sales Weren't...
Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / Who's Hot...And Not: Correction for Technology Stocks
--- Microsoft's Record Sales Weren't Enough to Save Sector From a Down Week
By Peter A. McKay
646 words
26 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections &Amplifications
THE LAST NAME of Geoff Dennis, a Smith Barney strategist, was incorrectly given as Davis in yesterday's
Tracking the Numbers column.
(WSJ Jan. 27, 2004)
(END)
EVEN UPBEAT news from Microsoft Corp. couldn't salvage the week for the technology sector, which took a
rare breather from its long-term rally while crude oil, foreign shares and the downtrodden gold market made
gains.
The week's trends essentially represented a 180-degree turn from the previous week's list of winners and
losers. But most analysts said the reversal could be short-lived.
For the week, the Nasdaq Composite Index fell 0.8%, or 16.59 points, to 2123.87. But it remains 6% higher
for the month, including a 4.86-point advance Friday.
The Dow Jones World Technology Index and the Dow Jones U.S. Technology Index were also lower last
week. Those moves came despite Microsoft's announcement late Thursday that it posted record fiscal
second-quarter sales of $10.15 billion, a 19% leap. Microsoft stock rallied 47 cents, or 1.7%, on the news
Friday, to $28.48 a share. For the week, the software titan's stock was up 2.4% on the Nasdaq Stock Market.
Analysts took Microsoft's sales gains, which came despite a 17% decline in the company's net income, as a
sign that technology spending has made a comeback. For that reason, many say the sector should take off
again, even if last week's declines in major tech indicators represented a slight correction.
"I still wouldn't want to own technology companies that are at the point of sale with the consumer. But there
are a lot of other companies in the supply chain that have room to do well," said Joseph A. Zock, president of
Capital Management Associates, a New York portfolio-management firm.
Mr. Zock said manufacturers and technology-services companies should be particularly well positioned. But
he doesn't believe the economy is strong enough that retail-dependent companies in technology or other
sectors have enough leverage yet to demand higher prices for their goods. Unlike technology shares, foreign
shares gained last week, after lackluster performance the previous week. The Dow Jones World Stock Index
rose nearly 1%.
Strategist Geoff Davis, of Smith Barney, said foreign shares, especially emerging markets, tend to do well in
environments in which investors switch from being cautious to more risk-tolerant. With interest rates low and
the economies of the U.S. and other powers beginning to show more robust growth, he said such an attitude
switch is under way.
He said emerging markets began a prolonged rally in June, with Latin America -- traditionally viewed as one
of the riskiest regions -- leading the way.
"We've become the flavor of the month in some sense," said Mr. Davis. "That might change later in the year,
when the [Federal Reserve] is likely to raise rates. But for now, it's following the classic pattern that you would
see, given the market conditions."
The other factor that has helped foreign shares is the weakness of the U.S. dollar, which was in full display
again last week after a brief respite. The currency's trade-weighted value dipped 0.33%.

plunge.
Crude-oil futures rose 91 cents, or 2.7%, to $34.94 a barrel at Nymex. But the broader Dow Jones-AIG
Commodity Index rose a more-modest 0.90%.

news came as the company reported a fiscal second-quarter loss of 15 cents a share, narrower than the
50-cent-a-share loss it incurred one year earlier and meeting Wall Street estimates.
F5 Networks also provided the market with an upbeat report. The company reported first-quarter earnings of
11 cents a share, compared with two cents a year ago and four cents higher than analysts' expectations. The
stock jumped 5, or 16%, to 36.05.
Eclipsys, a provider of health-care information technology, gained 2.83, or 24%, to 14.70, despite reporting a
fourth-quarter loss of 46 cents a share, much wider than the 31-cent-a-share loss Wall Street had expected.
But the company's revenue climbed 24%, higher than consensus estimates, and it announced plans to break
even in 2004, providing some relief for investors.
Market trackers said the small-cap market was due for a pause as two of the major indexes tracking these
stocks have hit notable highs in recent sessions. As a result, investors pocketed some profits from the group.
"This is the kind of environment where investors have some lingering skepticism in the recovery," said Cary
Nordan, equity analyst at BB&T Asset Management. "There are overhanging issues with the deficit and weak
dollar on one side and low interest rates on the other side."
The Russell 2000 Index of small stocks fell 5.75, or 0.96%, to 591.73. The Standard &Poor's SmallCap 600
Index fell 1.76, or 0.62%, to 282.42. The Nasdaq Composite Index of large- and small-cap stocks fell 23.44,
or 1.09%, to 2119.01.

from hold at Smith Barney. Among small-caps, Mindspeed Technologies fell 1.01, or 9.2%, to 10.
Echelon fell 1.20, or 9.6%, to 11.25 after the company reported a fourth-quarter net loss of one cent a share,
compared with net income of nine cents a share a year ago.
Verilink, a communication-equipment maker, shed 1.29, or 19%, to 5.69 after the company posted a

Airline stocks, which led the decliners Wednesday, reversed course, making the group the strongest

NYSE.

company reported fourth-quarter earnings of 24 cents a share, up from 10 cents a share one year earlier. The
company cited higher passenger loads, reversal of a tax allowance and lower per-flight costs for the increase.

Dow Industrials Climb 94.96
Wednesday's Markets
Dow Industrials Climb 94.96
By E.S. Browning
456 words
22 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
AFTER A ONE-DAY pullback, the Dow Jones Industrial Average resumed its advance, hitting a fresh
22-month high amid more upbeat earnings reports.
"The sense is that earnings are coming through and you've got the wind at your back," said Andy Brooks,
head of stock trading at Baltimore mutual-fund group T. Rowe Price.
J.P. Morgan Chase, a component of the Dow industrials, released earnings that far exceeded Wall Street
expectations. The big bank rose 2.6% and pulled up most of the financial sector. In addition came news that
new housing starts in December hit their highest monthly level in 19 years.
After seeing the Dow industrials rise 25% last year and the Nasdaq Composite Index soar 50%, many traders
are nervous about the risk of at least a temporary decline. That kind of worry sent stocks down at the start of
trading.
But the Dow soon reversed course and finished up 94.96 points, or 0.90%, at 10623.62. That marks the blue
chips' highest level since March 2002, 22 months ago, and represents a gain of more than 1.6% so far this
year. The Nasdaq Composite, which hit a 30-month high Tuesday, finished the day down, falling 0.26%, or
5.53 points, to 2142.45 -- still up almost 7% this year.
Technology stocks have risen so much in the past 15 months that investors can decide to sell them on the
faintest hint of bad news. Motorola, for example, reported a strong quarterly-earnings gain, but saw its stock
fall on news of disappointing cellphone sales.
On top of that, RF Micro Devices, a communications-chip maker, and Seagate Technology, which makes
computer drives, both fell after reducing forecasts for the current quarter. Lucent Technologies fell despite
posting a quarterly profit, because it indicated that sales this year could be lower than Wall Street had hoped.
But many fund managers who have been buying tech stocks believe the shares have more room to rise.
"We are in a healthy environment because interest rates are low and inflation is gone for all intents and
purposes, at least for now," said Stephen Coleman, chief investment officer of money-management firm
Daedalus Capital in St. Louis.
The dollar fell, gold fell and oil, which had been up sharply on Tuesday, also fell. Treasury bonds rose. The
10-year Treasury note rose 9/32, or $2.8125 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which moves inversely to
price, fell to 4.019%.
The S&P 500 rose 0.78%, or 8.85 points, to 1147.62, its own 22-month high and a gain of more than 3% this
year.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Vietnam Fears Chicken Sales Could Spread Virus
HANOI, Vietnam -- Vietnamese veterinary officials, worried that a deadly flu virus could be spreading further,
said nearly a million chickens believed to have been exposed to the bug were hastily sold in the past three
and a half weeks rather than killed as part of a continuing cull.
Officials don't know whether any of the chickens were infected with the strain of avian flu known as H5N1. But
they said the missing birds belonged to flocks in which H5N1 was detected in December and this month.
"This leads to a high risk that the virus will spread," said Hoang Van Nam, head of the epidemic division at
the Veterinary Department under the Ministry of Agriculture and Rural Development.
Officials said some farmers sold the chickens in different markets because they were reluctant to lose money
or failed to understand the risk during the outbreak's early stages in late December. Officials from the Food
and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations confirmed the number of "missing" chickens at about
890,000. Sale of the chickens with their feathers intact could pose a wider public-health risk because it is
possible for the virus to jump from chicken feathers to people, according to World Health Organization
experts.
The flu has killed five people and spread to poultry in 21 of Vietnam's 64 provinces. So far, the WHO hasn't
detected any signs of human-to-human transmission. To prevent further spread of the disease, officials will
order more chickens to be killed and will guarantee compensation to farmers for the loss of their birds.
The missing chickens "would be a cause for concern," said Bob Dietz, the WHO spokesman here. But the
extent of the risk varies. "If they were sold live, or freshly killed with their feathers intact, that would be more of
a threat," he said, because chicken feathers and dust could harbor the virus. "If the chickens were butchered
and plucked and sold as chicken meat, that would not be as much of a threat." Cooked chicken and eggs are
considered safe by the WHO.
-- Margot Cohen
Indonesia to Slow Privatization Before Elections
JAKARTA, Indonesia -- Indonesian officials said they plan to slow, but not stop, the country's privatization
process ahead of critical elections later this year.
Deputy State Enterprises Minister Bacelius Ruru said that the government has dropped plans to sell stakes in
national airline PT Garuda Indonesia, fertilizer company PT Pupuk Kaltim and airport operators PT Angkasa
Pura I and PT Angkasa Pura II. Last week, the government had said the four companies were among 14
state-owned ones to be privatized in 2004.
Separately, the Indonesian Bank Restructuring Agency said a consortium seeking to acquire another
state-held asset -- PT Bank Lippo -- has until Tuesday to submit a new bid for a 52.05% stake in the bank,
because its earlier bid was below the floor price set by the agency. The investment consortium, known as
Swissasia Global, "has one week to renew its bid," said IBRA spokesman Rohan Hafas.
Swissasia, which is led by Austria's Raiffeisen Zentralbank Oesterreich AG, is the only bidder IBRA has
short-listed for the Bank Lippo stake.
The delays reflect some of the difficulties Indonesia has encountered in privatizing state companies and
selling assets that the government took over after Asia's 1997-98 financial crisis.

of the vote, which could discourage investors. The government expects to raise five trillion rupiah ($596.8

THE AMERICAS
Canada Cuts Rates in Bid to Stimulate Economy
OTTAWA -- The Bank of Canada cut its benchmark interest rate to 2.5% from 2.75% in a bid to stimulate
economic growth, which has been slowed by the Canadian dollar's 22% rise against the U.S. dollar since the
start of last year.
The central bank, citing a subdued inflation outlook, appeared to leave the door open to another cut on
March 2, its next policy-announcement date.
The Canadian dollar's sharp rise against the greenback has weakened Canadian exports and increased
imports, sapping demand for Canadian goods and services, the bank said. The stronger currency makes
Canadian goods less competitive in the U.S., which takes in about 85% of Canada's exports and 62% of its
factory output.
While employment growth in Canada has remained strong, other recent indicators of domestic demand "have
been somewhat weaker than expected," leaving more slack in the economy than anticipated, the central
bank said. As a result, inflation is likely to remain below the central bank's 2% target "late into 2005," it
said.
Many economists predict Canada's gross domestic product, or the value of all goods and services produced
in a nation, will expand slightly more than 3% this year. Many caution that further rises in the Canadian dollar
could drag growth below that level.
Sherry Cooper, chief economist with the BMO Nesbitt Burns securities firm in Toronto, said the Bank of
Canada "is leaving the door wide open for another rate trim in March" unless domestic growth "soon revs up."
The Canadian dollar, which hit a 10 1/2-year high of 78.7 U.S. cents this month, slipped last week after
Canada posted a narrower-than-expected trade surplus for November. That data fueled expectations the
Bank of Canada would cut rates this week, making the currency less attractive to portfolio investors. Still, the
2.5% Canadian overnight rate remains well above the comparable U.S. federal-funds rate of 1%.
-- Christopher J. Chipello

The 0.15% levy is set to go into effect in February. The government hopes to raise up to 1.5 billion soles

banking sector to make it easier to track flows of cash in the economy. The tax is scheduled to last three

Payback Time: After Credit Binge In South Korea, Big Bill Comes Due --- Government Pushed Plastic To Juice Up the Economy; Banks, Borrowers...
Payback Time: After Credit Binge In South Korea, Big Bill Comes Due --- Government Pushed Plastic
To Juice Up the Economy; Banks, Borrowers Suffer --- Ms. Kim's $42,000 Tab
By Hae Won Choi and Gordon Fairclough
2,355 words
20 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
SEOUL, South Korea -- A household-debt crisis rocking South Korea provides a chilling warning to
developing nations around the world: Doling out credit cards to boost consumer spending can juice growth in
the short run, but the ultimate consequences -- for financial institutions and the economy -- can be dire.
South Korea once boasted one of the world's highest savings rates. But in a sharp about-face over the past
few years, consumers -- with government prodding -- have gone on a credit-fueled buying binge. Now, the bill
is coming due, as mounting credit-card defaults traumatize families and rock the nation's financial system.
Between 1999 and 2003, South Korea's total outstanding credit-card debt grew more than five-fold. By the
third quarter of last year, the latest period for which figures are available, Korean consumers had run up a
collective credit-card balance of $57.47 billion, compared with $10.95 billion at the end of 1999.
Many are having trouble paying their debt. South Korean regulators say the nationwide
credit-card-delinquency rate, defined as the percentage of loans that are more than 30 days overdue, jumped
to 13.5% in November, compared with 2.6% at the end of 2001. Some industry executives, however, say the
actual proportion of bad loans may be as high as 30%, given aggressive efforts by lenders to mask the
problem. In the U.S., by contrast, bankers are wringing their hands over new data that show Americans hit a
record-high credit card delinquency rate of 4.09% in the third quarter of 2003.
Debt-related murder-suicides and other crimes have become a regular staple of South Korean newspapers
and TV news broadcasts. Last summer, a 34-year-old woman pushed her two daughters, ages 3 and 7, out
the window of the family's 15th-floor apartment. Then, holding tight to her 6-year-old son's hand, she jumped,
too. Police say the woman, a dishwasher who they would identify only as Mrs. Sohn, was distraught over
$25,000 in credit-card debt that she and her husband, a construction worker, couldn't repay.
Because Korea's social structure makes family members feel accountable for each other's debts, one
person's credit problems often ripple outward through entire families and social networks, curbing the
spending of large groups of people and potentially prolonging a slump in consumer spending. Kim Jun Ho, an
animator in Seoul, complains bitterly that his entire family has been turned into "credit-card slaves," with
family members having to devote much of their income to supporting his sister and repaying her $42,000 in
credit-card debt.
All the bad loans are putting a serious strain on South Korea's banks and other financial institutions, which
are still recovering from the Asian financial crisis of the late 1990s, when bad loans to big conglomerates
brought many to the brink of collapse. Earlier this month, banks had to intervene to rescue the country's
largest card issuer, LG Card Co., from insolvency with a $3.19 billion bailout package.
Similar problems have also started to crop up in other Asian nations. As countries in the region tried to get
their economies rolling again in the wake of the late-1990s financial crisis, many nudged banks to make
consumer borrowing easier as part of a strategy to strengthen domestic consumption and reduce the
countries' reliance on exports. South Korea even offered consumers tax breaks for credit-card use.
In Taiwan, outstanding credit-card debt has ballooned 80% over the past three years. In Hong Kong, rising
card debt has helped spur a 28-fold increase in personal bankruptcies, compared with five years ago. And in
Singapore, personal bankruptcies were up about 20% in 2003 from the year before.
Some governments are now trying to rein in consumer credit. Thailand has raised income requirements for
cardholders and Taiwan has strengthened controls on card issuers. Singapore is clamping down on
aggressive credit-card marketing techniques.
Much of the pain from Asia's bad loans could be offset as the countries' export industries pick up steam.
Economists estimate that South Korea's gross domestic product will grow 5.2% in 2004, compared with an

--- Page 169 ---
Page 169 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
estimated 2.9% in 2003, powered by an expected 13.2% increase in exports. Even so, analysts worry that
credit problems could make Asia's economies extremely susceptible to external shocks and further constrain
growth if the problems continue to spread.
Kim Sang Mi, Mr. Kim's sister, got in on South Korea's credit boom -- and bust -- at the beginning. One of the
first things she did when she moved to Seoul in 2000 was go online and apply for two credit cards. She used
them to buy furniture and appliances for her new apartment. Then she branched out into purchases of hip
clothes and fancy cosmetics, things she couldn't get in the small town on Korea's southwest coast where she
grew up. She also started going out with friends to restaurants and bars, charging nearly everything. She
says she was motivated to use credit cards instead of cash because she wanted to take advantage of the tax
breaks offered by the South Korean government.
"I wanted to blend in, be like people in Seoul," says Ms. Kim, now 27 years old and an accountant at an
online game company. "I would buy things with my credit cards, making all sorts of distorted calculations in
my mind of how I would pay the bills." About a year into her shopping spree, Ms. Kim was struggling to pay
her bills. She started taking cash advances on some of her cards, which by then numbered five, to make the
payments on the others.
Ms. Kim borrowed thousands of dollars from her friends and family, wiping out much of the savings of her
brother and parents. Then, early last year, after she had run up a tab of more than $42,000, her juggling act
failed. Her card companies pressed her to restructure her debt. To do that, her parents and her brother had to
sign as guarantors.
Under her new deal with the card companies, Ms. Kim must devote nearly her entire salary of about $1,100 a
month to card payments for the next three years. She seldom goes out and volunteers to work late so that
she can get free meals from her company. Her brother pays most of her rent and living expenses, and for the
past two months, about half his salary has gone toward paying her card bills.
Unsustainable household debt burdens have led to serious declines in consumer spending in South Korea. In
the first nine months of 2003, the latest period for which data is available, private domestic consumption fell
1.1%, according to the country's central bank, compared with a 6.8% year-on-year increase in 2002. Korean
department store sales in 2003 fell 6.3% below 2002 levels.
The case of LG Card shows just how precarious South Korea's situation is. The company's lenders had to
bail it out for the second time in three months in early January, essentially agreeing to acquire the card issuer
by swapping $1.69 billion in outstanding debt for equity. LG Card's creditors, including some of the nation's
largest banks, also promised to lend a fresh $1.39 billion to keep the company afloat.
The latest rescue was organized by the state-run Korea Development Bank at the prodding of the South
Korean government, which feared the impact LG Card's failure could have on the rest of the financial system
and the national economy. Finance ministry officials pointed to a report by LG Card's auditors, which
estimated the potential cost to creditors and LG Card bondholders at $22.51 billion.
They also feared a contagion effect. If LG Card went out of business, they said, it could lead to a cascade of
delinquencies at other card issuers, since many Koreans use cash advances on one credit card to make their
payments on others. Last year, as LG Card teetered, it briefly suspended cash-advance services in
November. Even that short disruption caused a jump in delinquencies at other card issuers, says Patrick Han,
an analyst at J.P. Morgan in Seoul.
Other card issuers, including many of the country's biggest banks, had $3.71 billion in delinquent credit-card
loans on their books at the end of November, according to government regulators. Kookmin Bank, the
nation's largest lender, has said that it expects to post a net loss for 2003, largely as a result of increased
provisions for credit-card and household loans.
The current mess has its roots in a South Korean government campaign -- starting in 1999 -- to strengthen
domestic consumption. The government established rules allowing consumers to deduct some credit-card
purchases from their taxes and pushed for low interest rates to stimulate spending. It also lifted a ceiling on
cash advances. Previously, individuals had been allowed to take out only about $600 a month in cash
through their credit cards. The government further slashed taxes on luxury goods and cars to encourage
spending.
The government push came as the country's banks were casting about for a new way to make money after
the big corporate bankruptcies of the late 1990s. Household lending, and credit cards in particular, were seen
as a potential source of new profits.
For decades South Korea's banks had focused on funneling money from depositors to the nation's massive
family-controlled conglomerates, such as Samsung Group and LG Group. The banks had little experience in
consumer lending. Many started handing out credit cards and other unsecured loans without adequate

--- Page 170 ---
Page 170 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
risk-management controls. When the credit-card boom started, Korea had no independent organization that
tracked individuals' credit-worthiness, a service that is common in more developed countries. Now, banks and
other card issuers share data on delinquent customers.
A gold-rush mentality prevailed, as card issuers fought to win market share by offering customers ever-larger
amounts of credit at lower interest rates. Companies set up street-corner booths, getting signatures and
issuing cards on the spot without in-depth credit analysis, and with credit lines of thousands of dollars.
(MORE)
Between 1999 and 2003, the total number of credit cards in circulation more than tripled, hitting 72.8 million,
or about two cards for every person over the age of 17. The amount of outstanding credit jumped to more
than 15% of the country's annual economic output in 2002. It has since fallen, to about 11% of gross
domestic product. By comparison, U.S. credit-card debt amounted to about 6.8% of GDP in 2002, the most
recent full year for which data is available.
But a rising number of Korea's once-thrifty consumers, new to the concept of buying on credit, started falling
behind on their bills. As delinquencies mounted, card issuers scrambled to restructure loans and, in some
cases, mask the extent of their bad debt.
Nearly all Korean credit cards require that the entire balance be paid every month. So, as card customers fell
behind, companies gave them new loans with extended maturities and higher interest rates that borrowers
used to pay off their old debts. The most conscientious lenders required upfront payments and other proof of
creditworthiness before delinquent borrowers could qualify for loan-work-out programs.
But in other cases, new loans were extended with scant proof that they could be repaid. The goal in these
cases, some bankers say, was to hide the real number of nonperforming loans. In some cases, delinquent
borrowers were given new loans with a six-month or one-year moratorium on repayments. In essence, that
turned bad loans into good ones on the company's books.
In other cases, card issuers issued new loans to delinquent customers and then recorded the loans as cash
advances, even though no cash was ever given to the customers. Instead, the money was used to repay the
delinquent credit-card balances and make the accounts current.
Woori Finance Holdings Co., the country's second-largest financial institution, used this method regularly. It
first disclosed the practice at the end of September, when it had to meet stricter U.S. accounting standards in
order to trade on the New York Stock Exchange.
Woori, which is 87%-owned by the South Korean government, said it stopped using cash advances to bail out
defaulters in October, when it had $596.91 million of the so-called cash-advance-substitution loans on its
books. Soon after, regulators warned credit-card companies to halt such tactics.
Youm Eun Ha, a 27-year-old bookkeeper, says that when she fell behind in payments to LG Card in
September, the company restructured her loan, despite her objections, and gave her a six-month moratorium
on payments. Ms. Youm said LG told her that if she didn't restructure, it would garnishee 50% of her salary
every month.
Ms. Youm, who owes LG Card $12,650, says she was told she would have to start repaying her restructured
loan, which carried a slightly higher interest rate, at the end of March. Ms. Youm, who earns about $775 a
month, is already paying about $470 a month to another card company. "On my salary, there's no way I'll be
able to pay back the money" to LG Card, she says.
LG Card said it couldn't comment specifically on Ms. Youm's case. But a spokesman said that "when our
customers reach a point where they no longer can pay back their debt, we advise them to restructure," adding
that the company does "not force" customers to take the step.
---
Phillip Day in Singapore and Jason Dean in Taipei contributed to this article.

Moving the Market -- Tracking the Numbers / Who's Hot...and Not: Global Investors Voice U.S. Worries
--- Last Week's Jobless Data Curbed Some Momentum; 10-Year Treasury Yield Slips
By Craig Karmin
678 words
19 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
GLOBAL INVESTORS last week expressed renewed concern about the strength of the U.S. economy, but
their appetite for riskier securities doesn't seem to be waning just yet.
Many markets showed signs of a hangover from the Jan. 9 employment numbers, which showed that just
1,000 people were added to nonfarm U.S. payrolls in December, well below economists' expectations.
The news tempered some of the recent momentum pointing to a U.S. economic recovery and produced a
ripple effect throughout other assets and securities. The 10-year Treasury bond yield slipped below 4% last
week for the first time since Oct. 1. Japanese stocks, which often move in the opposite direction of U.S.
Treasury bonds, weakened.
"It was a week characterized by investors stepping back from their bets on a cyclical recovery," said David
Bowers, chief global investment strategist for Merrill Lynch.
Even so, better-than-expected earnings from International Business Machines on Thursday helped salvage
the week for U.S. stocks and counteracted Intel's earnings news, which came in below the unofficial "whisper"
expectations. The Dow Jones Industrial Average finished ahead 141.6 points for the week.
There also was a pause in investor enthusiasm for China. After buying into the China growth miracle, global
markets may be starting to reflect newfound concerns that the economy could be overheating and that Beijing
will raise interest rates in order to slow growth. The Hang Seng China Enterprise Index of mainland shares
trading in Hong Kong plunged 10%, with many investors eager for profit-taking after the index's 152%
advance in 2003.
Any near-term reassessment of China has an echo effect of its own, says Stuart A. Schweitzer, global
markets strategist for J.P. Morgan Fleming: "If Chinese authorities are seen as wanting growth to slow, at a
minimum that's going to slow the advance of commodity prices and could eventually bring them to a halt."
The Reuters CRB Commodity/Futures Index fell 0.04% on the week.
Gold did even worse, tumbling 4.6%. But investors say that was largely a function of the rebounding dollar,
since gold tends to move in the inverse direction of the U.S. currency.
While most analysts continue to view the dollar as being in the midst of a longer-term bear market, the
greenback enjoyed a bit of a comeback last week. The dollar was helped in part by comments from the
president of the European Central Bank that signaled concern about the strength of the euro and how it
could damp Europe's fledgling recovery. His words raised hopes that ECB will move sooner than expected to
cut rates, which would put pressure on the euro.
Yet if global investors expressed some doubts about a U.S. economic recovery or China's rapid expansion, "it
didn't appear that appetite for risk was waning," noted Mr. Bowers.
The Merrill Lynch U.S. Junk Bond Index gained 0.48% for the week, while many emerging-market stocks also
advanced. Still, there were signs that investors were getting weary of some of last year's biggest winners and
rotating into the emerging-market stocks that had lagged.
Seoul's Kospi Index, for instance, was one of the weaker Asian markets last year but rose 0.32% for the
week, as investors were attracted to stock valuations that are cheaper than other emerging markets and bet
that the South Korean economy is on the path to recovery.
Brazil, by contrast, was among the top performers in 2003, but the Bovespa slid 3.19% last week. Despite a
still positive medium-term outlook, the stock rise paused after Fitch ratings agency indicated a credit upgrade

--- Page 172 ---
Page 172 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
for Brazil may not come as soon as expected. New concerns about inflation also sparked fears that the
Brazilian central bank may turn cautious about cutting interest rates.

Rally by Dollar Should Continue Against the Euro
By Don Curren Dow Jones Newswires
475 words
19 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
TORONTO -- The U.S. dollar's bounce higher against the euro and a number of other currencies will probably
persist and remain the dominant theme in foreign-exchange markets this week.
The U.S. currency's break through key levels against the euro and other rivals late last week points to
continued short-term gains, market watchers said.
"If I were to die and come back as a headline writer, my headline would say something like `Dollar Correction
Has Legs,'" said Marc Chandler, chief currency strategist at HSBC in New York.
The U.S. dollar could move in the opposite direction against the yen. But declines against the yen will be
constrained by the threat of renewed market intervention in support of the dollar by Japanese officials. There
are few significant data releases in the U.S. or elsewhere scheduled for this week. A U.S. event with some
potential to roil currency markets is President Bush's State of the Union address tomorrow, analysts said.
U.S. markets are closed today for Martin Luther King Jr. Day.
Late Friday in New York, the dollar was trading at 106.72 yen, up from 106.48 yen late Thursday. The euro
was at $1.2373, well below its $1.2566 quote Thursday and five cents off its record high around $1.2900 hit a
week ago today. The dollar rose to 1.2679 Swiss francs from 1.2456 francs, while the pound fell to $1.7963
from $1.8223.
Weekly reversal patterns in the dollar's value against the euro, Swiss franc, Canadian dollar -- as well as in
the price of gold -- suggest further short-term dollar strength, said Jordan Kotick, technical market strategist
at J.P. Morgan.
The euro, in particular, is expected to bear the brunt of the U.S. dollar's corrective bounce after repeated
expressions of concern from European officials about the common currency's earlier strength and its impact
on the European economy.
The dollar's rally gained momentum after the Treasury Department on Friday released figures showing that
foreigners stepped up their net purchases of U.S. stocks. HSBC's Mr. Chandler said medium-term players will
tend to view euro pullbacks as new buying opportunities. The $1.2270 level is the next significant support
level for the euro, said George Davis, chief currency strategist at RBC Capital Markets in Toronto.
The dollar's movements against the yen have been limited by the threat of currency-market intervention from
the Bank of Japan. Japan's central bank is expected to keep monetary settings on hold at its policy meeting
today and tomorrow. But it may debate a future possible easing to forestall further strength in the yen.

Gold Prices Decline Sharply As U.S. Dollar Heads Higher
Commodities Report
Gold Prices Decline Sharply As U.S. Dollar Heads Higher
By Gavin Maguire
364 words
16 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Gold futures on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange fell more than $13
a troy ounce, or 3%, sparked by gains for the dollar and a deteriorating technical picture.
The most-active February contract dropped $13.30 to $408.70 an ounce.
Gold had scaled 14-year highs to about $430 an ounce spurred by the dollar's earlier losses. Gold's
counter-cyclical tendencies to the dollar, coupled with its appeal as a safe haven, had made it a popular
destination for speculative funds.
But the dollar stopped losing ground during the last few days and turned higher yesterday. Consequently,
gold prices were pushed lower from the start of the session. Adding to the downward pressure was a
deterioration of "technicals," or patterns in price charts, which emitted sell signals to chart-following funds as
prices ticked lower.
With potential buyers content to sit on the sidelines as the funds jettisoned some of their previously
purchased contracts, prices swiftly dropped to their lowest close in a month, undoing all the gains made since
late December.
Leonard Kaplan, president of money-management firm Prospector Asset Management, said he expects
further bailouts today. "I wouldn't be surprised if we were pushed back below $400, especially if the stock
markets stay strong and the dollar continues gaining."
In other commodity markets:
NATURAL GAS: Nymex futures fell 8.5% following a storage report that was just slightly below the market
expectation, even as bitter cold gripped the Northeast, spurring the highest daily gas usage of all time for
some utilities. February futures fell 54.2 cents to $5.845 a million British thermal units. The Energy
Information Administration reported that inventories fell by 153 billion cubic feet. "The bulls wanted 200 billion
cubic feet and they didn't get it," said George Speicher, a gas trader at Dow Inc. in Houston.
WHEAT: Chicago Board of Trade futures fell as speculators and local floor traders sold in the absence of any
bullish news. March futures dropped nine cents to $3.8625 a bushel.

Dollar Continues Its Recovery, Aided by Good Economic Data
Currency Trading
Dollar Continues Its Recovery, Aided by Good Economic Data
By Jamie McGeever Dow Jones Newswires
389 words
16 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- The dollar's corrective recovery continued with a batch of U.S. economic reports helping to
propel the currency to new highs for the week against its major European counterparts.
After advancing only modestly following broadly supportive inflation, jobless and retail-sales data early in the
U.S. session, the dollar got its real shot in the arm at noon from the Federal Reserve Bank of Philadelphia's
business conditions index for January, which came in at 38.8, above market estimates of 30. The dollar's
added impetus even lifted it against the yen, albeit with the suspected assistance of yet more intervention
from Japan's Ministry of Finance, dealers said.
Paul Podolsky, currency strategist at FleetBoston Financial in Boston, noted that unwinding of short dollar
positions from short-term players drove the market. "It's nothing fundamental . . . because there's nothing in
the data that suggests the Fed is going to raise rates," the prerequisite for foreign investors to start investing
en masse again in U.S. assets.
Inflationary pressures appear weak, with consumer prices rising only 0.2% in December and the core figure
up only 0.1%. Both these numbers were in line with economists' forecasts although retail sales last month
rose by only 0.5% compared with calls for a jump of 0.8%. Jobless claims last week fell by 11,000, more than
the anticipated 3,000. The dollar's correction was mirrored in other asset markets, too, as bond prices
continued to rise, pushing yields on the 10-year Treasury note firmly below 4%. Gold, which forged 14-year
highs last week, slumped over $10 to below $408.70 a troy ounce, a new month low.
In late trading in New York yesterday, the euro was at $1.2566, down from $1.2654 late Wednesday, and
down at 133.75 yen from 134.35 yen. The dollar rose to 106.48 yen from 106.21 yen, and gained more
against the Swiss franc to hit 1.2456 francs, up from 1.2335 francs. The pound fell to $1.8223, bouncing back
from an intraday low of $1.8180 but still weaker than $1.8323 late Wednesday.

The announcement that November exports were the strongest in three years helped push the Dow Jones
Industrial Average up 111.19 points, or 1.07%, to 10538.37 during regular trading hours, near the 21-month

The after-hours disclosure that J.P. Morgan will acquire Bank One fueled hopes that merger activity will help
drive stock gains this year.
Intel's announcement of record quarterly revenue and doubled earnings, however, wasn't enough to impress
investors, at least initially. They sent the stock down 2.8% in after-hours trading. The Intel news also could
affect trading today.
The yield of the 10-year Treasury note fell below the psychologically important 4% mark again, as Treasury
prices, which move inversely to the yield, rose.
The drumbeat of bullish news is enticing investors back to stocks, said Todd Clark, head of listed trading at
Wells Fargo Securities, a brokerage arm of Wells Fargo in San Francisco.
"It's earnings, it's the economy, it is the continued prospect of low interest rates and the backdrop of a softer
dollar that should make our companies more competitive," Mr. Clark said.
The dollar rose against the euro following the trade news. Oil prices rose; gold fell.
The Nasdaq Composite Index advanced 0.70%, or 14.69 points, to 2111.13, while the Standard &Poor's
500-stock index rose 0.83%, or 9.30 points, to 1130.52. Both indexes finished just short of recent highs.

improved.
Outside the U.S., stocks declined in dollar terms. The Dow Jones World Stock Index, excluding U.S. stocks,
fell 0.12%, or 0.18 point, to 149.55.
In major U.S. market action:
Stocks rose. On the Big Board, where 1.48 billion shares traded, 2,228 stocks rose and 1,035 fell.
Bonds gained. The 10-year Treasury note rose 8/32, or $2.50 for each $1,000 invested, to yield 3.988%. The
30-year bond was up 21/32, yielding 4.889%.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, it traded at 106.21 yen, down from 106.24, while the euro fell
against the dollar to $1.2654 from $1.2766.

The Making of a Star --- Hotels Grade Themselves, Reducing Value of Ratings; The Plaza vs. Comfort Inn
The Making of a Star --- Hotels Grade Themselves, Reducing Value of Ratings; The Plaza vs. Comfort
Inn
By Christina Binkley
1,290 words
15 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
Copyright 2004 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
WHEN THE new Pala Casino Resort and Spa threw itself a gala last year, the invitations read, "You're invited
to the grand opening of our fabulous four-star resort." But anyone who expected the stately appeal of a place
like New York's Pierre hotel -- which is rated four-star by the rigorous Mobil Travel Guides -- might be
disappointed.
So who gave the San Diego area resort the upscale four-star rating? Hotel director Joel Moore concedes the
hotel made it up. "We feel we're a four star," says Mr. Moore, who points out the room's 27-inch TVs and
lighted make-up mirrors. "We're of that caliber."
Star ratings are increasingly important as more travelers book hotel rooms online. On Priceline and Hotwire,
customers make lodging choices based on quality ratings without even knowing the identity of the hotel. But
there's nothing close to uniformity in assigning ratings, and travel Web sites are rife with complaints from
travelers who feel they were misled about the quality of a hotel.
There are growing cries for a clampdown on the grade inflation. BiddingforTravel.com -- a site devoted to
helping travelers score deals on the Priceline discount Web site -- has an entire forum on hotel "misratings."
Brian Ek of Priceline says, "We need a more homogeneous rating system across the industry." The confusion
irks travelers and is a headache for hotels and booking agents too, he says.
There is a wide divergence in how ratings are assigned. Mobil and AAA -- the gold standards in the U.S. --
both employ professional inspectors who anonymously tour and test establishments, turning over bedcovers
and sleuthing around bathrooms of hundreds of hotels a year. By contrast, many travel Web sites, including
Hotels.com, Priceline and Hotwire, leave the ratings up to their salespeople -- and sometimes the hotels
themselves. Priceline, for instance, gets lists of a hotel's amenities (like robes, a swimming pool and room
service), then proposes a rating based on the claims.
Frequently, ratings conflict to the point of being nonsense. Consider the Mondrian hotel in Los Angeles, a hip
Ian Schrager establishment on the Sunset Strip. Expedia and Hotels.com give it the top five-star rating -- the
same rating they give the truly luxurious Peninsula hotel in Beverly Hills. The Mondrian gets four stars from
Hotwire, and three stars from Mobil Travel Guides. The American Automobile Association doesn't rate it at all.
Travelocity customers rate it two smiley faces out of five.
The Mondrian gives itself a full five stars. Lesser ratings come from agencies that just don't "get" the hotel's
edgy quotient, says one of the hotel's marketing executives. "As far as the `lifestyle' type of hotel goes, we're
a five-star."
In Europe, some countries have government-regulated ratings. (France uses zero to four stars, for instance.)
But these various European ratings tend to be based on amenities, rather than quality. So a hotel might get a
high rating for offering private bathrooms, but the rating wouldn't reflect the fact that the bath is dirty.
Moreover, European hotel ratings are sometimes tied to taxation, with lower-rated hotels taxed at lower rates.
As a result, hotels have been known to seek lower ratings than they deserve.
In the U.S., no government agency lays out common rating principles.
These days, most big hotel companies sell about 10% of their rooms online, a number that's expected to
more than double in the next few years. Popular online travel sites, including Expedia, Hotels.com and
Travelocity, use rating systems to help guide travelers' choices.
Scores of postings on travel Web sites reflect the irritation or worse that an inaccurate rating can bring. A
central California systems analyst was highly annoyed after using the Hotwire discount site to bid on a
4.5-star Los Angeles hotel for $90 in recent months, only to get the Hilton Checkers, which he found was
rated three stars by AAA. Sometimes, the results can be scary. One young Brigham Young University

--- Page 184 ---
Page 184 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
student's $85 bid on Hotwire for a four-star hotel landed her in a seedy Los Angeles establishment with a
broken door lock and a stack of porn magazines in a drawer.
Such discrepancies are common. The Willard Inter-Continental hotel gets three stars from Mobil, four stars
from Priceline, and five stars from Hotwire. Ditto for the Omni in San Francisco and the Inter-Continental in
Houston. Priceline and Hotwire both think the Venetian hotel in Las Vegas, a Venice-themed extravaganza
with oversize rooms, rates a full top-of-the-line five stars. But Mobil is less impressed, giving it four stars.
Mobil Travel Guide is widely considered toughest: Only 30 hotels have its five-star rating. Out of 35 hotels in
the U.S., only four Ritz-Carltons got a Mobil five-star award for 2004, while seven rated only three stars --
relegating the Ritz-Carltons in Laguna Niguel, Calif.; Marina Del Rey, Calif.; and Atlanta to the same level of
the average Marriott, in Mobil's eyes.
Some groups' ratings are consistently more conservative -- or more generous -- than others. Priceline, based
in Norwalk, Conn., recently compared its ratings to Hotwire's for 632 hotels, finding that Hotwire's ratings
were higher 51.3% of the time.
"We think we're consistent with all of the other sites," says Amy Bohutinsky of Hotwire, who notes that
Hotwire's sales team visits nearly 7,000 hotels and compares their impressions to Mobil, AAA and other Web
sites.
All this comes at a time when hotels are competing hotly for high ratings, particularly from established raters
like Mobil Travel Guides and AAA. When those agencies began looking for separate bathtubs and showers in
luxury hotels in recent years, for instance, a round of expensive bath renovations ensued nationwide. Many
trends, such as offering high-thread-count linens and in-room coffee makers, spread quickly once established
as standards by rating agencies.
So what can a traveler do, short of become a rating scholar? If you know the name of the hotel, Web sites like
TripAdvisor.com and HotelShark.com offer homespun reviews sent in by travelers.
Richard Brodie, a Seattle-based former Microsoft executive, asks detailed questions about the services he
requires from a hotel. "At this point I'm most interested in high-speed Internet access, and the star ratings
don't include that," Mr. Brodie says. Mobil and others do factor connectivity into their ratings -- but don't list it
separately, so you can't be guaranteed you'll get speedy connections just because a hotel is rated four-star,
for instance.
---

Hotels often receive radically different ratings from travel Web sites and
rating services. A sampling:

---
How They Rate
A look at how the various hotel ratings systems are calculated.
-- Mobil: Professional inspectors visit hotels unannounced each year,
rating them on thousands of criteria.
-- AAA: Sixty-five inspectors make announced and unannounced hotel visits.
(Uses diamonds instead of stars.)
-- Priceline: Ratings are based on lists of amenities provided by hotels,

--- Page 185 ---
Page 185 of 189 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
and from recommendations of Priceline salespeople.
-- Expedia: One staffer speaks with hotel managers and Expedia sales
staff, but doesn't visit properties.
-- Hotwire: The site's sales reps rate hotels based on their sales visits
and hotels' own descriptions.
-- Hotels.com: Sales staff "inspect" most big-city properties. Hotels
sometimes rate themselves.
-- Travelocity: Uses AAA ratings, customer reviews.

Stocks Decline As Investors Decide to Wait
Tuesday's Markets
Stocks Decline As Investors Decide to Wait
By E.S. Browning
607 words
14 January 2004
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1

AFTER BIDDING up stocks at the start of the year in expectation of strong earnings news, investors did some
selling yesterday as they wrung their hands about how good the news actually may be. And investors
endured some extra drama during the day as stocks gyrated during a speech by Federal Reserve Chairman
Alan Greenspan in Germany.
After being down almost 118 points during the Greenspan speech, the Dow Jones Industrial Average finished
with a decline of 58 points, or 0.55%, at 10427.18. That left the blue-chip average down 0.26% since the year
began.
The Nasdaq Composite Index, whose sharp recent gains had left it at a 30-month high on Monday, fell
0.73%, or 15.34 points, to 2096.44. It still is up almost 5% on the year -- after rising 50% last year.
With Intel, Yahoo and Apple Computer all scheduled to report earnings after regular trading hours today,
some investors decided to pull back to see if results meet expectations.
"People wanted to take some profits and not be as aggressive in their buying until they see what Intel and
some of the others actually show on the earnings front," said Robert Harrington, co-head of U.S. stock trading
at brokerage firm UBS.
The day's big excitement came shortly after 11 a.m. EST when Fed Chairman Greenspan began speaking at
a meeting in Germany sponsored by that nation's central bank. Stocks fell sharply after Mr. Greenspan
acknowledged to a questioner that the threat of a sharp dollar decline is "a crucial issue." But Mr. Greenspan
emphasized that he sees "little evidence of stress" in funding the U.S. trade deficit, and stocks recovered.
Investors who had hoped for a more upbeat speech were disappointed, but they seemed to conclude that
there was little grist for additional worry in the Fed chairman's talk.
The dollar fell only slightly, remaining above the record low it hit against the euro Friday. Treasury bonds, a
refuge in times of worry, rose; gold fell.
The day's earnings worries were sparked by German software giant SAP, which disappointed investors with
news that fourth-quarter sales dropped 3%. That, together with news that Oracle Chief Executive Larry
Ellison is passing the job of chairman on to a colleague, helped knock technology stocks down. Shares in
computer-chip makers, which had been up more than 10% on the year, fell nearly 3%.
Technology-consulting firm Accenture said its profit in the current quarter could rise less than analysts
forecast.
Some investors fear that tech stocks are overdue for a pullback. Traders at UBS calculated that the Nasdaq
composite typically has fallen an average of 14% from its January high to its February low since 1987, Mr.
Harrington said. That sort of thinking led some investors to book some of their tech-stock profits now.
Some short sellers, investors who bet that stocks will decline, began placing bets that tech stocks will fall,
traders said. To do that, they borrowed the stocks from brokers and sold them in hopes of buying them back
cheaper later.The Standard &Poor's 500-stock index fell 0.53%, or 6.01 points, to 1121.22, still up almost 1%
since the year began.
The 10-year Treasury note rose 18/32, or $5.625 for each $1,000 invested. The yield, which moves inversely
to price, fell to 4.017%.
The dollar weakened to 106.24 yen, down from 106.69, as the euro rose against the dollar to $1.2766 from
$1.2747.
